Machine Learning Notes Introduction to Machine Learning Types Machine Learning (ML) is primarily categorized based on the amount and type of supervision a model receives during training. We can divide these into four main categories: 1. Supervised Learning 2. Unsupervised Learning 3. Semi-supervised Learning 4. Reinforcement Learning 1. Supervised Learning In Supervised Learning, the algorithm is trained on a "labeled" dataset. This means for every input provided, the corresponding output (target) is already known. The goal is to learn a mathematical mapping between inputs and outputs so the model can predict the output for new, unseen inputs. Sub-types: ● Regression: Used when the output variable is a continuous numerical value (e.g., predicting house prices, stock prices, or salary). ● Classification: Used when the output variable is a category or label (e.g., Email: Spam or Not Spam, Disease: Yes or No). Mathematical Explanation (The Relationship): The core idea is to find a function f(x) such that y = f(x) + e, where y is the output, x is the input, and e is the error . For a simple linear case: y = (w1 * x1) + (w2 * x2) + ... + (wn * xn) + b ● y : Predicted value. ● x : Input features (e.g., IQ, CGP A). ● w : Weights (importance of each feature). ● b : Bias (intercept).

--- Page 2 ---
Example Code (Regression): Python from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression import numpy as np # Input: IQ and CGP A X = np.array([[ 100 , 8.5 ], [ 110 , 7.5 ], [ 120 , 9.0 ]]) # Output: Salary (in LPA) y = np.array([ 10 , 8 , 12 ]) model = LinearRegression() model.fit(X, y) # Learning the relationship # Predict for a new student with IQ 115 and CGP A 8.0 prediction = model.predict([[ 115 , 8.0 ]]) print(prediction) 2. Unsupervised Learning In Unsupervised Learning, the data is "unlabeled." We only have input features and no target output. The algorithm tries to find hidden patterns or structures within the data. Main Applications: ● Clustering: Grouping similar data points together (e.g., segmenting customers based on buying habits). ● Dimensionality Reduction: Reducing the number of input variables (columns) while keeping the important information. This helps in visualization and faster processing. ● Anomaly Detection: Finding "odd" data points that don't fit the pattern (e.g., credit card fraud detection). ● Association Rule Learning: Finding rules that describe your data (e.g., "People who buy diapers also tend to buy beer"). Mathematical Explanation (Clustering): A common method is K-Means Clustering, which uses the Euclidean Distance formula to determine similarity between points: Distance = SquareRoot( (x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2 )

--- Page 3 ---
The algorithm iteratively moves "centroids" to minimize the sum of squared distances within each cluster . Example Code (Clustering): Python from sklearn.cluster import KMeans import numpy as np # Unlabeled data: IQ and CGP A of 5000 students X = np.array([[ 100 , 8.5 ], [ 110 , 7.5 ], [ 80 , 6.0 ], [ 85 , 6.5 ]]) # We want to group them into 2 clusters kmeans = KMeans(n_components= 2 ) kmeans.fit(X) # Check which cluster each point belongs to print(kmeans.labels_) 3. Semi-supervised Learning This is a middle ground. It uses a small amount of labeled data combined with a large amount of unlabeled data. ● Why? Labeling data is expensive and time-consuming (requires humans), while gathering raw data is cheap. ● Example: Google Photos. It uses unsupervised learning to group similar faces together (clustering). Then, it asks you to label one face (e.g., "Dad"), and it automatically labels all other photos in that cluster as "Dad" (supervised part). 4. Reinforcement Learning (RL) Reinforcement Learning does not rely on a fixed dataset. Instead, it involves an Agent interacting with an Environment. Core Components: 1. Agent: The learner (the algorithm). 2. Environment: The world the agent lives in (e.g., a Chess board or a road for self-driving cars).

--- Page 4 ---
3. Action: What the agent does. 4. Reward/Punishment: Feedback from the environment. ○ Good action -> Positive reward (+10 points). ○ Bad action -> Punishment (-10 points). 5. Policy: The strategy the agent uses to decide the next action based on the current state. The Process: The agent performs an action, gets a reward, and updates its "policy" to maximize total rewards over time. This is how machines learn to play complex games like Go or drive autonomous vehicles. Summary Table for Quick Reference: Type Data Type Goal Examples Supervised Labeled Predict target House price, Spam detection Unsupervised Unlabele d Find patterns Customer segments, PCA Semi-supervise d Mix Label efficiently Photo tagging, Speech analysis Reinforcement Interactiv e Maximize reward Robotics, Gaming (AlphaGo)

--- Page 5 ---
Batch Machine Learning vs. Online Machine Learning Machine Learning models can be categorized based on how they learn and are deployed in a "Production" environment. Production refers to the environment (server) where the code runs for actual customers, as opposed to a "Development" environment (the developer's local machine). 1. Batch Learning (Offline Learning) Batch Learning is the traditional way of training models. In this approach, the system is incapable of learning incrementally; it must be trained using all available data at once. How it Works: 1. Full Data Training: You take your entire dataset and use it to train the model. 2. Offline Training: Because training on a massive dataset is computationally expensive and time-consuming, it is typically done offline on powerful machines (Development Environment). 3. Deployment: Once the model is trained and tested, it is deployed to the server (Production). 4. Static Execution: The deployed model does not "learn" from the new live data it sees. It simply applies what it learned during the initial training phase. The Maintenance Cycle: To keep the model up-to-date with new trends (e.g., new movies on Netflix or new types of spam emails), the model must be retrained: ● Gather new data and merge it with old data. ● Retrain the model from scratch on the whole combined dataset. ● Stop the old system and replace it with the new version. This cycle can happen daily , weekly , or monthly . Mathematical Representation: In Batch Learning, the objective is to minimize the total cost function over the entire dataset $D$ of size $N$:

--- Page 6 ---
Cost = (1 / N) * Sum from i=1 to N of [ Loss(f(x_i, weights), y_i) ] Where: ● f(x_i, weights) is the model's prediction for input x_i . ● y_i is the actual target value. ● The weights are updated only after processing the entire batch (or large chunks of it). 2. Limitations of Batch Learning While simple to implement, Batch Learning has several drawbacks: A. Large Data Issues If the dataset grows extremely large (e.g., Terrabytes of data), it may exceed the hardware capacity (RAM/CPU) of a single machine. Training on the entire "chunk" becomes impossible or extremely slow . B. Hardware & Connectivity Constraints Some systems (like sensors in remote areas, software on satellites, or apps on phones in areas with no internet) cannot easily send data back to a central server for retraining and then download a new model. They need a system that can learn on the device itself. C. Lack of Real-time Adaptability Batch systems suffer from "Concept Drift." ● Example: A sudden news event (like a major government policy change) might change user interests instantly . ● A Batch model trained yesterday won't know about today's trend until it is retrained tomorrow . By then, the trend might already be over. 3. Comparison Summary Feature Batch Learning Online Learning

--- Page 7 ---
Learning Style All data at once (No incremental learning) One data point or "mini-batch" at a time Environment Offline (Development) Online (Live/Production) Resource Usage High (needs to process all data) Low (only processes new data) Adaptability Slow (requires full retraining) Fast (adapts to new patterns immediately) Example Concept Code: Simulating Batch Retraining In this Python example, notice how we have to combine old and new data to "update" the model's knowledge in a batch setup. Python import numpy as np from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression # Initial Dataset (Older trends) X_old = np.array([[ 1 ], [ 2 ], [ 3 ]]) y_old = np.array([ 10 , 20 , 30 ]) # Step 1: Initial Training model = LinearRegression() model.fit(X_old, y_old) print( "Initial Prediction for 4:" , model.predict([[ 4 ]])) # --- Time Passes (New data arrives) --- X_new = np.array([[ 4 ], [ 5 ]]) y_new = np.array([ 45 , 55 ]) # Notice the trend shifted slightly higher # Step 2: Batch Retraining # We cannot just "fit" the new data; we must combine it X_combined = np.vstack((X_old, X_new))

--- Page 8 ---
y_combined = np.concatenate((y_old, y_new)) # Retrain from scratch model.fit(X_combined, y_combined) print( "Updated Prediction for 4 after batch retraining:" , model.predict([[ 4 ]])) Note: The next topic in the series covers Online Learning in detail, explaining how to overcome these batch limitations. Reference Video: Types of Machine Learning - Batch vs Online Online Machine Learning (Incremental Learning) Online Machine Learning is a technique where a model is trained incrementally by feeding it data instances sequentially , either individually or in small groups called mini-batches. This is the opposite of Batch Learning, where the model is trained offline on a full dataset and then remains static in production. 1. How Online Learning Works Unlike Batch Learning, where the model is "frozen" after deployment, an Online Learning model continues to learn while it is live on the server . ● Sequential Feeding: Data arrives in a stream. The system learns from new data on the fly. ● Dynamic Update: As soon as new data points arrive, the model updates its internal parameters (weights and biases). ● Disposal of Data: Once the model has learned from the latest data, it can usually be discarded (unless you need it for re-evaluations), saving massive amounts of storage. The Workflow: 1. Initial Training: Start with a small representative dataset. 2. Deployment: Deploy the model to the production server .

--- Page 9 ---
3. Real-time Prediction & Learning: The model predicts for a user AND simultaneously uses the feedback/new data to update itself. 2. When to Use Online Learning 1. Concept Drift: When the nature of the data changes rapidly over time. ○ Example: Stock market prices or Twitter/X trends where what was relevant 1 hour ago is obsolete now. 2. Large Datasets (Out-of-Core Learning): When your dataset is too large to fit into a computer's main memory (RAM). ○ You can load small chunks (mini-batches), train the model, discard them, and load the next chunk. 3. Cost & Efficiency: It is often cheaper to update a model with 10 new data points than to retrain a massive model from scratch with 10 million + 10 points. 3. Mathematical Explanation: Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) Most online learning algorithms rely on Stochastic Gradient Descent. Unlike standard Gradient Descent which calculates the gradient using the entire dataset, SGD calculates it for one instance at a time. The Weight Update Rule: For each new data point $(x, y)$: 1. Calculate prediction: y_pred = f(x) 2. Calculate Loss: L = (y_pred - y)^2 (for regression) 3. Update Weight: W_new = W_old - (Learning_Rate * Gradient_of_Loss) Learning Rate ($\eta$): * High Learning Rate: The model adapts very quickly to new data but might "forget" old patterns just as fast (catastrophic forgetting). ● Low Learning Rate: The model is more stable but learns slowly and might lag behind new trends. 4. Implementation Example (Python)

--- Page 10 ---
We use SGDRegressor from Scikit-Learn, which provides a partial_fit method. This method allows the model to learn incrementally without forgetting what it learned previously . Python import numpy as np from sklearn.linear_model import SGDRegressor # Initialize the model model = SGDRegressor() # 1. First batch of data X1 = np.array([[ 1.2 , 0.5 ], [ 2.1 , 0.8 ]]) y1 = np.array([ 5.0 , 7.2 ]) # Use partial_fit instead of fit model.partial_fit(X1, y1) print( "Prediction after Batch 1:" , model.predict([[ 1.5 , 0.6 ]])) # 2. Second batch of data arrives later (Online) X2 = np.array([[ 1.8 , 0.9 ]]) y2 = np.array([ 6.8 ]) # Model updates without retraining on X1/y1 model.partial_fit(X2, y2) print( "Prediction after Batch 2 (updated):" , model.predict([[ 1.5 , 0.6 ]]))

--- Page 11 ---
5. Challenges & Risks ● Bad Data Sensitivity: If "garbage" or malicious data is fed into an online system, the model will learn from it immediately and start behaving poorly (e.g., a chatbot learning offensive language). ● Monitoring: You must have robust monitoring to detect if the model's performance starts dropping suddenly due to bad incoming data. ● Complexity: Managing a system that trains and predicts simultaneously on a server is technically more difficult than a static batch system. Comparison: Batch vs. Online Learning Feature Batch (Offline) Learning Online (Incremental) Learning Data Processing Processes all data at once. Processes data in small chunks or one-by-one. Retraining Must retrain from scratch for new data. Updates parameters on the go. System Speed Fast at prediction, slow at update. Fast at both prediction and update. Storage Needs to store all historical data. Can discard data after learning. Complexity Low. High (requires real-time monitoring). Reference Video: Online Machine Learning - CampusX

--- Page 12 ---
Instance-Based vs. Model-Based Learning Machine Learning models are categorized by how they "learn" or generalize from training data to make predictions on new, unseen data. This distinction is crucial for understanding how different algorithms handle data and storage. 1. Instance-Based Learning (Lazy Learning) Instance-Based Learning is often called "Lazy Learning" because the system does not try to build a mathematical model during the training phase. Instead, it simply "memorizes" the training instances. How it works: ● Memorization: The model stores the entire training dataset. ● Similarity Matching: When a new data point (query point) arrives, the algorithm looks at the most similar stored instances (neighbors) to make a prediction. ● Late Computation: No real work is done until a prediction is requested. Example: K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN) Imagine we want to predict if a student will be placed based on their IQ and CGP A. 1. We plot all historical students on a graph. 2. A new student comes in. 3. We find the 3 closest students (neighbors) on the graph. 4. If 2 out of 3 were placed, we predict "Yes" for the new student. Mathematical Concept (Euclidean Distance): To find the "nearest" neighbors, we calculate the distance between the new point $(x, y)$ and a training point $(x_i, y_i)$: Distance = SquareRoot( (x - x_i)^2 + (y - y_i)^2 ) 2. Model-Based Learning (Eager Learning) Model-Based Learning attempts to find a generalized "rule" or a mathematical function that describes the relationship between the input and the output.

--- Page 13 ---
How it works: ● Generalization: During training, the algorithm analyzes the data to find patterns and builds a mathematical model (e.g., a line or a curve). ● Parameter Estimation: It learns specific "parameters" (like weights and biases) that define this model. ● Discarding Data: Once the model is built, the training data can often be discarded because the mathematical formula alone is enough for predictions. Example: Linear Regression / Logistic Regression Instead of looking at neighbors, the model learns a Decision Boundary (a line). 1. The algorithm finds a line equation: Placement = (w1 * IQ) + (w2 * CGP A) + b . 2. For any new student, it simply plugs their IQ and CGP A into this formula. 3. If the result is above a certain threshold, it predicts "Placed." Key Differences Summary Feature Instance-Based Learning Model-Based Learning Learning Style Memorization (Lazy). Generalization (Eager). Prediction Basis Similarity to specific examples. A learned mathematical rule/formula. Storage Requirement High (must store all training data). Low (only needs to store the model parameters).

--- Page 14 ---
Computational Cost High during prediction (calculating distances). High during training, but very fast prediction. Examples K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN), SVM (with kernels). Linear Regression, Neural Networks, Decision Trees. Implementation Concept (Python) Instance-Based (KNN) Python from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier # Data is stored, not modeled mathematically during 'fit' knn = KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors= 3 ) knn.fit(X_train, y_train) # Prediction requires looking through all X_train points prediction = knn.predict(X_new) Model-Based (Logistic Regression) Python from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression # Finds the best 'weights' and 'bias' during 'fit' lr = LogisticRegression()

--- Page 15 ---
lr.fit(X_train, y_train) # Once fit is done, X_train is no longer needed # Prediction is just a quick math calculation: W*X + b prediction = lr.predict(X_new) Why it matters? ● Instance-based is useful when data is constantly changing and you want to avoid expensive retraining. ● Model-based is preferred for production systems where prediction speed and storage efficiency are critical. Reference Video: Instance-Based Vs Model-Based Learning - CampusX Challenges and Problems in Machine Learning Machine Learning (ML) is not just about writing code; it involves overcoming significant hurdles related to data, algorithms, and real-world deployment. Below are the 10 major challenges you will face as an ML practitioner . 1. Data Collection & Gathering ● The Issue: While beginners often get ready-made datasets (CSV/Excel) from sites like Kaggle, real-world data is rarely "ready ." ● Challenge: You often have to build your own data pipelines through Web Scraping (extracting data from websites) or using APIs (Application Programming Interfaces). ● Impact: Without high-quality data, the most advanced algorithm is useless.

--- Page 16 ---
2. Insufficient Data ● The Concept: Small datasets often lead to poor models. There is a famous concept called the "Unreasonable Effectiveness of Data." ● Mathematical Insight: If Algorithm A is sophisticated but has 1,000 data points, and Algorithm B is simple but has 1,000,000 points, Algorithm B will almost always win. ● Challenge: Deep Learning models, in particular , require massive amounts of data to perform well. 3. Non-representative Training Data ● Sampling Noise: When your training data is too small, it might contain random patterns that don't exist in the real world. ● Sampling Bias: When your data collection method consistently excludes or over-represents certain groups. ○ Example: Surveying only people in India to predict who will win the Cricket World Cup. This data is biased toward India and doesn't represent the global perspective. 4. Poor Quality Data ● The "Garbage In, Garbage Out" (GIGO) Principle: If your input data is noisy , has errors, or contains missing values, your output predictions will be equally bad. ● Reality: Data Scientists spend 60% to 80% of their time cleaning and preprocessing data (handling outliers, missing values, and formatting). 5. Irrelevant Features ● Feature Selection: Not all data columns (features) are useful for prediction. ○ Example: Using a person's "User ID" to predict their likelihood of having a heart attack is useless. ● Feature Engineering: Combining existing features to create more meaningful ones (e.g., combining Height and Weight into a single feature called BMI). 6. Overfitting

--- Page 17 ---
● Definition: When the model learns the "noise" in the training data rather than the actual pattern. It performs perfectly on training data but fails on new, unseen data. ● Visualization: Think of a model that "memorizes" every answer in a textbook but doesn't understand the concepts, failing the actual exam. ● Mathematical Sign: High training accuracy but low test accuracy . 7. Underfitting ● Definition: The opposite of overfitting. The model is too simple to learn the underlying structure of the data. ● Visualization: Trying to fit a straight line through data that clearly follows a complex curve. ● Mathematical Sign: Low accuracy on both training and test data. 8. Software Integration ● The Gap: A model working in a Python Jupyter Notebook is not a "product." ● Challenge: Integrating the model into various platforms (Android, iOS, Web, Windows). ● Example: Making a machine learning model run efficiently on a washing machine or a car's embedded system is extremely difficult due to hardware limitations. 9. Offline Learning & Deployment ● Concept Drift: The real world changes, but a deployed "Batch/Offline" model stays static. ● Challenge: Monitoring a model in production to see if it is still accurate and knowing exactly when to pull it back and retrain it with new data. 10. Cost Involved (Hidden Costs) ● Storage & Computation: Training large models on the cloud (AWS/GCP/Azure) can be incredibly expensive. ● Deployment Cost: Every time a user makes a prediction, it uses server resources that cost money . ● The Paper: "The Cost of AI" highlights how hidden costs often make ML projects non-viable for many businesses.

--- Page 18 ---
Technical Concept: Feature Engineering Example Creating a single meaningful feature from two raw ones to simplify the model's task: Python import pandas as pd # Sample raw data data = { 'Weight_kg' : [ 70 , 85 , 95 , 60 ], 'Height_m' : [ 1.7 , 1.8 , 1.9 , 1.6 ]} df = pd.DataFrame(data) # Feature Engineering: Creating BMI (Body Mass Index) # Mathematical Formula: BMI = Weight / (Height^2) df[ 'BMI' ] = df[ 'Weight_kg' ] / (df[ 'Height_m' ] ** 2 ) # Now, we can drop the original 'Height' and 'Weight' columns # because 'BMI' is a more relevant feature for fitness prediction. df_reduced = df[[ 'BMI' ]] print(df_reduced) Career Tip: MLOps Because these challenges are so common, a new field called MLOps (Machine Learning Operations) has emerged. It focuses on the end-to-end lifecycle of ML: from data gathering to deployment, monitoring, and cost management. Reference Video: Challenges in Machine Learning - CampusX

--- Page 19 ---
In-Depth Guide to Tensors in Machine Learning Tensors are the fundamental building blocks of almost all modern Machine Learning and Deep Learning frameworks (like TensorFlow and PyTorch). Understanding them is essential for handling data effectively . 1. What is a Tensor? At its simplest, a Tensor is a container for numbers. It is a mathematical structure used to store data in multiple dimensions. In Computer Science terms, a Tensor is synonymous with an N-dimensional array . ● 1D Tensor = Vector ● 2D Tensor = Matrix ● 3D+ Tensor = Higher-order Tensors Why Tensors? Machine learning libraries represent all data—text, images, sound, or video—as tensors to perform mathematical operations (like matrix multiplication) efficiently at scale. 2. Types of Tensors by Dimension (Rank) The "Rank" of a tensor is simply the number of dimensions it has. 0D Tensor (Scalar) A single number . It has no axes and is called a scalar . ● Example: 5 , 42.7 ● Rank: 0 1D Tensor (Vector) An array of numbers. It has exactly one axis. ● Example: [1, 2, 3, 4] ● Rank: 1

--- Page 20 ---
● Important Distinction: A 1D tensor is a vector . If it has 5 elements, it is called a "5-dimensional vector ," but it is still a 1D Tensor . 2D Tensor (Matrix) An array of vectors. It has two axes (Rows and Columns). ● Example: A table or spreadsheet. ● Rank: 2 3D Tensor An array of matrices. Think of it as a "cube" of numbers. ● Example: Time-series data (Samples, Timesteps, Features). ● Rank: 3 3. Mathematical Attributes of Tensors To define a tensor completely , we need three pieces of information: 1. Rank (Number of Axes): How many dimensions it has. 2. Shape: A tuple of integers that tells you how many elements are along each axis. ○ A matrix with 3 rows and 2 columns has a shape of (3, 2) . 3. Data Type (dtype): The type of data contained (e.g., float32 , int64 , uint8 ). Tensor Size Calculation: The total number of elements in a tensor is the product of its shape. Total Elements = Size_axis_1 * Size_axis_2 * ... * Size_axis_n 4. Practical Examples in Data Science A. Tabular Data (2D Tensors) Most common machine learning data. ● Shape: (Samples, Features) ● Example: 10,000 students with 3 features (IQ, CGP A, Attendance). ● Tensor: A matrix of shape (10000, 3) .

--- Page 21 ---
B. Natural Language Processing (3D Tensors) Text data processed in sequences. ● Shape: (Samples, Sequence_Length, Embedding_Dimension) ● Example: 100 sentences, each 20 words long, where each word is represented by a 50-sized vector . ● Tensor Shape: (100, 20, 50) . C. Image Data (4D Tensors) A collection of color images. ● Shape: (Batch_Size, Height, Width, Channels) ● Channels: 3 for RGB (Red, Green, Blue), 1 for Grayscale. ● Example: 128 images of 256x256 pixels in color . ● Tensor Shape: (128, 256, 256, 3) . D. Video Data (5D Tensors) A sequence of images (frames) over time. ● Shape: (Samples, Frames, Height, Width, Channels) ● Example: 10 videos, each 60 seconds long at 30 frames per second, sized 480x720. 5. Coding Concept: Creating Tensors with NumPy Since Tensors are essentially N-dimensional arrays, we use the numpy library to represent them in Python. Python import numpy as np # 1. Scalar (0D Tensor) scalar = np.array( 7 ) print( f"Rank: {scalar .ndim} , Shape: {scalar .shape} " ) # Rank: 0, Shape: ()

--- Page 22 ---
# 2. Vector (1D Tensor) vector = np.array([ 1 , 2 , 3 ]) print( f"Rank: {vector .ndim} , Shape: {vector .shape} " ) # Rank: 1, Shape: (3,) # 3. Matrix (2D Tensor) matrix = np.array([[ 1 , 2 ], [ 3 , 4 ], [ 5 , 6 ]]) print( f"Rank: {matrix.ndim} , Shape: {matrix.shape} " ) # Rank: 2, Shape: (3, 2) # 4. 3D Tensor (A collection of matrices) tensor_3d = np.array([[[ 1 , 2 ], [ 3 , 4 ]], [[ 5 , 6 ], [ 7 , 8 ]]]) print( f"Rank: {tensor_3d.ndim} , Shape: {tensor_3d.shape} " ) # Rank: 3, Shape: (2, 2, 2) Summary Cheat Sheet Data Type Tensor Rank Common Shape Single Value 0 () List of Features 1 (Features,)

--- Page 23 ---
Table of Data 2 (Samples, Features) Sequences/T ime Series 3 (Samples, Timesteps, Features) Color Images 4 (Samples, Height, Width, Channels) Video Data 5 (Samples, Frames, Height, Width, Channels) Reference Video: What are Tensors - CampusX Working with CSV Files in Machine Learning The most common data format in machine learning and data science is CSV (Comma Separated Values). This guide covers how to efficiently read and manipulate these files using the Python pandas library . 1. Importing Data To work with CSV files, we primarily use the pd.read_csv() function. From Local Machine: Python import pandas as pd df = pd.read_csv( 'train.csv' )

--- Page 24 ---
From a URL: You can directly pass a link to a CSV file. If the file is on a platform like GitHub, you might need the requests library to fetch it properly if a direct link doesn't work. Python import requests from io import StringIO url = "https://raw .githubusercontent.com/user/repo/main/data.csv" headers = { "User-Agent" : "Mozilla/5.0" } req = requests.get(url, headers=headers) data = StringIO(req.text) df = pd.read_csv(data) 2. Handling Different Delimiters (TSV Files) Some files use tabs ( \t ) instead of commas. These are called TSV (Tab Separated Values). Python # 'sep' parameter handles different separators df = pd.read_csv( 'movie_titles.tsv' , sep= '\t' ) 3. Dealing with Headers and Names

--- Page 25 ---
If your file doesn't have column names, or you want to rename them during import: ● names : Pass a list of column names. ● header : Specify which row to use as the header . Python # Assigning custom names to a file without a header row df = pd.read_csv( 'data.csv' , names=[ 'id' , 'name' , 'year' , 'rating' ]) # If the first row is actual data but treated as header , use header=None df = pd.read_csv( 'data.csv' , header= None ) 4. Selecting Specific Columns To save memory , you can import only the columns you need. Python # 'usecols' accepts a list of column names df = pd.read_csv( 'train.csv' , usecols=[ 'gender' , 'age' , 'salary' ]) 5. Skipping Rows and Limiting Data Useful for very large files or files with introductory text. ● skiprows : Skips specific rows. ● nrows : Only reads a specific number of rows. Python # Skip first row and read next 100 rows df = pd.read_csv( 'large_data.csv' , skiprows=[ 0 ], nrows= 100 )

--- Page 26 ---
6. Handling Character Encoding If you get a UnicodeDecodeError , it's because the file isn't in the default utf-8 format. Python # Common for European or older files df = pd.read_csv( 'spam.csv' , encoding= 'latin-1' ) 7. Parsing Dates By default, dates are read as strings (objects). To use date-specific functions, you must parse them. Python # 'parse_dates' converts columns to datetime objects df = pd.read_csv( 'ipl_matches.csv' , parse_dates=[ 'date' ]) 8. Data Transformation during Loading (Converters) You can apply functions to specific columns while the data is being loaded. Python def rename_team (name): if name == "Royal Challengers Bangalore" : return "RCB" return name

--- Page 27 ---
# 'converters' applies functions column-wise df = pd.read_csv( 'ipl.csv' , converters={ 'team1' : rename_team}) 9. Handling Missing Values (na_values) Sometimes "null" values are represented by symbols like - or ?? . You can tell pandas to treat these as NaN (Not a Number). Python df = pd.read_csv( 'data.csv' , na_values=[ '-' , '??' , 'None' ]) 10. Memory Optimization: Chunking When a dataset is too large to fit in RAM (e.g., 10GB file on 8GB RAM), use chunksize . This returns an iterable object. Python # Read file in chunks of 5000 rows chunks = pd.read_csv( 'huge_file.csv' , chunksize= 5000 ) for chunk in chunks: # Process each 5000-row chunk individually print(chunk.shape) Summary Table of Parameters Parameter Purpose

--- Page 28 ---
sep The delimiter (comma, tab, semicolon). index_col Set a specific column as the index. dtype Force specific data types for columns. skiprows Skip specific rows at the beginning. nrows Limit the number of rows to read. encoding Fix encoding issues (e.g., 'latin-1'). parse_dates Convert strings to datetime objects. converters Apply functions during loading. na_values Identify custom missing value markers. chunksize Load data in small batches. Reference Video: Working with CSV files - Day 15

Working with JSON and SQL Data in Machine Learning As you progress in machine learning, you'll encounter data beyond simple CSV files. JSON (JavaScript Object Notation) is a universal format used heavily in web APIs, while SQL (Structured Query Language) is the standard for extracting data directly from databases. 1. Working with JSON Files JSON is popular because it's human-readable and lightweight. It's the "universal language" for data exchange between different programming languages. Reading Local JSON Files: Pandas provides a straightforward function pd.read_json() . Python import pandas as pd # Load a JSON file into a DataFrame df = pd.read_json( 'recipes.json' ) df.head() Reading JSON from a URL: Many web APIs return data in JSON format. Always close connections in production! conn.close() Reference Video: Working with JSON/SQL - Day 16 Fetching Data from an API to Pandas DataFrame An API (Application Programming Interface) acts as a messenger between two software applications, allowing them to talk to each other . In Machine Learning, APIs are frequently used to pull live data from web servers directly into our analysis environment. 1. What is an API? Think of an API as a data pipeline. ● Analogy: When you book a train ticket on MakeMyT rip, the app doesn't have its own database of every train seat. Instead, it uses an API to ask the IARCTC server: "Is seat 15 in Coach B2 available for April 15th?" The IRCTC server sends back a data response (usually in JSON format). Why use APIs for Machine Learning? ● Live Data: Get real-time information (e.g., weather , stock prices). ● Massive Scale: Pull thousands of records automatically instead of manual entry .

--- Page 34 ---
● Universal Format: Most APIs return data in JSON, which is easily converted into a Pandas DataFrame. 2. Practical Project: The Movie Database (TMDB) In this session, we fetch "Top Rated Movies" from TMDB and create a complete dataset of ~8,500 movies. Prerequisites: 1. API Key: Register on TMDB and generate your own API key under Settings > API. 2. Libraries: You need pandas and requests . 3. Mathematical & Logical Workflow The goal is to turn multiple pages of JSON results into one large table (DataFrame). The Logic: 1. Request: Send an HTTP GET request to the API URL. 2. Parse: Convert the raw response into a JSON/Python dictionary . 3. Loop: Since results are paginated (e.g., 20 movies per page), we loop from Page 1 to Page 428. 4. Append: For every page, create a temporary DataFrame and "stack" it onto the main DataFrame. Expected Data Structure: ID Title Release Date Overview Popularity Vote Average Vote Count ... ... ... ... ... ... ... 4. Explanation Code Block (The Loop)

--- Page 35 ---
This code demonstrates how to handle pagination and build the dataset. Python import pandas as pd import requests # 1. Initialize an empty DataFrame to store all results main_df = pd.DataFrame() api_key = "YOUR_TMDB_API_KEY" # 2. Loop through pages (e.g., first 10 pages for demonstration) for i in range ( 1 , 11 ): url = f'https://api.themoviedb.org/3/movie/top_rated?api_key= {api_key} &language=en-US&page= {i} ' # Fetch data response = requests.get(url) data = response.json() # 'results' contains the list of movies on that page temp_df = pd.DataFrame(data[ 'results' ])[[ 'id' , 'title' , 'release_date' , 'overview' , 'popularity' , 'vote_average' , 'vote_count' ]] # Append temporary data to the main dataframe main_df = pd.concat([main_df, temp_df], ignore_index= True )

--- Page 36 ---
# 3. Final Result print( f"Total Movies Fetched: {main_df.shape[ 0 ]} " ) main_df.to_csv( 'top_rated_movies.csv' , index= False ) 5. Key Parameters Explained ● requests.get(url) : Hits the server and waits for a response. ● response.json() : A built-in method to parse JSON content directly into a Python dictionary . ● pd.concat() : Used to combine two DataFrames. Setting ignore_index=T rue ensures the row numbers (0, 1, 2...) are continuous and don't reset every 20 rows. ● Pagination ( &page={i} ): APIs often limit how much data they send at once. We use f-strings to dynamically change the page number in each loop iteration. 6. Where to Find More APIs? If you want to build your own unique datasets to upload to Kaggle or use in projects, explore: ● RapidAPI: A huge marketplace for thousands of free and paid APIs (Finance, Sports, Weather , etc.). ● Public APIs (GitHub List): Search for "public apis github" to find a curated list of free endpoints. Reference Video: Fetching Data From an API - Day 17 Fetching Data using Web Scraping

--- Page 37 ---
Web Scraping is the process of extracting data directly from a website's HTML code. This is a crucial skill for machine learning practitioners when a website does not provide an API or a downloadable dataset. 1. When to Use Web Scraping? ● No API Available: Many websites (like AmbitionBox or e-commerce sites) have valuable data but no public API. ● Restricted Access: Sometimes websites try to block scrapers. We can bypass basic blocks by mimicking a real web browser (using "Headers"). ● Custom Needs: When you need a specific piece of information that only exists on a webpage. 2. Mathematical Logic & Workflow Web scraping follows a sequential "Fetch-Parse-Extract" logic: 1. Request: Send an HTTP request to the URL. 2. Status Check: Ensure the request was successful (Status Code 200). If it's 403 (Forbidden), use "Headers" to act as a browser . 3. Parsing: Convert the messy HTML string into a structured tree (DOM) that Python can navigate. 4. Navigation: Find specific HTML "Tags" (like div , h2 , p ) and "Classes" that contain the data you want. 5. Iteration: Loop through multiple pages (Pagination) to collect a large dataset. 3. The "Browser Mimicking" Concept Websites often check the "User-Agent" of an incoming request. If it says "Python-requests," they might block it. By sending a custom header , we tell the website: "I am a standard Chrome browser on Windows." Code Explanation (Headers): Python headers = { 'User-Agent' : 'Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 6.3; Win64; x64) AppleW ebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/80.0.3987.162 Safari/537.36' }

--- Page 38 ---
# This header is passed into the requests.get() function 4. Key Libraries ● Pandas: To store and export the final data into a CSV. ● Requests: To fetch the webpage's HTML content. ● BeautifulSoup (bs4): The primary tool for "digging" into the HTML and finding data points. 5. Explanation Code Block: Scraping Multiple Pages In this example, we scrape data from AmbitionBox about companies (Name, Rating, Reviews, etc.) and loop through multiple pages. Python import pandas as pd import requests from bs4 import BeautifulSoup # Initialize the main dataframe final = pd.DataFrame() # Loop through 10 pages for j in range ( 1 , 11 ): url = f'https://www .ambitionbox.com/list-of-companies?page= {j} ' headers = { 'User-Agent' : 'Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 6.3; Win64; x64) AppleW ebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/80.0.3987.162 Safari/537.36' } webpage = requests.get(url, headers=headers).text

--- Page 39 ---
soup = BeautifulSoup(webpage, 'lxml' ) # Identify the container for each company company = soup.find_all( 'div' , class_= 'company-content-wrapper' ) name = [] rating = [] reviews = [] ctype = [] hq = [] how_old = [] no_of_employee = [] for i in company: # Extracting specific data points name.append(i.find( 'h2' ).text.strip()) rating.append(i.find( 'p' , class_= 'rating' ).text.strip()) reviews.append(i.find( 'a' , class_= 'review-count' ).text.strip()) # AmbitionBox often groups info like 'Public', 'Mumbai', '50+ years old' in the same class info = i.find_all( 'p' , class_= 'infoEntity' ) try : ctype.append(info[ 0 ].text.strip()) hq.append(info[ 1 ].text.strip()) how_old.append(info[ 2 ].text.strip())

--- Page 40 ---
no_of_employee.append(info[ 3 ].text.strip()) except IndexError: # Handle cases where some info might be missing pass # Create a temporary dataframe for the current page df = pd.DataFrame({ 'name' : name, 'rating' : rating, 'reviews' : reviews, 'type' : ctype, 'hq' : hq, 'old' : how_old, 'employees' : no_of_employee }) # Append to the final dataframe final = pd.concat([final, df], ignore_index= True ) # Export to CSV final.to_csv( 'companies.csv' ) 6. Important Notes for First-Time Readers ● .strip() : HTML text often comes with extra spaces or "newline" characters ( \n ). This method removes them.

--- Page 41 ---
● find() vs find_all() : Use find() for a single item (like a company name) and find_all() when you expect a list of items (like all the companies on a page). ● HTML Structure: Every website is different. You must right-click on the webpage and select "Inspect" to find the exact class names for the data you need. Summary Checklist 1. Identify the URL and how it changes with page numbers. 2. Set the Headers to avoid being blocked. 3. Inspect the Page to find the "Container" (usually a div ) for each row of data. 4. Find the unique Classes for each feature (Name, Price, Rating). 5. Write the Loop to traverse through all pages and save the results. Reference Video: Fetching data using Web Scraping - Day 18 Understanding Your Data (Exploratory Data Analysis - Day 19) When you first receive a dataset, the very first step is to "understand" it before applying any machine learning algorithms. This process involves asking a series of basic questions to get a clear picture of the data's structure, quality , and patterns. 1. How Big is the Data? Knowing the size of your dataset helps you decide whether you can process it in memory or need distributed computing. ● Function: df.shape ● Output: A tuple representing (number of rows, number of columns) . 2. How Does the Data Look?

--- Page 42 ---
It's important to visually inspect a few rows. Instead of just looking at the top rows, looking at random samples helps avoid bias if the data is sorted in a specific way. ● Head: df.head() (Shows first 5 rows). ● Sample: df.sample(5) (Shows 5 random rows). Recommended to avoid seeing only one "type" of data if the set is ordered by time or category . 3. What are the Data Types of Columns? Understanding if a column is numerical, categorical, or an object (string) is crucial for preprocessing. ● Function: df.info() ● Insight: This also shows how much memory the dataset is consuming. If memory is tight, you can convert float64 to float32 or int64 to int32 to save space. 4. Are There Any Missing Values? Missing data is a major hurdle in ML. You need to know which columns are incomplete. ● Function: df.isnull().sum() ● Logic: It counts the number of NaN (Not a Number) entries in each column. ● Decision: If a column has >70% missing values, you might drop it. Otherwise, you fill it (Imputation). 5. How Does the Data Look Mathematically? This gives a high-level summary of the distribution of numerical features. ● Function: df.describe() ● Mathematical Metrics Provided: ○ Count: Number of non-null values. ○ Mean: Average value. ○ Standard Deviation (Std): How spread out the data is. ○ Min/Max: Smallest and largest values. ○ 25%, 50%, 75% (Percentiles): ■ 50% is the Median. ■ If the Mean is much higher than the Median, the data is Right Skewed (outliers on the high end).

--- Page 43 ---
6. Are There Duplicate Values? Duplicate rows can mislead your model by giving too much importance to a single instance. ● Function: df.duplicated().sum() ● Action: Usually , duplicates are removed using df.drop_duplicates() . 7. How are the Features Correlated? Correlation tells you how two variables move together . ● Function: df.corr() ● Mathematical Derivation (Pearson Correlation Coefficient): The value ranges from -1 to +1. ○ +1: Perfect positive correlation (if X increases, Y increases). ○ -1: Perfect negative correlation (if X increases, Y decreases). ○ 0: No relationship. ● Use Case: If an input feature has a near-zero correlation with the target output, it might be an irrelevant feature that can be removed. Summary Code Block Here is the "Checklist" code you should run on every new dataset: Python import pandas as pd # Load dataset (Example: Titanic) df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' ) # 1. Size print( "Shape:" , df.shape) # 2. Preview random sample

--- Page 44 ---
print(df.sample( 5 )) # 3. Data types and memory df.info() # 4. Check for missing values print( "Missing Values:\n" , df.isnull(). sum ()) # 5. Mathematical Summary print(df.describe()) # 6. Check for duplicates print( "Duplicate count:" , df.duplicated(). sum ()) # 7. Correlation (Target: 'Survived') print( "Correlation with Survival:\n" , df.corr()[ 'Survived' ]) Key Insight: The Titanic Example ● Correlation: The video shows that Fare has a positive correlation with survival (richer people survived more), while Pclass has a negative correlation (as class number increases from 1 to 3, survival probability drops). ● Irrelevant Data: PassengerId has nearly 0 correlation with survival, meaning it is mathematically useless for prediction. Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA): Univariate Analysis Univariate Analysis is the simplest form of data analysis where you examine one variable (column) at a time. The goal is to describe the data and find patterns within that specific variable without looking at its relationship with other variables. 1. Types of Data Before starting, you must identify the type of data in your column: 1. Categorical Data: Qualitative data grouped into labels or categories (e.g., Gender , Survived [0 or 1], Pclass [1, 2, or 3]). 2. Numerical Data: Quantitative data consisting of numbers (e.g., Age, Fare, Height). 2. Analyzing Categorical Data For categorical data, we want to know the "Frequency" or "Distribution" of each category . A. Countplot (Frequency) A bar chart that shows the count of observations in each category . Python import seaborn as sns import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # Using the Titanic dataset sns.countplot(df[ 'Survived' ]) plt.show()

--- Page 46 ---
B. Pie Chart (Percentage) Useful for seeing the market share or percentage contribution of each category . Python # 'autopct' handles the mathematical percentage calculation automatically df[ 'Pclass' ].value_counts().plot(kind= 'pie' , autopct= '%.2f' ) plt.show() 3. Analyzing Numerical Data For numerical data, we want to understand the central tendency , spread, and the shape of the data distribution. A. Histogram Divides the data into "bins" (ranges) and shows how many data points fall into each range. Python plt.hist(df[ 'Age' ], bins= 20 ) plt.show() B. Distplot (KDE - Probability Density) A combination of a histogram and a Kernel Density Estimate (KDE). ● KDE Curve: Represents the probability density of the variable at any given value. ● Skewness: If the "tail" of the curve is longer on the right, it is Positively Skewed. If longer on the left, it is Negatively Skewed. Python sns.distplot(df[ 'Age' ]) plt.show()

--- Page 47 ---
C. Boxplot (Five-Number Summary) A boxplot provides a visual summary of five key mathematical points: 1. Minimum: The lowest value excluding outliers. 2. Q1 (25th Percentile): 25% of the data falls below this point. 3. Median (50th Percentile): The middle value of the dataset. 4. Q3 (75th Percentile): 75% of the data falls below this point. 5. Maximum: The highest value excluding outliers. Mathematical Outlier Detection: Points outside the "whiskers" are potential outliers. ● Interquartile Range (IQR): IQR = Q3 - Q1 ● Lower Bound: Q1 - 1.5 * IQR ● Upper Bound: Q3 + 1.5 * IQR Python sns.boxplot(df[ 'Age' ]) plt.show() 4. Mathematical Summary Functions (Pandas) While graphs are great, exact numbers help in precise reporting. Function What it Calculates df['Age'].mean() Average age. df['Age'].median() Middle value (less affected by outliers than mean).

--- Page 48 ---
df['Age'].std() Standard Deviation (how much values vary from the mean). df['Age'].skew() Measures the asymmetry of the distribution (0 = perfectly symmetrical). df['Age'].min() / .max() The absolute smallest and largest values in the column. Reference Video: EDA using Univariate Analysis - Day 20 EDA: Bivariate and Multivariate Analysis While Univariate analysis looks at one variable, Bivariate Analysis examines the relationship between two variables, and Multivariate Analysis looks at more than two variables simultaneously . This is essential for understanding how features interact and how they affect the target variable. 1. Numerical - Numerical Analysis When both columns contain numbers, we want to see if there's a correlation or a trend. A. Scatter Plot The most common tool for comparing two numerical variables. ● Logic: Each dot represents a single data point plotted on X and Y axes. ● Multivariate Extension: Use the hue parameter to add a categorical variable (color-coding dots by gender) or size to add another numerical dimension.

--- Page 50 ---

Pandas Profiling is a powerful Python library that automates the entire Exploratory Data Analysis (EDA) process. Instead of writing dozens of lines of code for univariate and bivariate analysis, you can generate a comprehensive interactive HTML report with just a few lines of code. 1. Installation and Basic Usage To use this tool, you first need to install the library using pip . Installation Code: Python # Run this in your terminal or a notebook cell pip install pandas-profiling Generating a Report: The following code imports a dataset (e.g., Titanic) and generates an HTML report. Python import pandas as pd from pandas_profiling import ProfileReport # 1. Load your data df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' ) # 2. Generate the profile report prof = ProfileReport(df) # 3. Export the report to an HTML file prof.to_file(output_file= 'output.html' )

--- Page 55 ---
2. Understanding the Report Sections The generated report is divided into several key sections, each providing deep mathematical and statistical insights. A. Overview This section gives a high-level summary of the entire dataset. ● Dataset Statistics: Total number of variables (columns), number of observations (rows), and total missing cells. ● Variable Types: Counts of how many columns are numeric, categorical, boolean, etc. ● Warnings: Automatically flags potential issues such as: ○ High Cardinality: A categorical column has too many unique values (e.g., Name). ○ High Correlation: Two variables are so similar that one might be redundant. ○ Missing Values: Highlights columns with a high percentage of null data. ○ Zeros: Flags columns with a large number of zero values. B. Variables (Univariate Analysis) For every column, the report provides: ● For Numeric Columns: Mean, median, minimum, maximum, range, interquartile range (IQR), standard deviation, skewness, and a histogram. ● For Categorical Columns: Number of unique values, most common values (modes), and a frequency bar chart or pie chart. C. Interactions (Bivariate Analysis) This section allows you to pick any two numerical variables and see a Scatter Plot of their relationship. It helps in identifying linear or non-linear patterns between features. D. Correlations The report generates multiple correlation matrices (heatmaps) using different mathematical methods:

--- Page 56 ---
● Pearson's R: Measures linear correlation. ● Spearman's Rho: Measures rank correlation (useful for non-linear but monotonic relationships). ● Kendall's Tau: Similar to Spearman's but better for smaller datasets. ● Phik (Φ k): A newer correlation coefficient that works for both categorical and interval variables. E. Missing Values Visualizes where data is missing using: ● Count: Bar chart of non-missing values per column. ● Matrix: A visual representation of where nulls appear in the rows. ● Heatmap/Dendrogram: Shows if the absence of data in one column is related to the absence of data in another . 3. Mathematical Significance Pandas Profiling performs the following calculations automatically: Skewness Calculation: It measures the lack of symmetry in a distribution. ● Standardized Skewness = Mean of ( (X - Mean) / StdDev )^3 ● If Skewness > 0, the tail is on the right side. Interquartile Range (IQR): ● IQR = Q3 (75th percentile) - Q1 (25th percentile) ● This is used to identify outliers in the boxplot and summary sections. 4. Why Use It? 1. Speed: It saves hours of manual plotting and table generation. 2. Completeness: It checks for things you might miss, like memory usage or "constant" columns that don't add value. 3. Portability: The HTML file can be shared with clients or team members who don't know how to code. Reference Video: Pandas Profiling - Day 22

--- Page 57 ---
Comprehensive Notes on Feature Engineering Feature Engineering is the process of using domain knowledge to extract or transform raw data into "features" (input variables) that make machine learning algorithms work better . It is often described as an art rather than a pure science because it relies heavily on a data scientist's intuition and understanding of the problem. 1. Why is Feature Engineering Important? A famous saying in machine learning is: "Garbage In, Garbage Out." ● Even a simple algorithm can perform exceptionally well if provided with high-quality , engineered features. ● Conversely , a powerful algorithm (like a Deep Neural Network) will fail if the features are poor or irrelevant. 2. Core Categories of Feature Engineering Feature Engineering can be broadly divided into four main areas: A. Feature Transformation Modifying existing features to make them more suitable for a model. ● Missing Value Imputation: Handling rows where data is absent (replacing with Mean, Median, or Mode). ● Handling Categorical Data: Converting text labels (like "Dog", "Cat") into numbers (One-Hot Encoding, Ordinal Encoding) because ML models only understand math. ● Outlier Handling: Detecting and removing "extreme" values that might skew the model's logic. ● Feature Scaling: Ensuring all features are on a similar scale (e.g., transforming both Age [0-100] and Salary [0-100,000] to a range of [0, 1]). B. Feature Construction Creating brand new columns from existing ones using logic or domain knowledge.

--- Page 58 ---
● Example (Titanic): Instead of using two separate columns SibSp (siblings/spouses) and Parch (parents/children), you create a single column FamilySize = SibSp + Parch + 1 . This provides a clearer picture of the passenger's social context. C. Feature Selection Identifying and keeping only the most important variables to reduce complexity and training time. ● Logic: Removing irrelevant features (like "Passenger ID" for predicting survival) prevents the model from learning "noise." D. Feature Extraction Using algorithms to create a smaller set of completely new features that capture the essence of the data. ● Common Method: PCA (Principal Component Analysis). It rotates and transforms high-dimensional data into fewer "Principal Components" without losing significant information. 3. Mathematical Concept: Feature Scaling (Standardization) Many algorithms (like KNN or SVM) calculate the Euclidean Distance between points. If one feature has a much larger range than another , it will dominate the distance calculation. Standardization Formula: z = (x - Mean) / Standard_Deviation This transforms the data so it has a Mean of 0 and a Standard Deviation of 1. 4. Explanation Code Block: Handling Categorical Data Since ML models cannot process text like "Male" or "Female" directly , we use One-Hot Encoding. Python

--- Page 59 ---

Reference Video: What is Feature Engineering - CampusX This video provides an overview of feature engineering, explaining its importance and the different techniques used to improve machine learning models. Feature Scaling: Standardization (Z-Score Normalization) Feature Scaling is a crucial step in Feature Engineering that involves bringing all independent features into a similar range. Standardization is one of the most popular techniques for this. 1. Why do we need Feature Scaling? Many Machine Learning algorithms use mathematical distances (like Euclidean Distance) to find patterns. ● Problem: If one feature has a range of 0-1 (e.g., CGP A) and another has 0-100,000 (e.g., Salary), the distance calculation will be dominated by the larger numbers.

--- Page 61 ---
● Consequence: The model will think Salary is much more important than CGP A simply because the numbers are bigger . Key Example: Algorithms like K-Nearest Neighbors (KNN), SVM, and Neural Networks (using Gradient Descent) are highly sensitive to the scale of the data. 2. Mathematical Explanation of Standardization Standardization transforms your data so that it has a Mean of 0 and a Standard Deviation of 1. This is also called Z-Score Normalization. The Formula: For every data point x_i in a feature: z = (x_i - Mean) / Standard_Deviation Where: ● Mean (u): The average value of all points in that feature. ● Standard Deviation (s): A measure of how spread out the numbers are. ● z: The new "standardized" value. Geometric Intuition: 1. Mean Centering: By subtracting the mean, we shift the entire dataset so its center is at 0. 2. Scaling: By dividing by the standard deviation, we "squash" or "stretch" the data so its spread is unit-sized (Standard Deviation = 1). 3. Practical Implementation with Python (Scikit-Learn) In Python, we use the StandardScaler class from Scikit-Learn. Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler

--- Page 62 ---
# 1. Load Data df = pd.read_csv( 'social_network_ads.csv' ) X = df[[ 'Age' , 'EstimatedSalary' ]] y = df[ 'Purchased' ] # 2. Split into Train and Test sets # IMPORT ANT: Always scale after splitting to avoid "Data Leakage" X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.3 ) # 3. Create and Fit the Scaler scaler = StandardScaler() # 'fit' calculates the Mean and Standard Deviation of the TRAINING set scaler .fit(X_train) # 4. Transform both sets # 'transform' applies the formula: (x - Mean) / StdDev X_train_scaled = scaler .transform(X_train) X_test_scaled = scaler .transform(X_test) # Convert back to DataFrame for easier viewing X_train_scaled = pd.DataFrame(X_train_scaled, columns=X_train.columns) print( "Mean of Scaled Age:" , X_train_scaled[ 'Age' ].mean()) # Close to 0 print( "Std of Scaled Age:" , X_train_scaled[ 'Age' ].std()) # Close to 1

--- Page 63 ---
4. Key Takeaways and Rules When to use Standardization? ● KNN & K-Means: Essential (distance-based). ● Principal Component Analysis (PCA): Essential (variance-based). ● Gradient Descent Algorithms: Linear Regression, Logistic Regression, and Neural Networks (converge much faster). ● Support Vector Machines (SVM): Essential. When is it NOT required? ● Tree-based models: Decision Trees, Random Forest, XGBoost, and Gradient Boosting. These algorithms don't use distances; they use "if-else" cuts which aren't affected by scale. Handling Outliers: Standardization does not remove outliers. Because it uses the Mean and Standard Deviation (which are heavily influenced by extreme values), the outliers remain outliers even in the standardized scale. You must handle them separately if needed. Summary Checklist: ● Scale after the train-test split. ● Use fit only on the Training set. ● Use transform on both the Training and Test sets. Reference Video: Feature Scaling - Standardization - Day 24 Normalization in Feature Engineering Normalization is a data preparation technique used to change the values of numeric columns in a dataset to a common scale, without distorting differences in the ranges of values or losing information. Unlike Standardization (which

--- Page 64 ---
centers data around a mean of 0 and a standard deviation of 1), Normalization typically "squeezes" data into a fixed range, like 0 to 1. 1. Why Normalization? When features have different units (e.g., Weight in kg vs. Height in cm), their magnitudes can differ wildly . Machine learning models that rely on distance calculations (like KNN) or gradient descent can perform poorly or take much longer to converge if features aren't on the same scale. Normalization eliminates unit-based bias. 2. Min-Max Scaling (The Most Common Technique) Min-Max scaling is the most popular form of normalization. it transforms all numerical values into a range between 0 and 1. Mathematical Derivation: For every data point x in a column: x_scaled = (x - x_min) / (x_max - x_min) ● Logic: * If x is the minimum value ( x_min ), the numerator becomes 0, so x_scaled is 0. ○ If x is the maximum value ( x_max ), the numerator and denominator are the same, so x_scaled is 1. ○ All other values fall proportionally between 0 and 1. Geometric Intuition: Imagine your data as a cloud of points in a multi-dimensional space. Min-Max scaling takes that cloud and compresses it so it fits perfectly inside a "unit box" (a square in 2D, a cube in 3D, or a hypercube in higher dimensions) where every side goes from 0 to 1. 3. Other Normalization Techniques A. Mean Normalization Instead of using the minimum, it centers the data using the mean. The resulting values usually range between -1 and 1.

--- Page 65 ---
Formula: x_scaled = (x - x_mean) / (x_max - x_min) ● Note: Scikit-Learn does not have a direct class for this; you have to implement it manually . B. Max Abs Scaling Useful for data that is already centered at zero or for sparse data (data with many zeros). It scales data to the range [-1, 1]. Formula: x_scaled = x / |x_max| ● When to use: Great for sparse datasets where you want to preserve zero entries. C. Robust Scaling If your data has outliers, Min-Max scaling is highly sensitive to them. Robust scaling uses the Median and Interquartile Range (IQR) to be "robust" against extreme values. Formula: x_scaled = (x - x_median) / IQR ● IQR = 75th Percentile - 25th Percentile. 4. Practical Implementation (Python) We use Scikit-Learn's MinMaxScaler . Python from sklearn.preprocessing import MinMaxScaler from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split import pandas as pd # Sample Data df = pd.read_csv( 'wine_data.csv' , usecols=[ 0 , 1 , 2 ]) X = df.drop( 'Class label' , axis= 1 )

--- Page 66 ---
