Fit to Training Data (Learns Min and Max) scaler .fit(X_train) # 4. Transform both Train and Test sets X_train_scaled = scaler .transform(X_train) X_test_scaled = scaler .transform(X_test) # Convert back to DataFrame to check results X_train_scaled = pd.DataFrame(X_train_scaled, columns=X_train.columns) print( "Min of scaled data:" , X_train_scaled. min ()) # Should be 0 print( "Max of scaled data:" , X_train_scaled. max ()) # Should be 1 5. Comparison: Normalization vs. Standardization Feature Standardization Normalization (Min-Max)

--- Page 67 ---
Output Range Unbounded (mostly -3 to 3) Strictly 0 to 1 Outliers Less affected by them Very sensitive to them When to use? Most algorithms (KNN, PCA, Gradient Descent) When you know the bounds (e.g., Image Pixels 0-255) Summary Checklist 1. Always split into training and testing sets before scaling to prevent "Data Leakage." 2. Use RobustScaler if your data has many outliers. 3. Use MinMaxScaler for image data (Deep Learning) or when you require a bounded 0-1 range. 4. StandardScaler is usually the safer "default" choice for most ML tasks. Reference Video: Feature Scaling - Normalization - Day 25 Encoding Categorical Data: Ordinal and Label Encoding Machine Learning algorithms require numerical input. When dealing with categorical data (data in text or label form), we must convert it into numbers. This process is called Encoding. 1. Types of Categorical Data Before encoding, you must identify which sub-type of categorical data you have: A. Nominal Data

--- Page 68 ---
Categories that have no inherent order or mathematical relationship between them. ● Examples: State (Maharashtra, Delhi, Goa), Gender (Male, Female), Branch (CSE, ECE, ME). ● Encoding Tool: One-Hot Encoding (covered in the next session). B. Ordinal Data Categories that have a defined order or rank. ● Examples: Education (High School < UG < PG), Review (Poor < Good < Excellent), Rank (1st, 2nd, 3rd). ● Encoding Tool: Ordinal Encoding. 2. Ordinal Encoding (for Input Features) Ordinal Encoding is used for independent variables (features) where the order matters. How it works: 1. Define the hierarchy of the categories. 2. Assign an integer starting from 0 to the lowest rank, increasing by 1 for each subsequent rank. Mathematical Example: Column: Education ● Categories: ['School', 'UG', 'PG'] ● Mapping: School -> 0 , UG -> 1 , PG -> 2 3. Label Encoding (for Target Labels) Label Encoding is mathematically similar to Ordinal Encoding but is strictly for the output variable (target label). Key Difference: ● Ordinal Encoder: Used on input features ($X$). Allows you to specify the order .

--- Page 69 ---
● Label Encoder: Used on output labels ($y$). Does not allow you to specify the order; it typically assigns numbers alphabetically . 4. Implementation Code Block (Python Scikit-Learn) A. Ordinal Encoding Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.preprocessing import OrdinalEncoder # Sample Data df = pd.DataFrame({ 'Review' : [ 'Good' , 'Average' , 'Poor' , 'Good' , 'Average' ], 'Education' : [ 'UG' , 'PG' , 'School' , 'PG' , 'UG' ] }) # Split first (Recommended) X_train, X_test = train_test_split(df, test_size= 0.2 ) # Initialize Encoder with specific order oe = OrdinalEncoder(categories=[ [ 'Poor' , 'Average' , 'Good' ], # Order for Review [ 'School' , 'UG' , 'PG' ] # Order for Education ])

--- Page 70 ---
# Fit and Transform X_train_encoded = oe.fit_transform(X_train) X_test_encoded = oe.transform(X_test) B. Label Encoding Python from sklearn.preprocessing import LabelEncoder # Sample Target (e.g., 'Yes' or 'No') y = [ 'Yes' , 'No' , 'No' , 'Yes' ] le = LabelEncoder() y_encoded = le.fit_transform(y) print(le.classes_) # Shows which index belongs to which label # Output: [0, 1] representing ['No', 'Yes'] 5. Summary Table for Beginners Feature Ordinal Encoding Label Encoding Applied to Input Features ($X$) Target Output ($y$) Order Control Yes, you define the ranks. No, usually alphabetical.

--- Page 71 ---
Scikit-Learn Class OrdinalEncoder LabelEncoder Best Use Case Ratings, Education Levels, Seniority Classification classes (Spam/Not Spam) Important Note: Do not use LabelEncoder for input features. It is technically possible, but OrdinalEncoder is designed for that purpose and handles DataFrames better . Reference Video: Encoding Categorical Data - CampusX One-Hot Encoding: Handling Nominal Categorical Data One-Hot Encoding (OHE) is a technique used to convert nominal categorical data (categories without a specific order) into a numerical format that machine learning models can understand. 1. What is One-Hot Encoding? In nominal data, like "Color" (Red, Blue, Green), there is no mathematical order (Red is not "greater than" Blue). If we assigned numbers like 1, 2, 3, a model might mistakenly learn that Green (3) is more important than Red (1). How it Works: 1. Identify Unique Categories: Find all unique labels in the column (e.g., Red, Blue, Green). 2. Create New Columns: Create a new binary column for each unique category . 3. Binary Assignment: For each row, place a 1 in the column corresponding to its category and 0 in all others.

--- Page 72 ---
Mathematical Representation: Original Data: [Red, Blue, Green] Encoded Vectors: ● Red: [1, 0, 0] ● Blue: [0, 1, 0] ● Green: [0, 0, 1] 2. The Dummy Variable Trap When we create $N$ columns for $N$ categories, we introduce a problem called Multicollinearity . This happens because the $N$-th column is perfectly predictable from the others. ● Example: If you have 3 colors and a row is NOT Red (0) and NOT Blue (0), it MUST be Green. ● The Trap: Including all $N$ columns can confuse models like Linear Regression. ● Solution: Always drop one column. We use $N-1$ columns to represent $N$ categories. 3. Handling High Cardinality If a column has many unique values (e.g., 500 different car brands), OHE will create 500 new columns, making the data massive and slow to process. Strategy: "Most Frequent" Encoding 1. Identify the top 10 or 20 most frequent categories. 2. Keep them as separate columns. 3. Group all other rare categories into a single new category called "Other" or "Uncommon." 4. Implementation Code Block (Python Scikit-Learn) While Pandas has get_dummies() , Scikit-Learn's OneHotEncoder is preferred for machine learning pipelines because it "remembers" the categories from the training set.

--- Page 73 ---
Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.preprocessing import OneHotEncoder # Sample Data df = pd.read_csv( 'cars.csv' ) # Columns: Brand, Fuel, Owner , Price X = df.drop( 'Price' , axis= 1 ) y = df[ 'Price' ] # Split Data X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 ) # Initialize OHE # drop='first' avoids the Dummy Variable Trap # sparse=False returns a standard array instead of a sparse matrix ohe = OneHotEncoder(drop= 'first' , sparse= False , dtype= 'int32' ) # Fit and Transform specific columns (Fuel and Owner) # Note: In real projects, we use ColumnT ransformer to automate this X_train_new = ohe.fit_transform(X_train[[ 'Fuel' , 'Owner' ]]) X_test_new = ohe.transform(X_test[[ 'Fuel' , 'Owner' ]]) print(X_train_new .shape)

--- Page 74 ---
5. Summary Table for Beginners Feature Ordinal Encoding One-Hot Encoding Data Type Ordinal (Ordered) Nominal (Unordered) Example Education (UG, PG, PhD) Gender , City, Color Output Single column with integers Multiple columns with 0s and 1s Trap Risk No Yes (Dummy Variable Trap) Key Rules 1. Use drop='first' to prevent multicollinearity . 2. Always handle rare categories before encoding if there are too many . 3. Use Scikit-Learn instead of Pandas for production-ready models. Reference Video: One Hot Encoding - Day 27 Column Transformer in Scikit-Learn A Column Transformer is a powerful feature in Scikit-Learn that allows you to apply different preprocessing transformations to different columns of your dataset simultaneously . Without it, you would have to manually split your data, transform each part, and then stitch them back together , which is tedious and prone to errors.

--- Page 75 ---
1. The Problem: Manual Preprocessing In a real-world dataset, you often have a mix of data types: ● Numerical columns with missing values (need Imputation). ● Categorical columns with a specific order (need Ordinal Encoding). ● Categorical columns without order (need One-Hot Encoding). ● Numerical columns that need scaling (Standardization). The Traditional Way: 1. Extract Column A -> Impute -> Save Result 1. 2. Extract Column B -> Encode -> Save Result 2. 3. Extract Column C -> Scale -> Save Result 3. 4. Join Result 1, 2, and 3 into a single array . 5. Repeat everything for the Test set. 2. The Solution: Column Transformer The ColumnT ransformer class from sklearn.compose automates this entire sequence. You define a list of "transformers," specify which columns they apply to, and run it once. Mathematical & Logical Concept: Think of the Column Transformer as a parallel processing engine. Input Data X is split vertically into the columns you specify . Each split goes through its designated mathematical function (Imputer , Encoder , etc.). Finally , the transformed splits are concatenated horizontally (along Axis 1) to form the final feature matrix. Resultant Matrix Shape: Total Rows x (Sum of new columns generated by each transformer) 3. Explanation Code Block: Using ColumnTransformer In this example, we handle a "Covid Patients" dataset where: 1. Fever: Needs Imputation (filling missing values with the mean).

--- Page 76 ---
2. Cough: Needs Ordinal Encoding (Mild < Strong). 3. Gender & City: Needs One-Hot Encoding (Nominal data). 4. Age: Stays as it is. Python from sklearn.compose import ColumnT ransformer from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer from sklearn.preprocessing import OneHotEncoder , OrdinalEncoder # 1. Define the Transformer transformer = ColumnT ransformer(transformers=[ # (name, transformer_object, column_names) ( 'tnf1' , SimpleImputer(), [ 'fever' ]), ( 'tnf2' , OrdinalEncoder(categories=[[ 'Mild' , 'Strong' ]]), [ 'cough' ]), ( 'tnf3' , OneHotEncoder(sparse= False , drop= 'first' ), [ 'gender' , 'city' ]) ], remainder= 'passthrough' ) # remainder='passthrough' keeps the columns not mentioned (like 'age') # 2. Fit and Transform the Training Data X_train_transformed = transformer .fit_transform(X_train) # 3. Transform the Test Data (No fitting here!) X_test_transformed = transformer .transform(X_test) 4. Key Parameters Explained ● transformers : A list of tuples. Each tuple contains:

--- Page 77 ---
○ Name: A string identifier (e.g., 'imputer'). ○ Estimator: The transformer object (e.g., StandardScaler() ). ○ Columns: The list of column names or indices to transform. ● remainder : * 'drop' (default): Removes columns not specified in the transformer . ○ 'passthrough' : Keeps original columns not specified. ● sparse_threshold : If the output is mostly zeros (from One-Hot Encoding), it returns a sparse matrix. Set to 0 to always get a dense NumPy array . 5. Summary Table for Beginners Feature Manual Preprocessing Column Transformer Effort High (lots of variables to track) Low (centralized definition) Risk of Data Leakage High Low Output Type Messy (mix of DF and Arrays) Consistent (NumPy Array) Integration Difficult to use in Pipelines Built for Pipelines Important Tip: Always use Scikit-Learn Indices or Column Names inside the transformer to ensure it works correctly when you switch between DataFrames and NumPy arrays. Reference Video: Column Transformer in Machine Learning - CampusX

--- Page 78 ---
Machine Learning Pipelines in Scikit-Learn A Pipeline is a mechanism in Scikit-Learn used to chain multiple steps of a machine learning workflow together . It ensures that the output of one step becomes the input to the next, creating a streamlined flow from raw data to a final prediction. 1. Why Use Pipelines? In a typical ML project, you perform several steps: 1. Imputation: Filling missing values. 2. Encoding: Converting text to numbers (One-Hot/Ordinal). 3. Scaling: Normalizing data ranges. 4. Feature Selection: Keeping only the best features. 5. Modeling: Training an algorithm (e.g., Decision Tree). The "Old Way" (Manual): You have to manually track every transformer and apply them in the exact same order to your training data and your testing/production data. If you change a step, you must update your code in multiple places. This is prone to Data Leakage and coding errors. The "Pipeline Way": You bundle all these steps into a single object. You only need to call pipe.fit() on your training data and pipe.predict() on new data. The pipeline handles all internal transformations automatically . 2. Mathematical & Logical Workflow Think of a Pipeline as a vertical stack of mathematical operations: ● Step 1 (Transformer): X1 = f1(X_raw) (e.g., Mean Imputation) ● Step 2 (Transformer): X2 = f2(X1) (e.g., One-Hot Encoding) ● Step 3 (Estimator): y_pred = Model.predict(X2) Logic: Every step except the last one must be a "Transformer" (has a .transform() method). The final step is typically an "Estimator" or "Model" (has a .fit() and .predict() method).

--- Page 79 ---
3. Explanation Code Block: Building a Pipeline In this example, we use the Titanic dataset to build a pipeline that handles missing values, encoding, scaling, and a Decision Tree model. Python from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline, make_pipeline from sklearn.compose import ColumnT ransformer from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer from sklearn.preprocessing import OneHotEncoder , MinMaxScaler from sklearn.tree import DecisionT reeClassifier from sklearn.feature_selection import SelectKBest, chi2 # 1. Handle Missing Values (Imputation) trf1 = ColumnT ransformer([ ( 'impute_age' , SimpleImputer(), [ 2 ]), # Age is at index 2 ( 'impute_embarked' , SimpleImputer(strategy= 'most_frequent' ), [ 6 ]) # Embarked index 6 ], remainder= 'passthrough' ) # 2. One Hot Encoding trf2 = ColumnT ransformer([ ( 'ohe_sex_embarked' , OneHotEncoder(sparse= False , handle_unknown= 'ignore' ), [ 1 , 6 ]) ], remainder= 'passthrough' ) # 3. Scaling trf3 = ColumnT ransformer([ ( 'scale' , MinMaxScaler(), slice ( 0 , 10 )) # Scale all columns

--- Page 80 ---
]) # 4. Feature Selection trf4 = SelectKBest(score_func=chi2, k= 8 ) # 5. Model Training trf5 = DecisionT reeClassifier() # --- Create the Pipeline --- pipe = Pipeline([ ( 'step1' , trf1), ( 'step2' , trf2), ( 'step3' , trf3), ( 'step4' , trf4), ( 'step5' , trf5) ]) # Train the entire chain pipe.fit(X_train, y_train) 4. Key Advantages for Production A. Code Simplicity In your production/website code, you only need to load one file (the exported pipeline) and call one function: Python

--- Page 81 ---
import pickle # Load the whole pipeline model = pickle.load( open ( 'pipe.pkl' , 'rb' )) # Predict on raw new data - NO manual preprocessing needed! result = model.predict(new_data) B. Prevention of Data Leakage By using a pipeline, you ensure that the parameters for scaling (like Mean or Min/Max) are calculated only on the training data and then applied to the test data. C. Cross-Validation Integration You can run cross-validation on the entire pipeline. This ensures that even the preprocessing steps are validated, not just the model. Python from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score cross_val_score(pipe, X_train, y_train, cv= 5 , scoring= 'accuracy' ).mean() 5. Pipeline vs. make_pipeline Feature Pipeline make_pipeline Naming You must name each step manually . Steps are named automatically based on classes.

--- Page 82 ---
Control Higher (good for debugging/tuning). Simpler/Faster to write. Summary Checklist ● Transformers: Use ColumnT ransformer inside pipelines to target specific columns. ● Indexing: Use column indices (e.g., [0, 1] ) instead of names if the intermediate output of a step is a NumPy array . ● Visualizing: Use sklearn.set_config(display='diagram') to see a visual flowchart of your pipeline in Jupyter . Reference Video: Machine Learning Pipelines A-Z - Day 29 Mathematical Transformations in Feature Engineering In machine learning, "Mathematical Transformation" is a feature engineering technique used to convert non-normal data distributions into normal (Gaussian) distributions. Many algorithms, especially linear models like Linear Regression and Logistic Regression, assume that the input data follows a normal distribution to perform optimally . 1. Why Transform Data? ● Model Performance: Algorithms like Linear Regression, Logistic Regression, and LDA perform better if features are normally distributed. ● Algorithm Assumptions: Some models assume that the error terms are normally distributed. ● Handling Skewness: Real-world data is often skewed. Transformations can "push" this skewed data towards a bell-shaped curve.

--- Page 83 ---
Note: Tree-based models (Decision Trees, Random Forest, XGBoost) generally do not require these transformations as they are not distance-based and don't assume normality . 2. Common Types of Transformations A. Log Transform One of the most popular techniques, especially for Right-Skewed data. It compresses the range of large values and expands the range of small values. ● Mathematical Formula: x' = log(x) or x' = log(1 + x) (to handle zeros). ● When to use: Right-skewed data (e.g., Wealth distribution, Ticket prices). ● Limitation: Cannot be used on negative values or zero (unless using log1p ). B. Reciprocal Transform Flips the distribution. Small values become large and large values become small. ● Mathematical Formula: x' = 1 / x ● When to use: Sometimes helpful for extremely skewed data. C. Power Transforms (Square & Square Root) ● Square Root Transform: x' = SquareRoot(x) . Used for right-skewed data. ● Square Transform: x' = x^2 . Used for Left-Skewed data. 3. How to Check for Normality? Before applying a transformation, you need to verify if the column is normal. 1. Distplot/Histogram: Visually check the "bell" shape. 2. Skewness Value: df.skew() . A value of 0 indicates perfect symmetry . 3. QQ Plot (Quantile-Quantile Plot): * If data points lie on the 45-degree line, the data is normally distributed. ○ If points deviate from the line, the data is skewed. 4. Implementation Code: Scikit-Learn FunctionTransformer

--- Page 84 ---
The FunctionT ransformer class in Scikit-Learn allows you to apply any mathematical function to your data. Python import numpy as np import pandas as pd import matplotlib.pyplot as plt import scipy .stats as stats from sklearn.preprocessing import FunctionT ransformer from sklearn.compose import ColumnT ransformer # Sample: Applying Log Transform to the 'Fare' column of Titanic # 'np.log1p' calculates log(1 + x) to handle zero values safely trf = FunctionT ransformer(func=np.log1p) # Fit and transform X_train_transformed = trf.fit_transform(X_train) # --- Visualization for verification --- plt.figure(figsize=( 14 , 4 )) plt.subplot( 121 ) stats.probplot(X_train[ 'Fare' ], dist= "norm" , plot=plt) plt.title( 'Fare Before Log Transform (QQ Plot)' ) plt.subplot( 122 ) stats.probplot(X_train_transformed[ 'Fare' ], dist= "norm" , plot=plt)

--- Page 85 ---
plt.title( 'Fare After Log Transform (QQ Plot)' ) plt.show() 5. Summary Matrix for Transformations Distribution Type Suggested Transform Scikit-Learn Function Right Skewed Log, Square Root, Reciprocal np.log1p , np.sqrt Left Skewed Square, Power lambda x: x**2 Target Distribution Normal (Gaussian) Goal of all transforms Practical Pro-Tip: Always split your data into Train and Test sets before applying these transformations. Apply the transformation to the training set and then use the same logic for the test set to avoid data leakage. Reference Video: Function Transformer - Day 30 Power Transformers: Box-Cox and Yeo-Johnson

--- Page 86 ---
Power Transformers are a family of parametric, monotonic transformations that are applied to make data more Gaussian-like (normal distribution). They are more sophisticated than simple function transforms (like log or square root) because they automatically find the best power parameter (lambda) to minimize skewness. 1. Why Use Power Transformers? Many machine learning algorithms, particularly linear models (Linear Regression, Logistic Regression, LDA), assume that the features follow a Gaussian distribution. Power transformers help: ● Stabilize Variance: Ensures the spread of data is consistent across the range. ● Minimize Skewness: Pulls skewed distributions towards a bell-shaped curve. ● Improve Performance: Leads to better results for distance-based and linear algorithms. 2. Types of Power Transforms A. Box-Cox Transform Developed by George Box and David Cox, this is a generalized power transform. ● Strict Constraint: It can only be applied to strictly positive data (x > 0). It does not work for zero or negative values. ● Mathematical Derivation: The transform is defined as: y = (x^lambda - 1) / lambda (if lambda != 0) y = ln(x) (if lambda = 0) ○ The algorithm searches for an optimal value of lambda between -5 and 5 to make the distribution as normal as possible. B. Yeo-Johnson Transform This is an extension of the Box-Cox transform designed to handle zero and negative values. ● Flexibility: It is the default choice in Scikit-Learn because it works on any numerical data. ● Mathematical Derivation: It uses a piecewise formula: ○ For x >= 0: Uses a formula similar to Box-Cox.

--- Page 87 ---
○ For x < 0: Uses a different power transform logic to handle negative magnitudes. 3. Comparing Methods Feature Box-Cox Yeo-Johnson Input Requirement Strictly Positive (x > 0) Any Real Number (Positive, Negative, Zero) Primary Goal Normality & Variance Stabilization Normality & Variance Stabilization Parameter Optimized Lambda Optimized Lambda Default in Sklearn No Yes 4. Practical Implementation (Python Scikit-Learn) We use the PowerT ransformer class. Note that this class automatically standardizes the data (sets mean to 0 and variance to 1) after applying the transform. Python import numpy as np import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.preprocessing import PowerT ransformer

--- Page 88 ---
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression from sklearn.metrics import r2_score # 1. 5. How to Check Results? To see if the transformer worked, you should look at: 1. QQ Plot: After transformation, the data points should align closely with the 45-degree diagonal line. 2. Histograms/KDE: The distribution should look like a bell curve. 3. Skewness: The value of df.skew() should move closer to zero. Summary Checklist ● Use Yeo-Johnson if your data contains zeros or negatives. ● Use Box-Cox only for strictly positive features. ● Always fit the transformer on the training set and transform the test set to prevent data leakage. ● These are most beneficial for Linear Models and Neural Networks. Reference Video: Power Transformer - Day 31 Handling Missing Numerical and Categorical Data In machine learning, "Missing Data" refers to the absence of values in certain rows or columns of your dataset. Most Scikit-Learn algorithms cannot handle missing values (NaN) automatically . If you try to train a model with missing data, your code will crash. Therefore, we must perform "Imputation"—the process of replacing missing data with estimated values. 1. Types of Imputation There are two broad ways to fill missing values: 1. Univariate Imputation: Filling the missing value of a column using only the values present in that same column (e.g., Mean/Median of the column). 2. Multivariate Imputation: Filling the missing value of a column by looking at relationships with other columns (e.g., using KNN or Iterative Imputation).

--- Page 90 ---
This guide focuses on Univariate Imputation using Scikit-Learn’ s SimpleImputer . 2. Handling Numerical Data For numerical columns (Age, Salary , Price), we generally use the Mean or Median. When to use what? ● Mean: Use this if your data is "Normally Distributed" (follows a bell curve) and does not have many outliers. ● Median: Use this if your data is "Skewed" or has "Outliers." The median is more robust because it is the middle value and isn't pulled away by extreme high or low numbers. Mathematical Explanation: Suppose we have a column: [10, 20, NaN, 40, 1000] 1. Mean Imputation: Calculation: (10 + 20 + 40 + 1000) / 4 = 1070 / 4 = 267.5 The NaN is replaced by 267.5. Note how the outlier (1000) made the mean very high. 2. Median Imputation: Sorted List: [10, 20, 40, 1000] Middle Value: (20 + 40) / 2 = 30 The NaN is replaced by 30. This is much more representative of the "normal" values in the list. Code Block: Numerical Imputation Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split # Sample Data df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' )

--- Page 91 ---
X = df[[ 'Age' , 'Fare' ]] y = df[ 'Survived' ] # Always split before imputation to avoid data leakage X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 ) # Define the imputer (using median) imputer = SimpleImputer(strategy= 'median' ) # Fit on training data only (calculate median) imputer .fit(X_train) # Transform both train and test X_train_filled = imputer .transform(X_train) X_test_filled = imputer .transform(X_test) 3. Handling Categorical Data For categorical columns (Gender , City, Color), we cannot calculate a Mean or Median. We use the Mode or a Constant. 1. Most Frequent (Mode): Replacing NaN with the category that appears most often. Use this when the missingness is random. 2. Constant/Missing Label: Replacing NaN with a specific word like "Missing" or "Unknown." Use this when the "missingness" itself might be a pattern the model needs to learn. Instead of just filling the number , we add a new boolean column (0 or 1) that tells the model: "This value was originally missing." Age_Is_Missing : [0, 1, 0] Code Block: Missing Indicator Python # You can do this automatically in SimpleImputer imputer = SimpleImputer(add_indicator= True ) # This will return the imputed column AND a new column indicating missingness

Code Block: GridSearch for Imputation Python from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline from sklearn.model_selection import GridSearchCV from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression # Create a pipeline pipe = Pipeline([ ( 'imputer' , SimpleImputer()), ( 'model' , LogisticRegression()) ]) # Define options to test param_grid = { 'imputer__strategy' : [ 'mean' , 'median' ] } # Run the search grid = GridSearchCV(pipe, param_grid, cv= 5 )

--- Page 94 ---
grid.fit(X_train, y_train) print( "Best strategy found:" , grid.best_params_) Summary for Beginners ● Imputation is mandatory because ML models don't like empty spaces. ● Numerical: Use Mean for normal data, Median for skewed data. ● Categorical: Use Most Frequent (Mode). ● Missing Indicator: Always a good idea to set add_indicator=T rue to let the model know which values were filled. ● Workflow: Always split into Train/T est BEFORE fitting the imputer to prevent the model from "cheating" by seeing test data averages. Reference Video: Handling Missing Data - Day 32 Handling Mixed Variables in Feature Engineering In real-world datasets, you will often encounter columns that don't fit strictly into "Numerical" or "Categorical" buckets. These are called Mixed Variables. A mixed variable contains both numbers and strings (text) within the same column. 1. What are Mixed Variables? A mixed variable is a feature where the data follows a pattern consisting of both a label and a numeric value. Examples from the Titanic Dataset: 1. Cabin: Values like "C85", "B5", or "E46". ○ The letter (C, B, E) represents the Deck. ○ The number (85, 5, 46) represents the Room Number .

--- Page 95 ---
2. Ticket: Values like "A/5 21171" or "PC 17599". ○ The text represents a ticket prefix or class. ○ The digits represent the unique ticket number . Why handle them? If you leave "C85" as a string, a machine learning model treats it as a unique category . It won't understand that "C85" and "C123" both belong to the same deck (C). By splitting them, we help the model find hidden patterns. 2. The Logical Derivation (The "How-To") The mathematical logic here is based on String Parsing and Extraction. We use Regular Expressions (Regex) to separate the components. Step 1: Identify the Components We look at a mixed variable and decide what parts are useful. Usually: ● The Categorical part (letters) might indicate a group or location. ● The Numerical part might indicate a sequence, price, or specific ID. Step 2: Extraction Formula We use logic to find: ● Numerical part: Find all digits (0-9) in the string. ● Categorical part: Find all characters (A-Z) in the string. Step 3: Imputation After splitting, you might find that some rows had only numbers or only letters. You must handle the resulting "NaN" (Not a Number) values in your new columns using standard imputation techniques (Mean, Median, or Mode). 3. Implementation Code Block (Python Pandas) We will simulate a dataset with a mixed variable and show how to extract the numerical and categorical parts. Python

--- Page 96 ---
import pandas as pd import numpy as np # Creating a sample dataset data = { 'mixed_col' : [ 'A123' , 'B456' , 'A789' , 'C111' , 'B222' , 'A333' ]} df = pd.DataFrame(data) print( "Original Data:" ) print(df) # 1. Extract the Categorical Part (The first letter) # .str[0] takes the character at index 0 df[ 'category_part' ] = df[ 'mixed_col' ]. str [ 0 ] # 2. Extract the Numerical Part # .str.extract('(\d+)') looks for one or more digits df[ 'numeric_part' ] = df[ 'mixed_col' ]. str .extract( '(\d+)' ) # 3. Convert numeric_part to actual integers (it's extracted as a string) df[ 'numeric_part' ] = pd.to_numeric(df[ 'numeric_part' ]) print( "\nTransformed Data:" ) print(df) # Now you can drop the original mixed column

--- Page 97 ---
df.drop(columns=[ 'mixed_col' ], inplace= True ) 4. Handling Titanic "Cabin" Column In the Titanic dataset, the Cabin column is a classic example. Here is how you handle it professionally: Python # Assuming 'df' is the Titanic dataframe # Cabin looks like: 'C123', 'E46', etc. # Extract the Deck (The Letter) df[ 'deck' ] = df[ 'Cabin' ]. str [ 0 ] # Extract the Room Number (The digits) df[ 'room_num' ] = df[ 'Cabin' ]. str .extract( '(\d+)' ) # Handle Missing Values # Fill missing decks with 'Unknown' (Categorical) df[ 'deck' ].fillna( 'Unknown' , inplace= True ) # Fill missing room numbers with 0 or the median (Numerical) df[ 'room_num' ].fillna( 0 , inplace= True ) df[ 'room_num' ] = df[ 'room_num' ].astype( int ) print(df[[ 'Cabin' , 'deck' , 'room_num' ]].head())

If the "Categorical part" has too many unique values (High Cardinality), you might need to group rare ones into an "Others" category before feeding it to your model. Reference Video: Handling Mixed Variables - Day 33 Handling Date and Time Features in Machine Learning Machine Learning models (like Linear Regression or Random Forest) are essentially mathematical engines. They cannot process a string like "2023-12-25 14:30:00" directly . If you feed a date as a string, the model treats it as a random

--- Page 99 ---
label. To make dates useful, we must perform Feature Engineering to extract numerical patterns. 1. Converting to Datetime Objects The first step in Python is converting a column from a "String" (object) to a "Datetime" object using Pandas. This unlocks specialized tools for extraction. Code Logic: df['date_column'] = pd.to_datetime(df['date_column']) 2. Extracting Date Features Once converted, we can break a single date into multiple columns. Each of these columns represents a specific numerical attribute that a model can learn from. A. Basic Components ● Year: Captures long-term trends (e.g., inflation over years). ● Month: Captures monthly seasonality (e.g., high sales in December). ● Day: Captures patterns within a month (e.g., salary day spending). B. Derived Components ● Day of the Week: (0 = Monday , 6 = Sunday). This helps models identify "Weekend vs. Weekday" behavior . ● Month Name: Often converted to numbers, but helps in identifying specific seasonal peaks. ● Quarter: (1, 2, 3, or 4). Useful for financial data analysis. ● Is Weekend?: A binary feature (0 or 1). Logical Derivation for "Is Weekend": If we define Day_of_week as d, where Monday is 0 and Sunday is 6: Is_W eekend = 1 if d > 4 else 0 3. Extracting Time Features

--- Page 100 ---
If your data includes timestamps (hours, minutes, seconds), these are vital for identifying daily cycles. ● Hour: (0 to 23). Helps identify morning peaks or late-night activity . ● Minute/Second: Usually less important unless dealing with high-frequency data like stock trading or server logs. 4. Time Difference (Duration) Often, the most "predictive" feature isn't the date itself, but the time elapsed between two events. Mathematical Formula: Duration = Event_T ime_2 - Event_T ime_1 Examples: ● Delivery Time: Delivery_Date - Order_Date ● User Seniority: Current_Date - Registration_Date 5. Comprehensive Code Implementation This block demonstrates how to take a raw dataset and extract all the features discussed above. Python import pandas as pd # Creating a sample dataset data = { 'date' : [ '2023-01-01 09:00:00' , '2023-05-15 18:30:00' , '2023-12-25 12:00:00' ]} df = pd.DataFrame(data) # Step 1: Convert to datetime object df[ 'date' ] = pd.to_datetime(df[ 'date' ])

--- Page 101 ---
# Step 2: Extract Date Features df[ 'year' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.year df[ 'month' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.month df[ 'day' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.day df[ 'day_of_week' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.dayofweek # Monday=0, Sunday=6 df[ 'day_name' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.day_name() df[ 'quarter' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.quarter # Step 3: Extract "Is Weekend" (Logical Transformation) # Using a lambda function: if dayofweek is 5 (Sat) or 6 (Sun), mark 1 df[ 'is_weekend' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.dayofweek.apply( lambda x: 1 if x > 4 else 0 ) # Step 4: Extract Time Features df[ 'hour' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.hour df[ 'minute' ] = df[ 'date' ].dt.minute # Step 5: Time Difference (Example) # Let's assume today is 2024-01-01 today = pd.to_datetime( '2024-01-01' ) df[ 'days_elapsed' ] = (today - df[ 'date' ]).dt.days # Display the result print(df.drop(columns=[ 'date' ]))

--- Page 102 ---
6. Summary for Beginners Feature Why it matters? Numerical Range Month Seasonality (Summer/W inter patterns) 1 to 12 Day of Week Weekly patterns (Shopping on Sundays) 0 to 6 Hour Daily cycles (Traffic peaks, electricity usage) 0 to 23 Duration Measures the gap between actions 0 to Infinity Crucial Note: After you extract these features, you should drop the original Date/T ime column, as the model cannot use it and it will cause errors during the training phase. Reference Video: Handling Dates and Time - Day 34 Handling Missing Data: Complete Case Analysis (CCA) Missing data is a common problem in machine learning. Scikit-learn and most other ML libraries cannot handle datasets with missing values ( NaN or None ). As a data scientist, you must resolve these missing values before training your model.

--- Page 103 ---
1. Options for Handling Missing Data When you encounter missing values, you generally have two paths: A. Removal (Complete Case Analysis) You simply delete the rows or columns that contain missing values. This is what we focus on in these notes. B. Imputation (Filling) You fill the missing spots with estimated values. ● Univariate: Filling values using only that specific column (e.g., Mean, Median, Mode, or Random values). ● Multivariate: Using other columns to predict the missing value (e.g., KNN Imputer or MICE - Multivariate Imputation by Chained Equations). 2. What is Complete Case Analysis (CCA)? Complete Case Analysis, also known as Listwise Deletion, consists of discarding observations (rows) where any of the variables (columns) are missing. In simple terms: if a row has even one missing value in any of the features you are using, you delete the entire row. You only analyze the "complete cases." 3. The Core Assumption: MCAR For CCA to be valid, your data must be Missing Completely At Random (MCAR). Mathematical Logic of MCAR: In plain terms, MCAR means that the probability of a value being missing is the same for all observations. It does not depend on the value itself or the values of other variables in the dataset. If we represent the missingness as a variable 'M': ● P(Missing | Data) = P(Missing) If this condition is met, the "complete cases" are effectively a random subsample of the original data. Therefore, the statistical properties (Mean, Variance,

--- Page 104 ---
Distribution) of the dataset will remain the same after you delete the missing rows. 4. When to Use CCA? While there is no hard rule, the industry standard suggests using CCA only if: 1. The data is Missing Completely At Random (MCAR). 2. The missing data is less than 5% of the total dataset. ○ Note: If a specific column has 95% missing data, it's better to drop that entire column rather than dropping rows. 5. Pros and Cons Advantages: ● Ease of implementation: No complex math or models needed; just a single line of code. ● Preserves Variable Relationships: Since you aren't "guessing" values (imputation), you aren't introducing artificial relationships between variables. Disadvantages: ● Data Loss: If missingness is spread across many rows, you might end up deleting a huge chunk of your dataset. ● Bias: If data is NOT MCAR (e.g., people with high salaries are less likely to disclose them), deleting those rows will bias your model. ● Production Issue: If your model is trained on "perfect" data (no missing values), it will fail or crash in a real-world scenario if it receives a missing value because it never learned how to handle one. 6. Code Implementation (Pandas) To perform CCA, we use the dropna() function in Python's Pandas library . Python import pandas as pd

--- Page 105 ---
import numpy as np # Load your dataset df = pd.read_csv( 'data.csv' ) # 1. Check percentage of missing values per column # Formula: (Sum of nulls / Total rows) * 100 missing_percent = df.isnull().mean() * 100 print(missing_percent) # 2. Identify columns with < 5% missing values to apply CCA cols_to_use = [col for col in df.columns if df[col].isnull().mean() < 0.05 and df[col].isnull().mean() > 0 ] # 3. Apply Complete Case Analysis # This drops rows where any of the specified columns have missing values new_df = df.dropna(subset=cols_to_use) # Compare sizes print( "Original size:" , len (df)) print( "Size after CCA:" , len (new_df)) 7. Validating the CCA (Crucial Step) After dropping rows, you must verify that the distribution of the data hasn't changed.

--- Page 106 ---
For Numerical Data: You should plot the Histogram or KDE (Kernel Density Estimate) of the column before and after dropping rows. If the curves overlap almost perfectly , the data was likely MCAR and CCA is safe. Python import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # Comparison plot for a numerical column 'training_hours' fig = plt.figure() ax = fig.add_subplot( 111 ) # Original data (Red) df[ 'training_hours' ].hist(bins= 50 , ax=ax, density= True , color= 'red' ) # Data after CCA (Green) new_df[ 'training_hours' ].hist(bins= 50 , ax=ax, density= True , color= 'green' , alpha= 0.8 ) plt.show() For Categorical Data: For categories, you check the Ratio of each category . Mathematical check: 1. Ratio_Before = Count(Category_A) / Total_Rows_Before 2. Ratio_After = Count(Category_A) / Total_Rows_After If Ratio_Before is approximately equal to Ratio_After , you are good to go. Python

--- Page 107 ---
# Check ratios for 'education_level' temp = pd.concat([ df[ 'education_level' ].value_counts() / len (df), # Ratio Before new_df[ 'education_level' ].value_counts() / len (new_df) # Ratio After ], axis= 1 ) temp.columns = [ 'original' , 'after_cca' ] print(temp) If you see a significant change (e.g., a category dropping from 20% of the data to 10%), it means the missing data was NOT random, and CCA has introduced a bias. In that case, you should use Imputation instead. Handling Missing Numerical Data: Univariate Imputation When dealing with missing values in numerical columns, we often use Univariate Imputation. This means we look only at the values within that specific column to fill in the missing spots, without considering information from other columns. 1. Types of Numerical Imputation There are several ways to fill (impute) numerical data. The three most common techniques are: 1. Mean/Median Imputation 2. Arbitrary Value Imputation 3. End of Distribution Imputation

--- Page 108 ---
2. Mean and Median Imputation This is the most common and simplest technique. You calculate the Mean (average) or Median (middle value) of the available data and use it to replace every missing entry in that column. Mathematical Logic: ● Mean: Sum of all non-missing values / Number of non-missing values. ● Median: The middle value when all non-missing values are sorted. When to use which? ● Normal Distribution: If your data is bell-shaped (symmetrical), Mean, Median, and Mode are all the same. You can use the Mean. ● Skewed Distribution: If your data has outliers or is pulled to one side, the Mean gets dragged by the outliers. In this case, the Median is a better representation of the "typical" value. Key Assumptions: ● Data is Missing Completely At Random (MCAR). ● The missing data is less than 5% (ideally). Pros: ● Very easy to implement. ● Fast for production servers. Cons: ● Changes the Variance: Because you are adding many identical values (the mean), the spread of the data shrinks. ● Distorts the Distribution: The "peak" of your histogram at the mean/median value will become unnaturally tall. ● Affects Covariance: It can weaken the mathematical relationship between this column and others. 3. Arbitrary Value Imputation

--- Page 109 ---
In this technique, you replace missing values with an arbitrary number that does not exist in the dataset, such as -1, 99, or 999. The Purpose: The goal is to intentionally "flag" the missing values so the machine learning model can differentiate between rows that had data and rows that didn't. Pros: ● Easy to implement. ● Captures the importance of "missingness" if the fact that data is missing is actually a signal. Cons: ● Distorts the mean and variance significantly . ● Creates artificial outliers. 4. End of Distribution Imputation This is a more sophisticated version of Arbitrary Value Imputation. Instead of picking a random number like 999, you pick a value at the far "tail" of the distribution. Mathematical Derivation: If the distribution is Normal (Gaussian): You use the 3-sigma rule. Values beyond 3 standard deviations are considered extreme. ● Value = Mean + 3 * Standard_Deviation ● Value = Mean - 3 * Standard_Deviation If the distribution is Skewed: You use the IQR (Interquartile Range) proximity rule: ● Upper_Limit = Q3 + 1.5 * IQR ● Lower_Limit = Q1 - 1.5 * IQR ● Where IQR = Q3 - Q1 (Q3 is 75th percentile, Q1 is 25th percentile).

--- Page 110 ---
By imputing with these values, you ensure the imputed value is far away from the rest of the data but still mathematically related to the distribution. 5. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) While you can do this in Pandas using fillna() , using Scikit-learn's SimpleImputer is better for production pipelines because it remembers the values from the training set to apply to the test set. Python from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer from sklearn.compose import ColumnT ransformer import pandas as pd # Load data df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' , usecols=[ 'Age' , 'Fare' , 'Survived' ]) # Always split before imputation to avoid data leakage X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(df.drop( 'Survived' , axis= 1 ), df[ 'Survived' ], test_size= 0.2 ) # Define different strategies for different columns # We use Median for 'Age' and Mean for 'Fare' trf = ColumnT ransformer([ ( 'impute_age' , SimpleImputer(strategy= 'median' ), [ 'Age' ]), ( 'impute_fare' , SimpleImputer(strategy= 'mean' ), [ 'Fare' ])

--- Page 111 ---
], remainder= 'passthrough' ) # Fit on training data (calculates mean/median) trf.fit(X_train) # Transform both train and test X_train_transformed = trf.transform(X_train) X_test_transformed = trf.transform(X_test) # To perform Arbitrary Value Imputation: # strategy='constant', fill_value=99 6. How to Validate Your Imputation To ensure your imputation didn't "break" the data, check these three things: 1. Variance Check: 2. Python print( 'Original Variance:' , X_train[ 'Age' ].var()) print( 'Imputed Variance:' , X_train_transformed_df[ 'Age' ].var()) 3. 4. (A slight decrease is normal, but a massive drop is a red flag). 5. Distribution Check: Plot a KDE (Kernel Density Estimate) plot before and after . If the shape changes drastically , try a different imputation method. 6. Correlation Check: Check df.corr() before and after . If the relationship between variables (e.g., Age and Fare) changes significantly , the imputation has introduced bias.

--- Page 112 ---
Summary Table Method When to use? Main Advantage Main Risk Mean Normal distribution, < 5% missing Simple Shrinks variance Median Skewed distribution, < 5% missing Robust to outliers Shrinks variance Arbitrary Data NOT missing at random Flags missingness Distorts stats End of Dist Data NOT missing at random Mathematical "flag" Creates outliers Note: Always calculate the imputation values (Mean/Median) on the Training Set only and use those same values to fill the Test Set. Calculating them on the whole dataset leads to Data Leakage. Handling Missing Categorical Data: Univariate Imputation When dealing with categorical data (text/labels), we cannot calculate the Mean or Median. Instead, we use techniques specific to categories. The two most common methods are Most Frequent Imputation (Mode) and Missing Category Imputation.

--- Page 113 ---
1. Most Frequent Imputation (Mode) This is the categorical equivalent of Mean/Median imputation. You find the category that appears most often in the column (the Mode) and use it to replace all missing values. Mathematical Logic: Let $C$ be the set of all non-missing categories in a column. The Mode $M$ is defined as: $M = \text{argmax}_{c \in C} (\text{count}(c))$ Basically , it's the category with the highest frequency . When to use? ● The data is Missing Completely At Random (MCAR). ● The missing data is less than 5%. ● There is a dominant category (one category that appears significantly more than the others). Pros: ● Very simple to implement. ● Works well if the most frequent category is highly probable. Cons: ● Changes the Distribution: By adding more of the most frequent category , you make the data even more biased toward that category . ● Deceiving Results: If categories are balanced (e.g., 30% New York, 30% London, 30% Paris), picking one arbitrarily as the mode to fill 10% missing data will distort the true ratio. 2. Missing Category Imputation Instead of "guessing" the missing value, you treat the absence of data as information itself. You replace all NaN values with a new label, typically the string "Missing" or "Unknown". Logic:

--- Page 114 ---
You are explicitly telling the machine learning model: "This specific data point was not provided." This creates a new, separate category in your data. When to use? ● The data is NOT Missing At Random. ● The missing percentage is high (e.g., > 10% or 20%). ● The fact that data is missing might be a signal (e.g., people without a garage might leave the 'Garage Quality' column blank). Pros: ● No "guessing" or distortion of existing categories. ● Captures the information that data was missing. Cons: ● Increases the number of categories (dimensionality), which might be an issue for models if you have many columns. 3. Implementation Code (Pandas) Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np # Load data df = pd.read_csv( 'house_price.csv' , usecols=[ 'GarageQual' , 'FireplaceQu' ]) # --- 1. Most Frequent Imputation --- # Find the mode mode_val = df[ 'GarageQual' ].mode()[ 0 ] # Fill missing values df[ 'GarageQual_imputed' ] = df[ 'GarageQual' ].fillna(mode_val)

--- Page 115 ---
# --- 2. Missing Category Imputation --- df[ 'FireplaceQu_imputed' ] = df[ 'FireplaceQu' ].fillna( 'Missing' ) 4. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) Using Scikit-Learn's SimpleImputer is preferred for production as it handles the training/test split correctly . Python from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer from sklearn.compose import ColumnT ransformer # Setup the strategies imputer_mode = SimpleImputer(strategy= 'most_frequent' ) imputer_missing = SimpleImputer(strategy= 'constant' , fill_value= 'Missing' ) # Apply to specific columns trf = ColumnT ransformer([ ( 'impute_mode' , imputer_mode, [ 'GarageQual' ]), ( 'impute_missing' , imputer_missing, [ 'FireplaceQu' ]) ], remainder= 'passthrough' ) # Fit and Transform # Note: Always fit on training data only to avoid leakage X_train_transformed = trf.fit_transform(X_train)

--- Page 116 ---
5. Validation Check To see if your imputation was successful and safe, compare the Probability Density Function (PDF) of the target variable (e.g., House Price) for the original categories vs. the imputed categories. Interpretation: ● If the price distribution for the "Mode" group stays roughly the same after imputation, the method is safe. ● If the "Missing" category shows a very different price distribution than other categories, it proves that the data was not missing at random, and creating a "Missing" label was the correct choice. Summary Table Feature Most Frequent (Mode) Missing Category Imputation Best For MCAR data, small % missing Non-random data, large % missing Data Type Categorical Categorical New Label? No, uses existing mode Yes, creates "Missing" label Risk Distorts ratios Adds a new category Pro Tip: For Fireplace Quality , often the data is missing because the house simply doesn't have a fireplace. In this scenario, "Missing" is a real feature of the house, not just an error in data collection.

--- Page 117 ---
KNN Imputer: Multivariate Imputation In previous notes, we discussed Univariate Imputation (using only one column to fill its own missing values). Today , we cover Multivariate Imputation, specifically the KNN Imputer . In multivariate imputation, we look at the entire row and the relationships between all columns to estimate a missing value. 1. The Core Idea of KNN Imputation KNN stands for K-Nearest Neighbors. The logic is simple: If a data point (row) has a missing value, we find other data points that are most "similar" to it. We then take the values from those similar rows to fill the gap. The Logic Steps: 1. Find the Neighbors: Identify the $K$ most similar rows (neighbors) to the row with the missing value. 2. Calculate the Value: Replace the missing value with the average (mean) of that specific feature from those $K$ neighbors. 2. Mathematical Derivation: Similarity & Distance How do we define "similarity"? We use distance. The closer two points are in multi-dimensional space, the more similar they are. A. Euclidean Distance (The Standard) In a 2D space (two columns), the distance between point A and point B is: Distance = sqrt( (x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2 ) In a 4D space (four columns): Distance = sqrt( (w2 - w1)^2 + (x2 - x1)^2 + (y2 - y1)^2 + (z2 - z1)^2 ) B. Nan-Euclidean Distance (Handling Missing Values) Standard Euclidean distance fails if one of the coordinates is missing. To solve this, Scikit-learn uses a modified formula called NaN-Euclidean Distance.

--- Page 118 ---
Formula: Distance = sqrt( (Total_Columns / Present_Columns) * Sum_of_Squared_Differences ) Weight Calculation: Weight = (Total_Number_of_Coordinates) / (Number_of_Present_Coordinates) Example: Imagine you have 3 columns: Age, Salary , and Experience. Row 1: [25, 50000, 2] Row 2: [24, NaN, 1.5] To find the distance between them, we only use the columns present in both (Age and Experience). 1. Sum_of_Squared_Differences = (25-24)^2 + (2-1.5)^2 = 1 + 0.25 = 1.25 2. Total_Columns = 3 3. Present_Columns = 2 4. Weight = 3 / 2 = 1.5 5. Final Distance = sqrt( 1.5 * 1.25 ) = sqrt( 1.875 ) 3. The "K" Factor The variable K determines how many neighbors you look at. ● If K = 1: You find the single closest row and copy its value. ● If K = 3: You find the 3 closest rows and take their average. 4. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) The KNNImputer class is available in sklearn.impute . Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split

--- Page 119 ---
from sklearn.impute import KNNImputer # Load dataset df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' , usecols=[ 'Age' , 'Pclass' , 'Fare' , 'Survived' ]) # Train-T est Split (Crucial to avoid data leakage) X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(df.drop( 'Survived' , axis= 1 ), df[ 'Survived' ], test_size= 0.2 ) # Initialize KNN Imputer # n_neighbors: The 'K' value (default is 5) # weights: 'uniform' (all neighbors equal) or 'distance' (closer neighbors have more say) imputer = KNNImputer(n_neighbors= 5 , weights= 'uniform' ) # Fit on training data and transform X_train_imputed = imputer .fit_transform(X_train) X_test_imputed = imputer .transform(X_test) # Convert back to DataFrame if needed X_train_imputed_df = pd.DataFrame(X_train_imputed, columns=X_train.columns) 5. Weights: Uniform vs. Distance ● Uniform (Default): Every neighbor gets one "vote." If you have 3 neighbors with values 10, 20, and 60, the imputed value is (10+20+60)/3 = 30 .

--- Page 120 ---
● Distance: Neighbors that are closer to your target row get more importance (weight). ○ Weight = 1 / distance ○ This is often more accurate because a row that is 1 unit away is more relevant than a row that is 10 units away . 6. Pros and Cons Pros: ● Highly Accurate: Usually outperforms Mean/Median imputation because it understands relationships between columns. ● No Manual Selection: It automatically finds the best values based on existing data patterns. Cons: ● Computationally Expensive: You have to calculate the distance from the missing row to every other row in the dataset. This is slow for very large datasets. ● Deployment Memory: To use this model in production, you must store the entire training dataset on your server to calculate distances for new incoming data. ● Outlier Sensitivity: It can be affected by outliers if K is too small. Summary Table Feature Univariate (Mean/Median) Multivariate (KNN) Accuracy Low/Medium High Speed Very Fast Slow

--- Page 121 ---
Memory usage Low High Best For Large datasets, Simple tasks Small/Medium datasets, Complex data Note: Before using KNN Imputer , it is usually a good idea to scale your data (StandardScaler or MinMaxScaler) because KNN relies on distance. If one column has values in the millions and another in the units, the distance calculation will be dominated by the larger numbers. Multivariate Imputation by Chained Equations (MICE) / Iterative Imputer MICE is one of the most sophisticated and popular techniques for handling missing data. Unlike simple mean/median imputation (univariate) or KNN (which uses distance), MICE treats each feature with missing values as a "target" and uses all other features as "predictors" in a machine learning model. 1. Core Concept: Why "Chained Equations"? The "chained" part of MICE refers to the fact that it runs a series of regression models (chains) in an iterative process. It doesn't just fill a value once; it keeps updating that value based on the latest estimates of all other columns until the values stabilize. Main Assumption: MICE works best when data is Missing At Random (MAR). This means the missingness in one column can be explained or predicted by the information available in other columns.

--- Page 122 ---
2. Mathematical Derivation: The Iterative Process Let's say you have a dataset with three columns: Age, Salary , and Experience, and all three have some missing values. Step 1: Initial Imputation Every missing value in the dataset is filled with a simple placeholder (usually the Mean of that column). Now, the dataset has no missing values, but many of the values are just "guesses." Step 2: The First Chain (Age) 1. Take the column Age. Change the placeholder (mean) values back to NaN (missing). 2. Now, treat Age as the Dependent Variable (Y) and Salary and Experience as Independent Variables (X). 3. Train a regression model: Age ~ Salary + Experience . 4. Use this model to predict the missing values in the Age column. 5. Update the Age column with these new, more accurate predictions. Step 3: The Second Chain (Salary) 1. Now take the column Salary . Change its initial placeholders back to NaN . 2. Treat Salary as (Y) and the updated Age and Experience as (X). 3. Train a model: Salary ~ Age + Experience . 4. Update the Salary column with the new predictions. Step 4: Repeat for all columns Perform this for every column that had missing data. This completes Iteration 1. Step 5: Iteration and Convergence Repeat Steps 2 through 4 for many cycles (e.g., 10 or 20 iterations). In each new cycle, the models use the updated values from the previous cycle. Convergence: You calculate the difference between the values in Iteration $N$ and Iteration $N-1$. Difference = Value(Iteration N) - Value(Iteration N-1)

--- Page 123 ---
When this difference becomes almost zero (or very small), it means the values have "converged," and you stop the process. 3. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) In Scikit-learn, MICE is implemented as the IterativeImputer . Since it is still technically an experimental feature, you must enable it first. Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np from sklearn.experimental import enable_iterative_imputer from sklearn.impute import IterativeImputer from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression # Sample Data df = pd.DataFrame({ 'Age' : [ 25 , 27 , 29 , 31 , np.nan], 'Experience' : [np.nan, 3 , 5 , 7 , 9 ], 'Salary' : [ 50 , np.nan, 110 , 140 , 170 ] }) # Initialize the Iterative Imputer # estimator: The model used to predict values (LinearRegression is standard) # max_iter: How many cycles to run # tol: The convergence threshold imputer = IterativeImputer(estimator=LinearRegression(), max_iter= 10 , random_state= 0 )

--- Page 124 ---
# Fit and Transform imputed_data = imputer .fit_transform(df) # Convert back to DataFrame df_imputed = pd.DataFrame(imputed_data, columns=df.columns) print(df_imputed) 4. Pros and Cons Advantages: ● High Accuracy: It captures the complex relationships between multiple variables. ● Robust: Works well for datasets where multiple columns have missing values simultaneously . ● Flexibility: You can use any regression model as an estimator (Decision Trees, Bayesian Ridge, etc.). Disadvantages: ● Computationally Expensive: You are essentially training multiple machine learning models for every single iteration. ● Memory Intensive: Like KNN, you often need the training distribution or the model weights during deployment. ● Slow: For very large datasets with many missing values, this can take a long time to converge. 5. Summary Table Feature Univariate (Mean) KNN Imputer MICE (Iterative)

--- Page 125 ---
Philosophy Simple Average Similarity of rows Predictive modeling Complexity Very Low Medium High Handle Outliers Poor Medium High Large Data Speed Fast Slow Slowest Key Takeaway: Use MICE when you believe your features are highly correlated and you want the most statistically sound imputation possible, even if it takes more time to compute. Outliers in Machine Learning: Identification and Treatment An outlier is a data point that is significantly different from the remaining data points in a dataset. In simple words, it’s like a student in a class who scores 100% when everyone else scores between 40% and 50%. 1. Why are Outliers a Problem? Outliers can severely distort the results of your machine learning models. Mathematical Impact on Linear Regression: In Linear Regression, the model tries to minimize the Sum of Squared Errors. If you have two points that are very far away (outliers), the model will "tilt" its best-fit line toward those points to reduce the large squared error they create.

--- Page 126 ---
● Result: The line no longer represents the majority of your data correctly . Which Algorithms are Sensitive to Outliers? Generally , any algorithm that calculates weights or uses distance is sensitive: ● Linear Regression (Best-fit line gets tilted) ● Logistic Regression ● SVM (Support Vector Machines) ● K-Means Clustering (The centroid gets pulled away) ● Neural Networks (Weights can explode) Which Algorithms are Robust (Not Sensitive)? Tree-based algorithms are generally immune because they split data based on conditions (e.g., Is Age > 30?) rather than calculating global weights: ● Decision Trees ● Random Forest ● XGBoost / Gradient Boosting 2. When to Keep vs. Remove Outliers? Not all outliers are "bad" data. ● Remove: If the outlier is an entry error (e.g., someone entering age as 300). ● Keep: If the outlier represents a real but rare event (e.g., detecting credit card fraud or identifying a rare disease). These outliers are actually the signal you are looking for. 3. How to Detect Outliers? There are three primary mathematical ways to identify outliers: A. Z-Score Method (For Normal Distributions) If your data follows a bell curve, 99.7% of data points fall within 3 standard deviations of the mean. ● Outlier Condition: ○ Value > Mean + 3 * Standard_Deviation ○ Value < Mean - 3 * Standard_Deviation

--- Page 127 ---
B. IQR Method (For Skewed Distributions) If your data is not normal (e.g., Income data), use the Interquartile Range (IQR). This is the method used by Box Plots. ● Interquartile Range (IQR) = Q3 - Q1 ● Outlier Condition: ○ Lower_Limit = Q1 - 1.5 * IQR ○ Upper_Limit = Q3 + 1.5 * IQR ○ Any value outside [Lower_Limit, Upper_Limit] is an outlier . C. Percentile Method You define a threshold (e.g., the top 1% and bottom 1%). ● Outlier Condition: ○ Value > 99th Percentile ○ Value < 1st Percentile 4. How to Treat Outliers? Once detected, you have two main strategies: 1. Trimming (Removal) You simply delete the rows that contain outliers. ● Pros: Extremely fast and easy . ● Cons: You might lose valuable data if you have too many outliers. 2. Capping (Winsorization) Instead of deleting the data, you "cap" the values at the limit. ● Logic: If your Upper Limit is 90 and you have a value of 150, you change 150 to 90. ● Pros: You don't lose any rows of data. 5. Python Implementation Example Detection using IQR:

--- Page 128 ---
Python import pandas as pd import seaborn as sns df = pd.read_csv( 'data.csv' ) # 1. Calculate Q1 and Q3 Q1 = df[ 'Salary' ].quantile( 0.25 ) Q3 = df[ 'Salary' ].quantile( 0.75 ) IQR = Q3 - Q1 # 2. Define Limits upper_limit = Q3 + 1.5 * IQR lower_limit = Q1 - 1.5 * IQR # 3. Trimming df_trimmed = df[(df[ 'Salary' ] < upper_limit) & (df[ 'Salary' ] > lower_limit)] # 4. Capping (Winsorization) import numpy as np df[ 'Salary' ] = np.where(df[ 'Salary' ] > upper_limit, upper_limit, np.where(df[ 'Salary' ] < lower_limit, lower_limit, df[ 'Salary' ])) Summary Table

--- Page 129 ---
Method Best Distribution Formula for Limits Z-Score Normal Mean +/- 3 * StdDev IQR Skewed Q1 - 1.5 IQR and Q3 + 1.5 IQR Percentile Any Custom (e.g., 1% and 99%) Note: Always visualize your data using a Box Plot or Histogram before deciding whether a point is truly an outlier or just a rare natural occurrence. Outlier Detection and Removal: Z-Score Method The Z-Score Method is one of the most widely used techniques for identifying and treating outliers in a dataset. 1. The Core Assumption: Normality For the Z-score method to work correctly , the feature (column) you are analyzing must be Normally Distributed (or close to it). This means when you plot the data, it should look like a symmetric "Bell Curve." Why only Normal Distribution? In a normal distribution, data follows a predictable pattern based on the mean and standard deviation: ● 68% of data falls within 1 standard deviation of the mean. ● 95% of data falls within 2 standard deviations of the mean.

--- Page 130 ---
● 99.7% of data falls within 3 standard deviations of the mean. Anything beyond 3 standard deviations is extremely rare (only 0.3% of the data) and is mathematically considered an outlier . 2. Mathematical Derivation To find these outliers, we convert every data point into a "Z-score." The Z-Score Formula: Z = (X - Mean) / Standard_Deviation Where: ● X is the individual data point. ● Mean (often written as Mu) is the average of the column. ● Standard_Deviation (Sigma) is the spread of the data. Interpreting the Z-score: ● A Z-score of 1 means the point is 1 standard deviation above the mean. ● A Z-score of -2 means the point is 2 standard deviations below the mean. ● If |Z| > 3, the point is an outlier . Defining the Boundaries: Instead of calculating the Z-score for every point, we can just pre-calculate the "safe" limits: ● Upper Limit = Mean + 3 * Standard_Deviation ● Lower Limit = Mean - 3 * Standard_Deviation Any value higher than the Upper Limit or lower than the Lower Limit is an outlier . 3. How to Treat Detected Outliers Once you identify the rows with outliers, you have two options: A. Trimming (Deletion) You simply drop those rows from your dataset.

--- Page 131 ---
● Use this if you have a massive dataset and the number of outliers is small (e.g., losing 5 rows out of 1,000). B. Capping (Winsorization) Instead of deleting the row, you "squash" the outlier value to the boundary . ● If a value is 9.5 and your Upper Limit is 8.8, you change the 9.5 to 8.8. ● If a value is 4.2 and your Lower Limit is 5.1, you change the 4.2 to 5.1. ● This is safer because you keep all your observations (rows). 4. Implementation Code (Pandas) Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np import seaborn as sns import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # Load sample data df = pd.read_csv( 'placement_data.csv' ) # 1. Visualize to check for Normality sns.distplot(df[ 'cgpa' ]) plt.show() # 2. Calculate Mean and Standard Deviation mean = df[ 'cgpa' ].mean() std = df[ 'cgpa' ].std()

--- Page 132 ---
# 3. Define the Limits (3-sigma rule) upper_limit = mean + 3 * std lower_limit = mean - 3 * std print( "Upper Limit:" , upper_limit) print( "Lower Limit:" , lower_limit) # --- APPROACH 1: TRIMMING --- # Filter out rows outside the limits new_df_trimmed = df[(df[ 'cgpa' ] <= upper_limit) & (df[ 'cgpa' ] >= lower_limit)] # --- APPROACH 2: CAPPING --- # Replace values > upper with upper_limit and < lower with lower_limit df[ 'cgpa' ] = np.where( df[ 'cgpa' ] > upper_limit, upper_limit, np.where( df[ 'cgpa' ] < lower_limit, lower_limit, df[ 'cgpa' ] ) ) 5. Summary Checklist for Beginners

--- Page 133 ---
1. Plot your data: Use a histogram or distribution plot. Does it look like a bell? 2. Calculate limits: Find the Mean and Standard Deviation. Multiply the Std Dev by 3 and add/subtract it from the Mean. 3. Find Outliers: Check if any points are outside these bounds. 4. Decide treatment: * If you want to keep the dataset size same, use Capping. ○ If you want a "cleaner" dataset and don't mind losing a few rows, use Trimming. Key Term: The Empirical Rule or the 68-95-99.7 Rule is the basis for using 3 standard deviations as the cutof f for outliers. Outlier Detection and Removal: IQR Method The Interquartile Range (IQR) Method is the go-to technique for identifying outliers in Skewed Distributions (data that is not bell-shaped). While the Z-score method is for normal distributions, the IQR method is more robust and is the mathematical basis for Box Plots. 1. Key Concepts: Percentiles and Quartiles To understand IQR, you first need to understand quartiles: ● Q1 (25th Percentile / First Quartile): 25% of the data points are smaller than this value. ● Q2 (50th Percentile / Median): 50% of the data points are smaller than this value. ● Q3 (75th Percentile / Third Quartile): 75% of the data points are smaller than this value. What is IQR?

--- Page 134 ---
The Interquartile Range (IQR) is the middle 50% of your data. It is the distance between the 75th and 25th percentiles. IQR = Q3 - Q1 2. Mathematical Derivation of Outlier Limits The IQR method uses a "Proximity Rule" to define boundaries. Any data point that falls too far away from the middle box (the quartiles) is labeled an outlier . The Standard Proximity Rule: ● Lower Limit = Q1 - 1.5 * IQR ● Upper Limit = Q3 + 1.5 * IQR Why 1.5? The multiplier 1.5 was proposed by mathematician John Tukey . It is a standard "gate" that works well for most datasets. ● If you want to be more strict (only find extreme outliers), you can use 3.0 instead of 1.5. 3. Step-by-Step Implementation 1. Calculate Q1 and Q3: Use the quantile() function. 2. Calculate IQR: Q3 - Q1 . 3. Determine Boundaries: Calculate the Upper and Lower limits using the formula above. 4. Identify Outliers: Find all values where X > Upper_Limit or X < Lower_Limit . 5. Treat Outliers: Choose between Trimming (deleting the rows) or Capping (replacing the value with the limit). 4. Implementation Code (Pandas & NumPy) Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np

--- Page 135 ---
import seaborn as sns import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # Load sample data df = pd.read_csv( 'placement_data.csv' ) # 1. Visualize to see the skewness and outliers sns.boxplot(df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ]) plt.show() # 2. Calculate Q1, Q3, and IQR q1 = df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ].quantile( 0.25 ) q3 = df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ].quantile( 0.75 ) iqr = q3 - q1 # 3. Calculate Boundaries upper_limit = q3 + 1.5 * iqr lower_limit = q1 - 1.5 * iqr print( "Upper Limit:" , upper_limit) print( "Lower Limit:" , lower_limit) # 4. Find Outliers (Optional: just to see them) outliers = df[(df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] > upper_limit) | (df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] < lower_limit)]

--- Page 136 ---
# --- OPTION A: TRIMMING (Deleting) --- new_df_trimmed = df[(df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] <= upper_limit) & (df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] >= lower_limit)] # --- OPTION B: CAPPING (Winsorization) --- # We use np.where(condition, value_if_true, value_if_false) df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] = np.where( df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] > upper_limit, upper_limit, np.where( df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] < lower_limit, lower_limit, df[ 'placement_exam_marks' ] ) ) 5. Summary Table: Z-Score vs. IQR Feature Z-Score Method IQR Method Best Data Distribution Normal (Bell Curve) Skewed (Not Bell Curve) Outlier Threshold 3 Standard Deviations 1.5 * IQR

--- Page 137 ---
Robustness Sensitive to extreme outliers Very robust Visualization Tool Histogram / KDE Box Plot Beginners' Logic Tip: ● If your data is symmetric and nice, go with Z-Score. ● If your data has a "long tail" on one side (like income or exam marks), go with IQR. ● Always use a Box Plot to verify . If you see dots beyond the "whiskers," those are the outliers your code is trying to find. Outlier Detection and Removal: Percentile Method The Percentile Method is a straightforward and intuitive technique for handling outliers. Unlike the Z-score (which requires a Normal distribution) or the IQR method (which targets the middle 50%), the Percentile method allows you to set custom thresholds based on the percentage of data you want to exclude from the extremes. 1. What are Percentiles? A percentile is a measure that indicates the value below which a given percentage of observations in a group of observations falls. ● 99th Percentile: 99% of the data points are smaller than this value. Only 1% of the data is larger . ● 1st Percentile: Only 1% of the data is smaller than this value. 99% of the data is larger . ● 50th Percentile: This is the Median. 50% of the data is above it, and 50% is below .

--- Page 138 ---
Mathematical Definition: If the 99th percentile of a height dataset is 190 cm, it means that if you pick a person at random, there is a 99% chance they are shorter than 190 cm. 2. The Logic of Outlier Detection In this method, you manually decide a "cutoff" for what you consider an outlier at both ends of the distribution. Standard Thresholds: Most data scientists use the 1% and 99% rule: ● Any value above the 99th percentile is an outlier . ● Any value below the 1st percentile is an outlier . You can adjust this based on your data: ● Strict: 0.5th and 99.5th percentiles. ● Loose: 5th and 95th percentiles. 3. Treatment Techniques A. Trimming (Removal) You identify the rows falling outside your percentile boundaries and delete them. ● Example: In a dataset of 1000 rows, a 1% and 99% trim will remove exactly 20 rows (10 from the bottom, 10 from the top). B. Winsorization (Capping) Named after Charles Winsor , this technique involves "capping" the outliers instead of deleting them. ● The Rule: Any value greater than the 99th percentile is replaced by the value of the 99th percentile. Any value smaller than the 1st percentile is replaced by the value of the 1st percentile. ● Why use it? It prevents data loss while still removing the extreme influence of outliers.

--- Page 139 ---
4. Implementation Code (Pandas & NumPy) Python import pandas as pd import numpy as np import seaborn as sns # Load dataset (e.g., Height and Weight data) df = pd.read_csv( 'weight-height.csv' ) # 1. Define Upper and Lower Limits # Using 1% (0.01) and 99% (0.99) thresholds upper_limit = df[ 'Height' ].quantile( 0.99 ) lower_limit = df[ 'Height' ].quantile( 0.01 ) print( "Upper Limit:" , upper_limit) print( "Lower Limit:" , lower_limit) # --- APPROACH 1: TRIMMING --- # Keep only data within the limits df_trimmed = df[(df[ 'Height' ] <= upper_limit) & (df[ 'Height' ] >= lower_limit)] # --- APPROACH 2: WINSORIZA TION (CAPPING) --- # np.where(condition, value_if_true, value_if_false) df[ 'Height_capped' ] = np.where( df[ 'Height' ] >= upper_limit,

--- Page 140 ---
upper_limit, np.where( df[ 'Height' ] <= lower_limit, lower_limit, df[ 'Height' ] ) ) # Comparison of stats print( "Original Max:" , df[ 'Height' ]. max ()) print( "Capped Max:" , df[ 'Height_capped' ]. max ()) 5. Visual Validation To ensure the process worked, you should use a Box Plot. ● Before treatment, you will see dots (outliers) beyond the whiskers. ● After Percentile-based trimming or capping, the box plot whiskers will extend to the new limits, and the extreme dots will disappear . 6. Summary Comparison Method Best for... Limit Formula Z-Score Normal Distributions Mean +/- 3 * StdDev IQR Skewed Distributions Q1 - 1.5 IQR / Q3 + 1.5 IQR

--- Page 141 ---
Percentile Any Distribution / Custom Control .quantile(0.01) / .quantile(0.99) Pro-Tip: The Percentile method is "distribution-agnostic," meaning it doesn't care if your data is a bell curve or skewed. It simply cuts off a specific percentage of the data. This makes it very flexible for real-world datasets that don't follow perfect mathematical shapes. Feature Construction and Feature Splitting In previous notes, we focused on Feature Transformation (handling missing data, outliers, and scaling). Now, we move to a more creative part of Feature Engineering: Feature Construction and Feature Splitting. Unlike transformation, which follows set mathematical rules, construction and splitting are manual processes driven by your intuition and domain knowledge. 1. Feature Construction Feature Construction is the process of manually creating new features from the existing ones. The goal is to highlight a relationship that the machine learning model might struggle to "see" on its own. The Logic / Mathematical Explanation: Instead of giving the model two separate pieces of information, we combine them into a single, more powerful signal. Example (Titanic Dataset): We have two columns: 1. SibSp: Number of Siblings and Spouses traveling with the passenger . 2. Parch: Number of Parents and Children traveling with the passenger .

--- Page 142 ---
While these are useful, what really matters for survival might be the Total Family Size. Mathematical Step: Family_Size = SibSp + Parch + 1 (The "+ 1" represents the passenger themselves) We can then take it a step further and "bin" this into categories: ● If Family_Size = 1 -> Alone ● If Family_Size > 1 and Family_Size <= 4 -> Small Family ● If Family_Size > 4 -> Large Family By doing this, you are simplifying the data for the model, which can lead to a direct increase in accuracy . Code Implementation (Pandas): Python import pandas as pd # Load sample data df = pd.read_csv( 'titanic.csv' ) # 1. Construct 'Family_Size' df[ 'Family_Size' ] = df[ 'SibSp' ] + df[ 'Parch' ] + 1 # 2. Construct 'Family_T ype' based on Family_Size def transform_family (size): if size == 1 : return 'Alone' elif size > 1 and size <= 4 :

--- Page 143 ---
return 'Small' else : return 'Large' df[ 'Family_T ype' ] = df[ 'Family_Size' ].apply(transform_family) # 3. Drop the old columns as the new constructed one is more useful df.drop(columns=[ 'SibSp' , 'Parch' , 'Family_Size' ], inplace= True ) 2. Feature Splitting Feature Splitting is the opposite of construction. It involves breaking down a complex, "dirty" feature into multiple simpler features. This is common when one column contains multiple pieces of information packed into a single string. The Logic: Often, a single cell contains data that is not "atomic" (meaning it has multiple parts). By splitting it, we allow the model to treat each part as a separate feature. Example (Titanic Names): A passenger's name looks like this: "Braund, Mr. Owen Harris" or "Heikkinen, Miss. Laina". Inside this string, there is a Title (Mr, Miss, Mrs, Master , etc.). Why split it? In the 1910s, titles reflected social status and priority for lifeboats. "Miss" and "Mrs" (women) had a higher survival rate than "Mr". Extracting this title creates a very strong predictor for the model. Code Implementation (Pandas): Python

--- Page 144 ---
# Extracting Titles from the 'Name' column # The format is: Surname, Title. Firstname # Step 1: Split by comma and take the second part # Step 2: Split the result by dot and take the first part # Step 3: Remove extra spaces df[ 'Title' ] = df[ 'Name' ]. str .split( ', ' , expand= True )[ 1 ]. str .split( '.' , expand= True )[ 0 ] # Now you have a clean Title column! print(df[ 'Title' ].value_counts()) # You can even use this to create a boolean 'Is_Married' feature df[ 'Is_Married' ] = 0 df.loc[df[ 'Title' ] == 'Mrs' , 'Is_Married' ] = 1 3. Summary: Why do this? Technique Goal Key Driver Feature Construction Combine simple features into a complex signal. Domain Knowledge & Intuition Feature Splitting Break a complex feature into simple parts. Data Cleaning & Pattern Extraction When to use these?

--- Page 145 ---
● Construction: Use when you believe the "interaction" between two variables is more important than the variables themselves (e.g., combining Height and Weight to get BMI). ● Splitting: Use when a feature contains text or timestamps that have hidden patterns (e.g., splitting a Date into Day, Month, and Year). Note: Feature construction is highly experimental. You should always test your model's accuracy before and after construction to see if the new feature actually helps or just adds noise. Curse of Dimensionality The Curse of Dimensionality is a critical concept in machine learning that explains why adding more features (dimensions) to a dataset doesn't always lead to a better model. In fact, after a certain point, it can significantly degrade the performance of your machine learning algorithms. 1. What is the Curse of Dimensionality? As you increase the number of features (dimensions) in your data, the "volume" of the space grows so fast that the data points you have become very sparse. This sparseness is a problem for any method that relies on statistical significance or distance calculations.
