The Hughes Phenomenon: As the number of features increases, the accuracy of a classifier also increases until the optimal number of features is reached. Adding more features beyond this point results in a decrease in accuracy . ● Optimality: There is a "sweet spot" for the number of features. ● Model Complexity: More features lead to higher complexity , making the model prone to overfitting. It starts learning noise instead of actual patterns.

--- Page 146 ---
2. Why is High Dimensionality a Problem? A. Data Sparsity Imagine you are looking for a lost wallet: 1. 1D (A Line): You only have to search along a road. Easy . 2. 2D (A Square): You have to search an entire field. Harder . 3. 3D (A Cube): You have to search a multi-story building. Much harder . In higher dimensions (e.g., 100D), the "space" is so vast that your data points are almost never near each other . B. Distance Metric Breakdown Many algorithms (like KNN, K-Means, SVM) rely on measuring the distance between points (Euclidean distance). ● In high dimensions, the distance between the nearest point and the farthest point becomes almost the same. ● If all points are equally "far," the concept of a "neighbor" becomes meaningless. The model can no longer distinguish between similar and dissimilar points. C. Computational Cost ● Memory: Storing 1000 features per row takes much more space than storing 10. ● Time: Training a model on 1000 features is exponentially slower . 3. Mathematical Explanation (Without LaTeX) The Volume Problem: Think of a unit hypercube (a cube where every side is 1). ● In 1D, the "volume" (length) is 1. ● In 2D, the volume is 1 * 1 = 1. ● In ND, the volume is 1 to the power of N. Now, imagine we want to capture 10% of the data. ● In 1D, we need 10% of the length (0.1).

--- Page 147 ---
● In 2D, we need a square with sides of sqrt(0.1) = 0.316 (which is 31% of the total width). ● In 10D, we need a side length of (0.1) to the power of (1/10) = 0.80. We need to cover 80% of each dimension just to capture 10% of the data! Conclusion: To maintain the same data density as you add dimensions, you need an exponential increase in the number of data points. Usually , we don't have that much data, leading to the "curse." 4. How to Fix it: Dimensionality Reduction To combat this curse, we use Dimensionality Reduction techniques. There are two main approaches: A. Feature Selection You pick a subset of the original features and discard the rest. ● Forward Selection: Start with 0 features and add the best one at each step. ● Backward Elimination: Start with all features and remove the least useful one at each step. B. Feature Extraction You transform the high-dimensional data into a lower-dimensional space. The new features (called "components") are combinations of the old ones. ● PCA (Principal Component Analysis): The most popular linear technique. ● LDA (Linear Discriminant Analysis): Focuses on maximizing class separability . ● t-SNE / UMAP: Non-linear techniques often used for visualization. 5. Summary Table Feature Count Model Performance Computational Cost Risk

--- Page 148 ---
Low Underfitting (too simple) Low Missing patterns Optimal Best Accuracy Medium None High (Curse) Overfitting (too complex) High Learning noise Key Takeaway: More data (rows) is usually good, but more features (columns) can be dangerous. Always strive for the simplest model that explains the data (Occam's Razor). Principal Component Analysis (PCA): Geometric Intuition Principal Component Analysis (PCA) is one of the most popular and reliable Unsupervised Machine Learning techniques used for Feature Extraction and Dimensionality Reduction. It transforms high-dimensional data into a lower-dimensional form while preserving as much of the data's "essence" or variance as possible. 1. Why Use PCA? There are two primary reasons to use PCA in a machine learning pipeline: 1. Faster Execution: By reducing the number of features (e.g., from 100 columns to 10), the training and prediction speed of algorithms increase significantly with minimal loss in accuracy .

--- Page 149 ---
2. Visualization: Humans can only visualize data in 2D or 3D. If your dataset has 50 features, PCA can squash it into 2D or 3D components so you can plot it and see clusters or patterns. 2. Geometric Intuition: The Photographer Analogy Imagine a 3D soccer match. A photographer wants to capture the action for a newspaper , which is 2D. ● The soccer match exists in 3D. ● The photograph is 2D. ● The photographer moves around the field to find the angle that captures the most players and the most action without them overlapping too much. PCA works exactly like this. It searches for the "best angle" (Principal Component) in high-dimensional space to project the data onto a lower-dimensional plane such that the spread (variance) of the data points is maximized. 3. Mathematical Derivation: Variance and Spread In PCA, the term Variance is synonymous with information. The more spread out the data points are on an axis, the more information that axis carries. Logic of Feature Selection vs. Extraction: ● Feature Selection: You have "Rooms" and "Grocery Shops." You decide "Grocery Shops" isn't important and delete the whole column. ● Feature Extraction (PCA): You have "Rooms" and "Washrooms." Both are important. PCA combines them into a single new feature called "Size," which captures the essence of both. The Variance Rule: If you project data points onto an axis: ● If the points are all squashed together , the variance is low (information is lost). ● If the points are widely spread out, the variance is high (information is preserved).

--- Page 150 ---
PCA's Mathematical Goal: Find a new axis (Principal Component) such that the variance of the projected points on this axis is at its maximum. 4. How PCA Works (Step-by-Step) 1. Standardization: Scale the data so all features have a mean of 0 and a standard deviation of 1. 2. Covariance Matrix: Calculate how variables change together . 3. Eigenvectors and Eigenvalues: * Eigenvectors: The directions of the new axes (Principal Components). ○ Eigenvalues: The "strength" or amount of variance captured by each axis. 4. Principal Components: The axis with the highest eigenvalue is PC1, the second highest is PC2, and so on. 5. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) This example shows how to use PCA for visualization by reducing a high-dimensional dataset (like MNIST or Iris) into 2D. Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler from sklearn.decomposition import PCA import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # Load sample data (Example: 4 features) df = pd.read_csv( 'iris.csv' ) X = df.drop( 'target' , axis= 1 ) # 1. Standardization is MANDA TORY for PCA scaler = StandardScaler()

--- Page 151 ---
X_scaled = scaler .fit_transform(X) # 2. Initialize PCA # n_components=2 means we want to reduce the data to 2 dimensions pca = PCA(n_components= 2 ) # 3. Fit and Transform X_pca = pca.fit_transform(X_scaled) # 4. View the result # The new 'Principal Components' don't have original names pca_df = pd.DataFrame(X_pca, columns=[ 'PC1' , 'PC2' ]) # 5. Check how much information was preserved print( "Explained Variance Ratio:" , pca.explained_variance_ratio_) # Example output: [0.72, 0.23] -> PC1 keeps 72% info, PC2 keeps 23% info. # Total 95% of original data essence is kept in just 2 columns! # 6. Plotting the results plt.scatter(pca_df[ 'PC1' ], pca_df[ 'PC2' ]) plt.xlabel( 'Principal Component 1' ) plt.ylabel( 'Principal Component 2' ) plt.title( '2D Projection of High-Dimensional Data' ) plt.show()

--- Page 152 ---
6. Summary: Key Takeaways for Beginners ● PCA is Unsupervised: It doesn't look at the target (Y) variable; it only looks at the relationship between features (X). ● Information = Variance: PCA tries to find the direction where data is most "stretched out." ● New Features are Different: The columns you get after PCA (PC1, PC2) are combinations of original columns. You lose the original meaning (e.g., you can't tell which part is "Age" and which is "Salary"). ● Scaling is Essential: If one feature is in the range of 0-1 and another is 0-1000, PCA will be biased toward the larger numbers. Always use StandardScaler first. Note: PCA helps solve the "Curse of Dimensionality" by keeping the most important patterns while discarding the noise or redundant information. Principal Component Analysis (PCA): Mathematical Derivation and Steps PCA is a dimensionality reduction technique that transforms high-dimensional data into a lower-dimensional form. The core mathematical objective is to find new axes (Principal Components) that maximize the variance of the projected data points. 1. Mathematical Problem Formulation The goal of PCA is to find a unit vector u (direction) such that the variance of the data points when projected onto u is maximized. Projections and Vectors: Every data point can be treated as a vector x . When we project this vector onto a unit vector u , the length of the projection is calculated as:

--- Page 153 ---
● Projection = u^T * x (The dot product of the unit vector and the data point). Variance Maximization: If we have a set of data points, we want to find the direction u where the variance of these projected points is at its maximum. The variance for a set of projections is: ● Variance = (1/n) * Sum of (u^T * x_i - u^T * x_mean)^2 Mathematically , solving this optimization problem leads us to a specific property of the Covariance Matrix . 2. Step-by-Step Mathematical Solution To perform PCA manually or algorithmically , follow these steps: Step 1: Mean Centering (Standardization) Shift the data so that the mean of each feature is 0. ● Calculation: Subtract the mean of each column from every data point in that column. ● Result: The dataset is now centered around the origin (0,0). Step 2: Compute the Covariance Matrix Calculate how each feature varies with every other feature. ● For a 2D dataset with features X and Y, the Covariance Matrix is a 2x2 matrix: ○ Top-Left: Variance of X ○ Bottom-Right: Variance of Y ○ Top-Right & Bottom-Left: Covariance between X and Y. ● Why? This matrix captures the spread and the orientation of the data. Step 3: Eigenvalue and Eigenvector Decomposition Decompose the Covariance Matrix to find its Eigenvalues and Eigenvectors. ● Eigenvectors: These are the directions of the new axes (Principal Components). ● Eigenvalues: These represent the "magnitude" or the amount of variance captured by each Eigenvector . Step 4: Selecting Principal Components Sort the Eigenvalues in descending order .

--- Page 154 ---
● The Eigenvector corresponding to the largest Eigenvalue is PC1 (it captures the most information). ● The Eigenvector with the second largest Eigenvalue is PC2 , and so on. Step 5: Transforming the Data (Projection) Project the original data onto the selected Principal Components. ● Formula: New_Data = Original_Data * Eigenvector_Matrix. ● This step effectively "rotates" your data into the new coordinate system. 3. Implementation Code (Step-by-Step Python) This code demonstrates the manual steps of PCA using NumPy to show exactly how the math works. Python import numpy as np import pandas as pd from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler # 1. Create a dummy 3D dataset data = { 'F1' : [ 2.5 , 0.5 , 2.2 , 1.9 , 3.1 ], 'F2' : [ 2.4 , 0.7 , 2.9 , 2.2 , 3.0 ], 'F3' : [ 0.1 , 0.5 , 0.2 , 0.3 , 0.4 ] } df = pd.DataFrame(data) # 2. Mean Centering (Standardization) scaler = StandardScaler()

--- Page 155 ---
df_scaled = scaler .fit_transform(df) # 3. Compute Covariance Matrix # We transpose df_scaled because np.cov expects features in rows cov_matrix = np.cov(df_scaled.T) # 4. Calculate Eigenvalues and Eigenvectors eigenvalues, eigenvectors = np.linalg.eig(cov_matrix) print( "Eigenvalues:" , eigenvalues) print( "Eigenvectors Matrix:\n" , eigenvectors) # 5. Sort Eigenvalues and select Top 2 for 2D projection # Indices of eigenvalues in descending order idx = eigenvalues.argsort()[::- 1 ] eigenvalues = eigenvalues[idx] eigenvectors = eigenvectors[:, idx] # Select top 2 eigenvectors (PC1 and PC2) pc_matrix = eigenvectors[:, : 2 ] # 6. Transform the data (Project onto PC1 and PC2) # Resulting shape will be (5 rows, 2 columns) final_data = np.dot(df_scaled, pc_matrix)

--- Page 156 ---
transformed_df = pd.DataFrame(final_data, columns=[ 'PC1' , 'PC2' ]) print( "\nTransformed 2D Data:\n" , transformed_df) 4. Summary Table of Key Concepts Concept What it represents in PCA Mean Centering Moving the cloud of data to the center (0,0). Covariance Matrix A summary of the spread and relationships in the data. Eigenvector The direction of a Principal Component (e.g., PC1). Eigenvalue The amount of variance (information) in that direction. Projection The actual process of squashing the data onto the new axis. Note: The number of Principal Components is always less than or equal to the number of original features. If you start with 10 features, you can have a maximum of 10 PCs.

--- Page 157 ---
Principal Component Analysis (PCA) on MNIST: Practical Implementation The MNIST dataset is a collection of 70,000 small images of handwritten digits (0-9). Each image is 28x28 pixels, resulting in 784 features (dimensions) per image. This high dimensionality makes it perfect for demonstrating PCA's power in visualization and speed optimization. 1. The Challenge: High Dimensionality ● Dimensions: 784 (each pixel is a feature). ● Problem: We cannot visualize a 784-dimensional space. Furthermore, running algorithms like KNN on this data is extremely slow because calculating distances in 784D is computationally heavy . ● Goal: Reduce 784 features to 2 or 3 for visualization, and to ~100-200 for model training while keeping most of the information. 2. Mathematical Logic: Explained Variance Ratio When you reduce 784 dimensions to 100, how do you know if you've kept the "important" stuff? We use the Explained Variance Ratio . The Formula: Every Principal Component (PC) has an associated Eigenvalue. The percentage of total information (variance) that a specific component captures is: Ratio_i = Eigenvalue_i / Sum_of_all_Eigenvalues Cumulative Variance: To find the optimal number of components, we sum these ratios: Cumulative_V ariance = Sum(Ratio_1 + Ratio_2 + ... + Ratio_k) Usually , we aim to keep 90% to 95% of the total variance. 3. Implementation Steps

--- Page 158 ---
Step 1: Data Preprocessing (Standardization) PCA is a variance-maximizing tool. If one pixel has values from 0-255 and another feature is scaled differently , PCA will be biased. Note: For image data, dividing by 255 is a form of scaling, but StandardScaler is the standard for PCA. Step 2: Applying PCA We use Scikit-learn's PCA class. We can either specify the number of components (e.g., n_components=2 ) or the percentage of variance we want to keep (e.g., n_components=0.95 ). Step 3: Visualization By reducing the data to 2 or 3 components, we can use a scatter plot. Points belonging to the same digit (e.g., all "0"s) should form clusters in this new reduced space. 4. Code Example (Scikit-Learn) Python import pandas as pd import matplotlib.pyplot as plt from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler from sklearn.decomposition import PCA from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier # 1. Load MNIST (Assumed available as a CSV or via fetch_openml) # X contains 784 columns, y contains labels (0-9) X, y = load_mnist_data() # 2. Standardization scaler = StandardScaler()

--- Page 159 ---
X_scaled = scaler .fit_transform(X) # 3. PCA for Visualization (2D) pca_2d = PCA(n_components= 2 ) X_pca_2d = pca_2d.fit_transform(X_scaled) # Plotting the 2D clusters plt.figure(figsize=( 8 , 6 )) plt.scatter(X_pca_2d[:, 0 ], X_pca_2d[:, 1 ], c=y, cmap= 'tab10' , alpha= 0.5 ) plt.colorbar() plt.title( 'MNIST 2D Projection using PCA' ) plt.show() # 4. Finding the Optimal Components (e.g., to keep 90% variance) pca_90 = PCA(n_components= 0.90 ) X_pca_90 = pca_90.fit_transform(X_scaled) print( "Original Features:" , X.shape[ 1 ]) # 784 print( "Reduced Features for 90% variance:" , X_pca_90.shape[ 1 ]) # Usually around 150-200 features 5. How to Choose the Number of Components? You can plot the Scree Plot (Cumulative Explained Variance vs. Number of Components). Scree Plot Interpretation:

--- Page 160 ---
● The X-axis: Number of Principal Components. ● The Y-axis: Cumulative Variance Explained. ● The Elbow: Look for the "elbow" in the curve where adding more components gives diminishing returns. For MNIST , you'll see that the first 100-200 components capture almost all the important patterns, while the remaining 500+ are mostly noise. 6. Summary Table Metric Without PCA With PCA (90% Variance) Number of Features 784 ~150-200 Training Speed Slow Fast Visualization Impossible Possible (in 2D/3D) Information Kept 100% 90% (Noise is removed) 7. When Does PCA Fail? 1. Circular/Symmetric Data: If the variance is equal in all directions (like a perfect circle), PCA won't know which axis to pick. 2. Non-Linear Patterns: PCA is a linear transformation. If your data is arranged in a complex spiral or "Swiss Roll" shape, PCA will fail to capture the structure. In those cases, techniques like t-SNE or UMAP are better . Key Takeaway: PCA is not just for making things smaller; it's a "noise filter." By keeping the components with the most variance, you often keep the actual "signal" of the digit and discard the random pixel noise.

--- Page 161 ---
YouTube URL: https://www .youtube.com/watch?v=tofVCUDrg4M Simple Linear Regression: Intuition and Mathematical Derivation Linear Regression is often the first machine learning algorithm taught because it is easy to understand, mathematically transparent, and serves as the foundation for more complex models. 1. What is Linear Regression? Linear Regression is a Supervised Machine Learning algorithm used to solve Regression problems (where the output is a continuous numerical value). It works by establishing a linear relationship between input variables (features) and an output variable (target). Types of Linear Regression: 1. Simple Linear Regression: One input feature and one output target (e.g., predicting Salary based on Experience). 2. Multiple Linear Regression: Multiple input features and one output target (e.g., predicting House Price based on Area, Bedrooms, and Age). 3. Polynomial Linear Regression: Used when the relationship between data points is non-linear (curved). 2. Geometric Intuition Imagine you have a scatter plot of data points representing CGP A on the x-axis and Package (Salary) on the y-axis. ● Most students with higher CGP A get higher packages, so the points trend upward. ● However , real-world data is "noisy" (soft linear). Not all points fall on a perfect line because of factors like interview performance or luck.

--- Page 162 ---
The Goal: Find the "Best Fit Line" that passes through these points such that it minimizes the total error. 3. Mathematical Derivation The "Best Fit Line" is a straight line, and every straight line is governed by a simple algebraic equation. The Equation of a Line: y = m * x + b Where: ● y : The predicted value (Target, e.g., Package). ● x : The input feature (e.g., CGP A). ● m : The Slope (also called Coef ficient or Weight). It represents how much y changes for every 1-unit change in x . ● b : The y-intercept (also called Intercept or Bias). It is the value of y when x is zero. Interpretation for Beginners: ● m (Weight): This tells the model how important the feature x is. If m is high, the package depends heavily on CGP A. If m is near zero, CGP A doesn't matter much. ● b (Bias/Offset): This is the baseline. For example, even if someone has 0 years of experience, a company might offer a minimum base salary . That base salary is the intercept b . 4. How the Algorithm "Learns" When you "train" a Linear Regression model, the computer is trying to find the best possible values for m and b. It starts with random values and adjusts them until the line is as close as possible to all data points. This closeness is measured by a Loss Function (usually Mean Squared Error), which we will derive in the next lesson. 5. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn)

--- Page 163 ---
This example shows how to use the LinearRegression class to predict a package based on CGP A. Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # 1. Load data df = pd.read_csv( 'placement.csv' ) # 2. Define X (Input) and y (Target) X = df[[ 'cgpa' ]] # Must be 2D array for sklearn y = df[ 'package' ] # 3. Split into training and testing sets X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 , random_state= 2 ) # 4. Initialize and train the model lr = LinearRegression() lr.fit(X_train, y_train) # 5. Make a prediction # Predict package for a student with 8.5 CGP A prediction = lr.predict([[ 8.5 ]])

--- Page 164 ---
print( f"Predicted Package for 8.5 CGP A: {prediction[ 0 ]} LPA" ) # 6. Access the learned parameters print( "Slope (m):" , lr.coef_) print( "Intercept (b):" , lr.intercept_) # 7. Visualize the Best Fit Line plt.scatter(df[ 'cgpa' ], df[ 'package' ]) plt.plot(X_train, lr.predict(X_train), color= 'red' ) # The best fit line plt.xlabel( 'CGP A' ) plt.ylabel( 'Package (LPA)' ) plt.show() 6. Summary Table: Key Components Component Algebraic Term ML Term Real-world meaning y Dependent Variable Target / Label What we are predicting (Package) x Independent Variable Feature The data we have (CGP A) m Slope Coef ficient / Weight Impact of CGP A on Package

--- Page 165 ---
b Y-intercept Intercept / Bias Base value of Package Key Takeaway: Simple Linear Regression is about finding the one line that minimizes the "mistakes" (distance from points to the line). Once you have m and b , you have a formula that can predict the future. YouTube URL: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=UZPfbG0jNec Simple Linear Regression: Mathematical Formulation and Coding This note covers the complete mathematical derivation of the "Best Fit Line" using Ordinary Least Squares (OLS) and how to implement this logic from scratch in Python without using Scikit-Learn. 1. The Goal: Finding the Best Fit Line In Simple Linear Regression, we want to find a line: y = m * x + b The objective is to find the values of m (slope) and b (intercept) that result in the minimum total error across all data points. 2. The Loss Function (Error Function) To find the "best" line, we first need to define "badness" or error. We use the Sum of Squared Errors (SSE) as our loss function. Why Squared Errors? 1. Positive Values: It ensures that errors below the line (negative) don't cancel out errors above the line (positive).

--- Page 166 ---
2. Differentiable: Unlike absolute values (Mean Absolute Error), squared terms are easy to differentiate, which is necessary for calculus. Mathematical Expression of Loss (E): E = Sum of (y_actual - y_predicted)^2 E = Sum of (y_i - (m * x_i + b))^2 We want to find m and b such that E is at its absolute minimum. 3. Mathematical Derivation using Calculus To find the minimum of a function, we take its partial derivative with respect to each variable and set it to zero. A. Deriving the Intercept (b) Take the partial derivative of E with respect to b: dE / db = Sum of 2 * (y_i - m * x_i - b) * (-1) = 0 After simplifying and dividing by the number of points (n): b = mean(y) - m * mean(x) This proves that the best fit line always passes through the mean point (mean_x, mean_y) of the dataset. B. Deriving the Slope (m) Take the partial derivative of E with respect to m, substitute the value of b found above, and solve for m: dE / dm = Sum of 2 * (y_i - m * x_i - b) * (-x_i) = 0 The final closed-form formula for m is: m = [Sum of (x_i - mean_x) * (y_i - mean_y)] / [Sum of (x_i - mean_x)^2] 4. Coding Simple Linear Regression from Scratch Now we can implement these formulas in a Python class. This class will behave exactly like Scikit-Learn's LinearRegression .

--- Page 167 ---
Python import numpy as np class MyLR: def __init__ (self): self.m = None self.b = None def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # Calculate means num = 0 # Numerator den = 0 # Denominator x_mean = X_train.mean() y_mean = y_train.mean() # Calculate slope (m) using the formula # m = sum((xi - x_mean) * (yi - y_mean)) / sum((xi - x_mean)^2) for i in range (X_train.shape[ 0 ]): num = num + ((X_train[i] - x_mean) * (y_train[i] - y_mean)) den = den + ((X_train[i] - x_mean)** 2 ) self.m = num / den

--- Page 168 ---
# Calculate intercept (b) # b = y_mean - m * x_mean self.b = y_mean - (self.m * x_mean) print( "Slope (m):" , self.m) print( "Intercept (b):" , self.b) def predict (self, X_test): # Return y = m*x + b return self.m * X_test + self.b # Usage Example: # lr = MyLR() # lr.fit(X_train, y_train) # prediction = lr.predict(8.5) 5. Ordinary Least Squares (OLS) vs. Gradient Descent The formulas derived above are known as the Closed-Form Solution or OLS . ● OLS (Ordinary Least Squares): Solves the problem using a direct formula. It is extremely fast for small datasets with few features. ● Gradient Descent: An iterative approach that "steps" toward the minimum. This is used for very large datasets where the OLS matrix calculations become too slow or memory-intensive. Note: Scikit-Learn's LinearRegression class uses OLS internally , while SGDRegressor uses Gradient Descent.

--- Page 169 ---
6. Summary for First-Time Readers 1. Objective: Draw a line through data points with minimal "mistakes." 2. The Mistake: The vertical distance between a point and the line. 3. The Math: Square all the mistakes, add them up, and use calculus to find the values of m and b that make this sum as small as possible. 4. The Formula: ○ Slope (m) is the covariance of X and Y divided by the variance of X. ○ Intercept (b) is the mean of Y minus the weighted mean of X. Key Takeaway: You don't need fancy libraries to build a linear regression model. The entire intelligence of the algorithm is contained in those two simple mathematical formulas. YouTube URL: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=dXHIDLPKdmA Regression Metrics: MAE, MSE, RMSE, R2 Score & Adjusted R2 Score When you build a regression model, you need to know how well it is performing. Regression metrics provide numerical values that help you evaluate the accuracy of your model and compare it with other models. 1. Mean Absolute Error (MAE) MAE is the simplest metric to understand. It calculates the average of the absolute differences between the actual values and the predicted values. Mathematical Logic: 1. Find the distance (error) between each actual point (y) and the predicted point (y_hat). 2. Take the absolute value of each error (ignore positive/negative signs).

--- Page 170 ---
3. Sum them up and divide by the total number of points (n). Formula: MAE = (1/n) * Sum of |y_i - y_hat_i| Characteristics: ● Unit: The result is in the same unit as the target variable (e.g., if you are predicting salary in LPA, MAE will be in LPA). ● Robustness: It is relatively robust to outliers because it doesn't "penalize" large errors as heavily as MSE does. ● Downside: The absolute value function is not differentiable at zero, which makes it difficult to use in some optimization algorithms like Gradient Descent. 2. Mean Squared Error (MSE) MSE is the most popular loss function for regression. It squares the errors before averaging them. Mathematical Logic: 1. Find the error (y - y_hat) for each point. 2. Square each error. 3. Sum the squares and divide by n. Formula: MSE = (1/n) * Sum of (y_i - y_hat_i)^2 Characteristics: ● Differentiable: The squared function is smooth and differentiable everywhere, making it ideal for mathematical optimization. ● Sensitivity to Outliers: Because you are squaring the error, a large error (outlier) becomes massive, pulling the average toward it. It heavily penalizes large mistakes. ● Unit: The unit is squared (e.g., LPA^2), making it harder to interpret intuitively . 3. Root Mean Squared Error (RMSE)

--- Page 171 ---
RMSE is simply the square root of MSE. It was created to bring the MSE back to the original unit of the target variable. Formula: RMSE = sqrt( (1/n) * Sum of (y_i - y_hat_i)^2 ) Characteristics: ● Unit: Same as the target variable (easy to interpret). ● Popularity: Widely used in research and industry competitions (like Kaggle). ● Constraint: Still sensitive to outliers, just like MSE. 4. R-Squared (R2 Score) While MAE/MSE/RMSE depend on the scale of your data, R2 Score is a relative metric that tells you what percentage of the data's variance is explained by your model. It is also called the Coefficient of Determination . Mathematical Logic: It compares your model's error to the error of a "naive" model (a model that just predicts the Mean of Y for every input). Formula: R2 = 1 - (SSR / SSM) ● SSR (Sum of Squared Residuals): Sum of (y_actual - y_predicted)^2 -> Error from your model. ● SSM (Sum of Squared Total): Sum of (y_actual - y_mean)^2 -> Error if you just used the average. Interpretation: ● R2 = 1: Your model is perfect (explained 100% variance). ● R2 = 0: Your model is as good as just guessing the average. ● R2 < 0: Your model is worse than just guessing the average (this happens if you choose a very bad model). 5. Adjusted R-Squared

--- Page 172 ---
R2 Score has a flaw: it always increases or stays the same when you add more features, even if those features are useless noise (like adding "Temperature" to predict "Placement Package"). Adjusted R2 penalizes you for adding unnecessary features. It only increases if the new feature significantly improves the model. Formula: Adjusted_R2 = 1 - [ ((1 - R2) * (n - 1)) / (n - k - 1) ] ● n: Number of rows. ● k: Number of independent features. 6. Code Implementation (Scikit-Learn) Python from sklearn.metrics import mean_absolute_error , mean_squared_error , r2_score import numpy as np # y_test: Actual values from your dataset # y_pred: Values predicted by your model # 1. MAE mae = mean_absolute_error(y_test, y_pred) print( "MAE:" , mae) # 2. MSE mse = mean_squared_error(y_test, y_pred) print( "MSE:" , mse)

--- Page 173 ---
# 3. RMSE rmse = np.sqrt(mse) print( "RMSE:" , rmse) # 4. R2 Score r2 = r2_score(y_test, y_pred) print( "R2 Score:" , r2) # 5. Adjusted R2 Score # Assuming X_test is your input feature matrix n = X_test.shape[ 0 ] # number of rows k = X_test.shape[ 1 ] # number of features adj_r2 = 1 - (( 1 - r2) * (n - 1 ) / (n - k - 1 )) print( "Adjusted R2:" , adj_r2) 7. Summary Checklist ● Use MAE if you want an easy-to-understand error in the same unit and your data has outliers. ● Use MSE/RMSE if you want to penalize large errors more heavily or if you are using Gradient Descent. ● Use R2 Score to get a general idea of how "good" your model is on a scale of 0 to 1. ● Always use Adjusted R2 if you are working with multiple features to ensure you aren't being misled by "irrelevant" data. Note: Low error (MAE/RMSE) and High R2 Score (close to 1) indicate a better performing model.

--- Page 174 ---
Multiple Linear Regression: Geometric Intuition & Explanation Multiple Linear Regression (MLR) is an extension of simple linear regression. While simple linear regression deals with a single input feature, MLR handles situations where the output depends on two or more input features. 1. Core Concept In Simple Linear Regression, we find a "Best Fit Line" in a 2D space. In Multiple Linear Regression: ● With 2 input features , we find a "Best Fit Plane" in a 3D space. ● With n input features , we find a "Best Fit Hyperplane" in an (n+1) dimensional space. The objective remains the same: find a mathematical surface that passes as closely as possible to all data points, minimizing the total error. 2. Mathematical Explanation The Equation of the Hyperplane The relationship is expressed as: y = beta_0 + (beta_1 * x_1) + (beta_2 * x_2) + ... + (beta_n * x_n) Where: ● y : The dependent variable (Target, e.g., Placement Package). ● x_1, x_2, ..., x_n : Independent variables (Features, e.g., CGP A, IQ, Gender). ● beta_0 : The Intercept (Bias). It is the value of y when all x values are zero. ● beta_1, beta_2, ..., beta_n : The Coefficients (Weights). They represent the impact of each specific feature on the target. Interpretation of Coefficients (Beta Values): ● beta_i tells us how much the target y is expected to change for every 1-unit increase in x_i , assuming all other features remain constant. ● A higher beta value suggests that the specific feature has a stronger influence on the prediction.

--- Page 175 ---
3. Geometric Intuition 2D vs 3D vs ND: 1. Simple Linear Regression (1 Feature): Data points are on a 2D plot (x, y). The model is a Line . 2. MLR with 2 Features: Data points float in a 3D space (x1, x2, y). Imagine a room where points are scattered like dust; the model is a Flat Sheet (Plane) cutting through them. 3. MLR with n Features: We cannot visualize this, but mathematically , it is a Hyperplane in a higher-dimensional space. 4. Why Use MLR? Most real-world problems are complex. For example, a student's placement package doesn't just depend on CGP A. It also depends on: ● IQ level ● Technical skills (test scores) ● Communication skills ● Previous experience Multiple Linear Regression allows the model to "weigh" each of these factors to provide a more accurate prediction than simple regression could. 5. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) Using Scikit-Learn, the code for MLR is identical to simple linear regression. The only difference is that the input X contains multiple columns. Python import pandas as pd from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression from sklearn.metrics import r2_score, mean_absolute_error

--- Page 176 ---
# 1. Create or Load Data # Features: CGP A, IQ | Target: Package (LPA) data = { 'cgpa' : [ 8.5 , 7.2 , 9.1 , 6.4 , 8.8 ], 'iq' : [ 110 , 100 , 120 , 90 , 115 ], 'package' : [ 4.5 , 3.2 , 5.8 , 2.1 , 4.9 ] } df = pd.DataFrame(data) # 2. Define Features (X) and Target (y) X = df[[ 'cgpa' , 'iq' ]] # Multiple columns y = df[ 'package' ] # 3. Train-T est Split X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 , random_state= 2 ) # 4. Train the Model lr = LinearRegression() lr.fit(X_train, y_train) # 5. Prediction y_pred = lr.predict(X_test) # 6. Coef ficients and Intercept print( "Intercept (Beta_0):" , lr.intercept_)

--- Page 177 ---
print( "Coef ficients (Beta_1, Beta_2):" , lr.coef_) # 7. Model Performance print( "R2 Score:" , r2_score(y_test, y_pred)) 6. Summary for First-Time Readers ● Goal: Predict one number (y) using multiple other numbers (x1, x2, etc.). ● The Model: Instead of a line, it's a plane or hyperplane. ● The Math: Each feature gets its own "importance score" (coef ficient). ● Visualization: Possible up to 2 features (3D). Beyond that, we rely purely on mathematical logic. Note: In the next lesson, we will dive into the mathematical formulation of how these beta values are calculated using Matrix Algebra (the OLS method for MLR). Multiple Linear Regression: Mathematical Formulation (Closed-Form Solution) Multiple Linear Regression (MLR) extends Simple Linear Regression by using multiple input features to predict a single target value. The "Closed-Form Solution" refers to solving the problem using a direct mathematical formula rather than an iterative process like Gradient Descent. 1. The Core Objective In MLR, we want to find a set of coefficients (Beta values) such that the predicted value is: y_hat = beta_0 + beta_1 * x_1 + beta_2 * x_2 + ... + beta_n * x_n

--- Page 178 ---
The objective is to find the vector BETA that minimizes the total error (Sum of Squared Errors). 2. Matrix Representation (The Foundation) To handle multiple features efficiently , we represent the data as matrices. A. The Features Matrix (X) Imagine you have n rows and k features. To account for the intercept (beta_0), we add a column of 1s at the beginning of our matrix. ● X has dimensions [n rows, k + 1 columns]. B. The Target Vector (y) ● y is a column vector containing the actual output values for all n rows. ● y has dimensions [n rows, 1 column]. C. The Coefficient Vector (Beta) ● Beta is the vector we need to find. ● Beta has dimensions [k + 1 rows, 1 column]. The Prediction Equation in Matrix Form: y_hat = X * Beta 3. Mathematical Derivation of the Formula Our goal is to minimize the Error Vector (e): e = y - y_hat e = y - (X * Beta) The Loss Function (L) is the sum of squared residuals, which in matrix form is: L = e^T * e L = (y - X * Beta)^T * (y - X * Beta) Expansion:

--- Page 179 ---
L = y^T * y - (X * Beta)^T * y - y^T * (X * Beta) + (X * Beta)^T * (X * Beta) Using matrix properties, the middle two terms are scalars and identical, so: L = y^T * y - 2 * Beta^T * X^T * y + Beta^T * X^T * X * Beta Minimization: To find the minimum, we take the derivative of L with respect to Beta and set it to zero: dL / dBeta = -2 * X^T * y + 2 * X^T * X * Beta = 0 The Final Formula (Normal Equation): X^T * X * Beta = X^T * y Beta = (X^T * X)^(-1) * X^T * y This is the Closed-Form Solution for Multiple Linear Regression. 4. Implementation Code from Scratch (NumPy) We can now implement this direct formula in a Python class. Python import numpy as np class MyMultipleLR: def __init__ (self): self.coef_ = None self.intercept_ = None self.beta = None def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # 1. Add a column of 1s to X_train for the intercept (beta_0)

--- Page 180 ---
# np.insert(matrix, index, value, axis) X_train = np.insert(X_train, 0 , 1 , axis= 1 ) # 2. Calculate Beta using the Normal Equation # Formula: Beta = inv(XT * X) * XT * y # Note: @ is the symbol for matrix multiplication in Python X_transpose = X_train.T self.beta = np.linalg.inv(X_transpose @ X_train) @ X_transpose @ y_train # 3. Extract Intercept and Coef ficients self.intercept_ = self.beta[ 0 ] self.coef_ = self.beta[ 1 :] print( "Intercept (beta_0):" , self.intercept_) print( "Coef ficients:" , self.coef_) def predict (self, X_test): # Add the column of 1s to test data X_test = np.insert(X_test, 0 , 1 , axis= 1 ) # y = X * Beta return X_test @ self.beta # Usage: # lr = MyMultipleLR() # lr.fit(X_train, y_train)

--- Page 181 ---
# y_pred = lr.predict(X_test) 5. Why do we need Gradient Descent if we have this formula? The formula (X^T * X)^(-1) * X^T * y involves a matrix inversion. The Complexity Problem: If you have n features, the matrix (X^T * X) has a size of n x n. The time complexity of inverting an n x n matrix is roughly O(n^3). ● If you have 10 features: 10^3 = 1,000 operations (very fast). ● If you have 10,000 features: 10,000^3 = 1,000,000,000,000 operations (extremely slow). Conclusion: ● Use the Closed-Form Solution (like Scikit-Learn's LinearRegression ) for small to medium datasets with few features. ● Use Gradient Descent (like Scikit-Learn's SGDRegressor ) for large-scale datasets with thousands of features. 6. Summary for First-Time Readers 1. Multiple Features: We stack our inputs into a matrix X and outputs into a vector y . 2. The Goal: Find the weight vector Beta that makes the predictions as close to y as possible. 3. The Math: By using calculus on the squared error matrix, we get a direct formula for Beta. 4. The Tool: NumPy's linear algebra functions allow us to calculate this formula in just a few lines of code. Key Takeaway: The "Learning" in Linear Regression is actually just solving one big matrix multiplication and inversion problem.

--- Page 182 ---
YouTube URL: https://www .youtube.com/watch?v=NU37mF5q8VE Multiple Linear Regression: Implementing Code From Scratch This guide explains how to build a Multiple Linear Regression model using the Normal Equation without relying on libraries like Scikit-Learn for the logic. We will derive the math, explain the steps, and write the Python code. 1. The Core Concept In Multiple Linear Regression, we try to predict a dependent variable (y) based on multiple independent variables (x1, x2, ..., xn). The relationship is represented by the equation: y = b0 + b1 x1 + b2 x2 + ... + bn*xn Where: ● y : The predicted value. ● b0 : The Intercept (the value of y when all x are 0). ● b1, b2, ... bn : The Coef ficients (weights) for each feature. To find the best values for these coefficients, we use a mathematical approach called the Normal Equation . 2. Mathematical Derivation (Plain Text) To find the optimal coefficients (Beta) in one step, we use matrix algebra. The Formula: Beta = (X_transpose * X)_inverse * X_transpose * Y Breaking down the components: 1. X (Feature Matrix) : This is a matrix containing all your input data. ○ The "Trick" : To calculate the intercept (b0) along with the other coefficients, we must add a column of 1s at the very beginning of our X matrix. This represents the constant term. 2. X_transpose : We flip the X matrix (rows become columns).

--- Page 183 ---
3. (X_transpose * X) : We perform matrix multiplication between the transpose and the original matrix. 4. _inverse : We find the inverse of the resulting matrix from step 3. 5. Y (Target Vector) : This is the column containing the actual values we want to predict. By multiplying these together , we get a vector Beta where the first value is the Intercept (b0) and the rest are the Coef ficients (b1, b2, etc.). 3. Implementation Logic 1. Initialization : Create a class and define placeholders for the intercept and coefficients. 2. The Fit Method : ○ Take the input data (X_train). ○ Insert a column of 1s at index 0. ○ Apply the Normal Equation formula using NumPy's linear algebra tools. ○ Store the first value as the intercept_ and the rest as coef_ . 3. The Predict Method : ○ Take new data (X_test). ○ Calculate: y_pred = (X_test dot_product coef_) + intercept_ . 4. Python Code From Scratch Python import numpy as np class MyMultipleLinearRegression: def __init__ (self): self.coef_ = None self.intercept_ = None

--- Page 184 ---
def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # 1. Add a column of 1s at the beginning of X_train for the intercept X_train = np.insert(X_train, 0 , 1 , axis= 1 ) # 2. Calculate the coefficients using the Normal Equation # Formula: Beta = (X.T * X)^-1 * X.T * y # Calculate (X_transpose * X) inverse x_transpose_x_inv = np.linalg.inv(np.dot(X_train.T , X_train)) # Multiply by X_transpose and then by y_train beta = np.dot(np.dot(x_transpose_x_inv , X_train.T), y_train) # 3. Extract the intercept and coefficients self.intercept_ = beta[ 0 ] self.coef_ = beta[ 1 :] print( "Model Trained Successfully ." ) print( f"Intercept: {self.intercept_} " ) print( f"Coef ficients: {self.coef_} " ) def predict (self, X_test): # Formula: y = (X_test . Coef fs) + Intercept y_pred = np.dot(X_test, self.coef_) + self.intercept_ return y_pred

--- Page 185 ---
5. How to Use the Code You can test this using the Diabetes dataset from Scikit-Learn to see if your "from scratch" model matches the professional version. Python from sklearn.datasets import load_diabetes from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.metrics import r2_score # Load data X, y = load_diabetes(return_X_y= True ) # Split data X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 , random_state= 2 ) # Create and train our custom model lr = MyMultipleLinearRegression() lr.fit(X_train, y_train) # Make predictions y_pred = lr.predict(X_test) # Check performance print( f"R2 Score: {r2_score(y_test, y_pred)} " )

--- Page 186 ---
6. Key Takeaways for Beginners ● No Iterations : Unlike Gradient Descent (which we often see in Deep Learning), the Normal Equation finds the answer in a single mathematical step. ● The Column of 1s : This is the most important coding step. Without it, your matrix multiplication won't account for the intercept, and your predictions will be significantly off. ● Matrix Multiplication vs. Element-wise : We use np.dot() for matrix multiplication. Using the standard * operator in NumPy would perform element-wise multiplication, which is incorrect for this derivation. ● Scale : This direct method works perfectly for small to medium datasets. However , because calculating a matrix inverse is computationally expensive, Gradient Descent is preferred for "Big Data." https://www .youtube.com/watch?v=VmZWXzxmNrE Gradient Descent From Scratch: Detailed Notes and Implementation Gradient Descent is a first-order iterative optimization algorithm used to find the local minimum of a differentiable function. In machine learning, it is the backbone of training models, from Linear Regression to Deep Neural Networks. 1. The Core Idea Imagine you are standing on a hill with a blindfold on. Your goal is to reach the lowest point of the valley . ● Step 1 : You feel the slope (gradient) of the ground under your feet. ● Step 2 : You take a small step in the opposite direction of the slope (downhill). ● Step 3 : You repeat this until the ground feels flat. In mathematical terms: ● The Loss Function (e.g., Mean Squared Error) represents the hill. ● The Minimum of the loss function represents the best model parameters (coef ficients/intercepts). ● The Gradient tells us the direction and steepness of the slope. 2. Mathematical Derivation (Simple Linear Regression)

--- Page 187 ---
In Simple Linear Regression, we want to find the best values for m (slope) and b (intercept) to minimize the Loss Function (L). The Loss Function (MSE): L = (1/n) * Sum[(y_actual - (m * x + b))^2] The Update Rule: We update our parameters iteratively using a "Learning Rate" (denoted as alpha or lr ): ● New_b = Old_b - (lr * dL/db) ● New_m = Old_m - (lr * dL/dm) Calculating Partial Derivatives: To find the direction, we differentiate the loss function with respect to b and m . 1. Gradient with respect to b (dL/db): dL/db = -2/n * Sum[y - (m*x + b)] 2. Gradient with respect to m (dL/dm): dL/dm = -2/n * Sum[(y - (m*x + b)) * x] 3. Key Hyperparameters ● Learning Rate (lr) : Controls the size of the steps. ○ Too Small : Convergence is very slow. ○ Too Large : You might overshoot the minimum and never reach it (divergence). ● Epochs : The number of times the algorithm iterates through the entire dataset to update the parameters. 4. Implementation Code From Scratch (Python) This code implements Gradient Descent for a Simple Linear Regression model where we find both the slope ( m ) and the intercept ( b ). Python import numpy as np class GradientDescentRegressor:

--- Page 188 ---
def __init__ (self, learning_rate= 0.01 , epochs= 100 ): self.m = 100 # Initial random slope self.b = - 100 # Initial random intercept self.lr = learning_rate self.epochs = epochs def fit (self, X, y): n = len (X) for i in range (self.epochs): # 1. Calculate the gradients (slopes of the loss function) # Derivative w.r.t intercept (b) loss_slope_b = - 2 * np. sum (y - self.m * X.ravel() - self.b) / n # Derivative w.r.t slope (m) loss_slope_m = - 2 * np. sum ((y - self.m * X.ravel() - self.b) * X.ravel()) / n # 2. Update the parameters self.b = self.b - (self.lr * loss_slope_b) self.m = self.m - (self.lr * loss_slope_m) print( f"Final Intercept (b): {self.b} " ) print( f"Final Slope (m): {self.m} " )

--- Page 189 ---
def predict (self, X): return self.m * X + self.b # Example Usage: # X = np.array([1, 2, 3, 4, 5]) # y = np.array([5, 7, 9, 11, 13]) # y = 2x + 3 # gd = GradientDescentRegressor(learning_rate=0.01, epochs=1000) # gd.fit(X, y) 5. Why Gradient Descent? You might ask: "Why not use the Normal Equation (formula) we learned before?" 1. Efficiency : For massive datasets (millions of rows), calculating a matrix inverse (Normal Equation) is extremely slow and memory-intensive. Gradient Descent is much faster for "Big Data." 2. Generality : Gradient Descent can be used for almost any differentiable function, whereas the Normal Equation only works for Linear Regression. 3. Optimization : It is the foundation for Batch, Stochastic, and Mini-batch Gradient Descent, which are used to train complex models like Neural Networks. 6. Visualizing the Process 1. Convergence Plot : If you plot the Loss (Cost) vs. the Number of Epochs, you should see the loss decreasing over time and eventually flattening out. This flattening indicates that the model has "converged" to a minimum. 2. 3D Surface : In a 3D plot (with m , b , and Loss ), the algorithm looks like a ball rolling down a bowl toward the bottom. 7. Important Note on Data Scaling

--- Page 190 ---
Gradient Descent is sensitive to the scale of features. If one feature is in the range of 0-1 and another is 0-10,000, the "bowl" becomes very elongated, making it hard for the algorithm to converge. Standardization (scaling features to have a mean of 0 and variance of 1) is highly recommended before running Gradient Descent. YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=ORyfPJypKuU Batch Gradient Descent: Concepts, Derivation, and Implementation Batch Gradient Descent is a type of Gradient Descent where we calculate the gradient of the cost function with respect to the parameters using the entire dataset for every single update. 1. Key Characteristics ● Update Frequency : The model parameters (weights and bias) are updated only once per "epoch" (one full pass through the training data). ● Speed : It is computationally expensive for large datasets because it processes all rows to make a single update. ● Convergence : It provides a stable and smooth convergence toward the global minimum, especially for convex functions like Mean Squared Error (MSE). 2. Mathematical Derivation for Multiple Features In a scenario with multiple features (x1, x2, ..., xn), we have multiple coefficients (beta1, beta2, ..., betan) and one intercept (beta0). The Model: y_hat = beta0 + beta1 x1 + beta2 x2 + ... + betan*xn The Loss Function (MSE): L = (1/n) * Sum[(y_actual - y_hat)^2] The Gradient Updates: For each parameter , we calculate the partial derivative of the loss function.

--- Page 191 ---
1. For the Intercept (beta0): dL/d_beta0 = -2/n * Sum[y_actual - y_hat] Update: beta0 = beta0 - (learning_rate * dL/d_beta0) 2. For any Coef ficient (beta_j): dL/d_beta_j = -2/n * Sum[(y_actual - y_hat) * x_j] Update: beta_j = beta_j - (learning_rate * dL/d_beta_j) 3. Vectorization: The Secret to Efficiency Instead of updating each coefficient individually in a loop, we use Matrix Multiplication . This allows us to update all coefficients simultaneously . Gradient Vector = (-2/n) * (X_transpose * (y_actual - y_hat)) Where: ● X : Feature matrix. ● y_actual : Actual target values. ● y_hat : Predicted values. 4. Implementation Code From Scratch (Multiple Features) This implementation works for any number of input columns. Python import numpy as np class BatchGradientDescent: def __init__ (self, learning_rate= 0.01 , epochs= 100 ): self.coef_ = None self.intercept_ = None self.lr = learning_rate self.epochs = epochs

--- Page 192 ---
def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # Initialize parameters: Intercept at 0, all coefficients at 1 self.intercept_ = 0 self.coef_ = np.ones(X_train.shape[ 1 ]) n = X_train.shape[ 0 ] # Number of rows for i in range (self.epochs): # 1. Predict y_hat for all rows at once y_hat = np.dot(X_train, self.coef_) + self.intercept_ # 2. Calculate the derivative for the intercept intercept_derivative = - 2 * np.mean(y_train - y_hat) self.intercept_ = self.intercept_ - (self.lr * intercept_derivative) # 3. Calculate the derivative for all coefficients at once # This is the vectorization step coef_derivative = - 2 * np.dot((y_train - y_hat), X_train) / n self.coef_ = self.coef_ - (self.lr * coef_derivative) print( "Final Intercept:" , self.intercept_) print( "Final Coef ficients:" , self.coef_) def predict (self, X_test):

--- Page 193 ---
return np.dot(X_test, self.coef_) + self.intercept_ 5. Why "Batch" Gradient Descent? The term "Batch" refers to the fact that we use the entire batch of data to calculate the gradient. ● Pros : It is theoretically sound and produces a direct path to the minimum. ● Cons : If your dataset has 10 million rows, you have to do 10 million calculations just to update the weights once. This makes it very slow for massive data. In such cases, people prefer Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) or Mini-Batch Gradient Descent . 6. Tips for First-Time Users 1. Start Small : Use a small learning rate (like 0.01 or 0.001) to ensure the model doesn't diverge. 2. Scale your Data : Batch Gradient Descent works best when all features are on a similar scale (e.g., between 0 and 1). 3. Monitor Loss : If your loss isn't decreasing, your learning rate might be too high or your features might not be scaled. YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=Jyo53pA yVAM Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD): Detailed Notes and Implementation Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) is an optimization technique that addresses the scalability issues of Batch Gradient Descent, making it suitable for very large datasets and deep learning. 1. The Core Concept: Why SGD? In Batch Gradient Descent , the model updates its weights only once after looking at the entire dataset .

--- Page 194 ---
● Problem : If you have 1 million rows, calculating the gradient requires 1 million calculations for just one update. This is slow and requires massive memory . ● Solution : Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) . "Stochastic" means random. Instead of waiting to see all rows, SGD updates the model parameters after looking at a single, randomly selected row . Comparison: ● Batch GD : 1 Pass through data (1 Epoch) = 1 Update. ● SGD : 1 Pass through data (1 Epoch) = n Updates (where n is the number of rows). 2. Mathematical Derivation The update rules for the Intercept (beta0) and Coef ficients (beta_j) are conceptually the same as Batch GD, but without the summation over all rows. The Model for one row i : y_hat_i = beta0 + beta1 x1_i + ... + betan xn_i Update Rule for Intercept (beta0): New_beta0 = Old_beta0 - (learning_rate * Gradient_i) Where Gradient_i = -2 * (y_actual_i - y_hat_i) Update Rule for Coefficient (beta_j): New_beta_j = Old_beta_j - (learning_rate * Gradient_ij) Where Gradient_ij = -2 * (y_actual_i - y_hat_i) * x_ij Crucial Change : In Batch GD, we averaged these gradients across all n rows. In SGD, we take the gradient from just one random row . 3. Key Features and Trade-offs ● Speed : SGD is much faster to start converging because it makes updates immediately . ● Convergence Path : While Batch GD takes a direct, smooth path to the minimum, SGD takes a noisy , zig-zag path .

--- Page 195 ---
● Global Minimum : The noise in SGD is actually a benefit for non-convex functions (like in Deep Learning). The randomness helps the model "jump out" of local minima to find the global minimum. ● Stability : SGD never truly settles at the exact minimum; it "oscillates" (bounces) around it because the gradient of a single row is a noisy estimate of the true gradient. 4. SGD Implementation From Scratch (Python) Python import numpy as np import random class SGDRegressor: def __init__ (self, learning_rate= 0.01 , epochs= 100 ): self.coef_ = None self.intercept_ = None self.lr = learning_rate self.epochs = epochs def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # Initialize intercept at 0 and all coefficients at 1 self.intercept_ = 0 self.coef_ = np.ones(X_train.shape[ 1 ]) for i in range (self.epochs): for j in range (X_train.shape[ 0 ]):

--- Page 196 ---
# 1. Select a random row index idx = random.randint( 0 , X_train.shape[ 0 ] - 1 ) # 2. Pick that specific row and target X_row = X_train[idx] y_actual = y_train[idx] # 3. Predict for that single row y_hat = np.dot(X_row , self.coef_) + self.intercept_ # 4. Calculate gradients for that single row intercept_grad = - 2 * (y_actual - y_hat) coef_grad = - 2 * (y_actual - y_hat) * X_row # 5. Update parameters immediately self.intercept_ = self.intercept_ - (self.lr * intercept_grad) self.coef_ = self.coef_ - (self.lr * coef_grad) print( "Final Intercept:" , self.intercept_) print( "Final Coef ficients:" , self.coef_) def predict (self, X_test): return np.dot(X_test, self.coef_) + self.intercept_

--- Page 197 ---
5. Advanced Concept: Learning Schedules Since SGD bounces around the minimum, a common trick is to gradually reduce the learning rate as training progresses. This is called a Learning Schedule . ● Early on : High learning rate to move fast toward the valley . ● Later on : Tiny learning rate to "settle down" and stop bouncing so much around the minimum. 6. Using Scikit-Learn's SGD In real-world projects, you use the SGDRegressor class from sklearn.linear_model . It is highly optimized and includes features like regularization ( penalty ) and learning schedules ( learning_rate='invscaling' ). Key Parameters : ● max_iter : Number of epochs. ● eta0 : Initial learning rate. ● learning_rate : Strategy to change lr (e.g., 'constant', 'optimal', 'invscaling'). YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=V7KBAa_gh4c Mini-Batch Gradient Descent: Detailed Notes and Implementation Mini-batch Gradient Descent is a variation of Gradient Descent that splits the training dataset into small batches that are used to calculate the model error and update the model coefficients. It strikes a balance between the efficiency of Batch Gradient Descent and the robustness of Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD). 1. The Core Concept In previous notes, we discussed: ● Batch Gradient Descent : Updates parameters after looking at the entire dataset. (1 update per epoch). ● Stochastic Gradient Descent (SGD) : Updates parameters after looking at one single row . (n updates per epoch).

--- Page 198 ---
Mini-batch Gradient Descent is the middle ground. It updates parameters after looking at a small group of rows (called a "batch"). ● If you have 1,000 rows and a batch size of 50, you will make 20 updates to the model parameters in one single epoch. 2. Mathematical Derivation The derivation follows the same logic as Batch Gradient Descent, but the summation is only performed over the rows within the specific batch. The Update Rule: For each batch of size k : ● New_beta = Old_beta - (learning_rate * Gradient_batch) Calculating Gradient for a Batch: 1. Intercept (beta0): dL/d_beta0 = -2/k * Sum[y_actual_i - y_hat_i] (Summing from i=1 to k) 2. Coef ficient (beta_j): dL/d_beta_j = -2/k * Sum[(y_actual_i - y_hat_i) * x_ij] (Summing from i=1 to k) The result : You get a stable gradient estimate (better than SGD's single row) but don't have to wait for the whole dataset (faster than Batch GD). 3. Advantages of Mini-batch GD ● Computational Efficiency : It utilizes vectorized operations (matrix multiplication) for the batch, which is very fast on modern hardware (CPUs/GPUs). ● Stable Convergence : The gradient is less noisy than SGD, so the path to the minimum is smoother . ● Memory Management : It doesn't require the entire dataset to be loaded into memory at once, unlike Batch GD. 4. Mini-batch Implementation From Scratch (Python) Python import numpy as np

--- Page 199 ---
import random class MiniBatchGDRegressor: def __init__ (self, batch_size, learning_rate= 0.01 , epochs= 100 ): self.coef_ = None self.intercept_ = None self.lr = learning_rate self.epochs = epochs self.batch_size = batch_size def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # Initialize parameters self.intercept_ = 0 self.coef_ = np.ones(X_train.shape[ 1 ]) for i in range (self.epochs): # Create a list of all row indices and shuffle them indices = list ( range (X_train.shape[ 0 ])) random.shuf fle(indices) # Divide indices into batches for j in range ( 0 , X_train.shape[ 0 ], self.batch_size): batch_indices = indices[j : j + self.batch_size]

--- Page 200 ---
# Extract the current batch X_batch = X_train[batch_indices] y_batch = y_train[batch_indices] # 1. Predict for the batch y_hat = np.dot(X_batch, self.coef_) + self.intercept_ # 2. Calculate derivatives for the batch intercept_der = - 2 * np.mean(y_batch - y_hat) coef_der = - 2 * np.dot((y_batch - y_hat), X_batch) / X_batch.shape[ 0 ] # 3. Update parameters self.intercept_ = self.intercept_ - (self.lr * intercept_der) self.coef_ = self.coef_ - (self.lr * coef_der) print( "Final Intercept:" , self.intercept_) print( "Final Coef ficients:" , self.coef_) def predict (self, X_test): return np.dot(X_test, self.coef_) + self.intercept_ 5. Summary Comparison

--- Page 201 ---
Feature Batch GD Stochastic GD Mini-batch GD Rows per update Entire Dataset 1 Row Small Group (Batch) Speed per update Slow Fast Medium Path to Minimum Smooth/Direct Noisy/Zig-zag Relatively Smooth Memory usage High Low Efficient/Medium Best For Small Datasets Online Learning Large Scale / Deep Learning 6. Tips for choosing Batch Size ● Common batch sizes are powers of 2 (e.g., 32, 64, 128, 256). This is because computer memory is organized in binary , making these sizes more efficient for hardware processing. ● If the batch size is too small, it behaves like SGD. ● If the batch size is too large (approaching the dataset size), it behaves like Batch GD. Polynomial Regression: Comprehensive Notes and Implementation Polynomial Regression is a form of linear regression in which the relationship between the independent variable (X) and the dependent variable (y) is modeled as an nth-degree polynomial. 1. Why Polynomial Regression? Standard Linear Regression assumes a straight-line relationship between variables. However , in the real world, data often follows a curved pattern (non-linear). If you force a straight line onto a curve, you get high error (bias). Polynomial Regression allows the model to "bend" to fit the data. 2. The Mathematical Equation Simple Linear Regression: y = b0 + b1*x Polynomial Regression (Degree 2): y = b0 + b1 x + b2 (x^2) General nth Degree: y = b0 + b1 x + b2 (x^2) + ... + bn*(x^n) Important : Even though we have terms like x^2 and x^n, this is still considered Linear Regression because the relationship is linear with respect to the coefficients (b0, b1, ...) . 3. Feature Transformation To implement Polynomial Regression, we don't change the algorithm; we change the features . We take an input column x and create new columns: x^2 , x^3 , etc. Example: If your original data is X = [2, 3, 4], and you use Degree 2: ● New X = [[1, 2, 4], [1, 3, 9], [1, 4, 16]] ● The '1' represents x^0 (intercept).

--- Page 203 ---
● The middle column is x^1. ● The last column is x^2. 4. Implementation Code (Scikit-Learn) Python import numpy as np import matplotlib.pyplot as plt from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression from sklearn.preprocessing import PolynomialFeatures from sklearn.metrics import r2_score # 1. Generate Non-Linear Data X = 6 * np.random.rand( 100 , 1 ) - 3 y = 0.5 * X** 2 + X + 2 + np.random.randn( 100 , 1 ) # 2. Split Data X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 , random_state= 2 ) # 3. Apply Polynomial Transformation (Degree 2) poly = PolynomialFeatures(degree= 2 , include_bias= True ) X_train_trans = poly.fit_transform(X_train) X_test_trans = poly.transform(X_test) # 4. Train Linear Regression on transformed features

--- Page 204 ---
lr = LinearRegression() lr.fit(X_train_trans, y_train) # 5. Predict and Evaluate y_pred = lr.predict(X_test_trans) print( "R2 Score:" , r2_score(y_test, y_pred)) # 6. Visualize X_new = np.linspace(- 3 , 3 , 100 ).reshape( 100 , 1 ) X_new_poly = poly.transform(X_new) y_new = lr.predict(X_new_poly) plt.plot(X_new , y_new , "r-" , label= "Predictions" ) plt.plot(X, y, "b." , label= "Actual Data" ) plt.legend() plt.show() 5. Overfitting vs. Underfitting ● Underfitting (Degree 1) : Using a straight line for a curve. The model is too simple and has high error on both training and test data. ● Balanced (Degree 2 or 3) : The model captures the general curve of the data without being distracted by noise. ● Overfitting (Degree 10+) : The model becomes too flexible. It tries to pass through every single training point, including random noise. ○ Result : Very high accuracy on training data, but very poor accuracy on new (test) data.

--- Page 205 ---
6. Multiple Polynomial Regression If you have two input features x1 and x2 and use Degree 2, the model generates: ● 1 (Intercept) ● x1 , x2 (Original features) ● x1^2 , x2^2 (Squared features) ● x1*x2 (Interaction term) The interaction term x1*x2 is crucial because it captures how features affect each other . 7. Key Takeaways 1. Selection : Always start with a low degree (2 or 3) and increase only if necessary . 2. Preprocessing : It is often helpful to Scale (Standardize) your data before applying high-degree polynomial regression to avoid numerical instability . 3. Cross-V alidation : Use cross-validation to find the "optimal degree" where the model generalizes best to unseen data. YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=BNWLf3cKdbQ Bias-Variance Trade-off: Comprehensive Notes for Machine Learning The Bias-V ariance Trade-off is a fundamental concept that explains why machine learning models perform well or poorly on unseen data. It is the key to understanding Overfitting and Underfitting . 1. The Core Definition When we train a model, the total error can be broken down into three parts: 1. Bias Error : Error due to simplistic assumptions in the learning algorithm. 2. Variance Error : Error due to the model's sensitivity to small fluctuations in the training set. 3. Irreducible Error : Noise that cannot be removed by any model.

--- Page 206 ---
Total Error = (Bias)^2 + Variance + Irreducible Error 2. Understanding Bias Bias is the difference between the average prediction of our model and the correct value which we are trying to predict. ● High Bias : The model is too simple and fails to capture the underlying patterns of the data. This leads to Underfitting . ● Analogy : A student who only memorizes one formula and tries to apply it to every math problem regardless of its type. ● Result : High error on both Training and Test data. 3. Understanding Variance Variance refers to the model's sensitivity to the specific training data used. ● High Variance : The model is too complex and captures the "noise" (random fluctuations) in the training data rather than the actual pattern. This leads to Overfitting . ● Analogy : A student who memorizes every single specific question and answer from a textbook but fails to solve a slightly different question in the exam. ● Result : Very low error on Training data, but high error on Test data. 4. The Trade-off The goal of machine learning is to find the "Sweet Spot" between Bias and Variance. ● Low Complexity (Simple Model) : High Bias, Low Variance. ● High Complexity (Complex Model) : Low Bias, High Variance. ● Optimal Model : Moderate complexity that minimizes the total error. 5. Overfitting vs. Underfitting Summary Feature Underfitting Optimal Fit Overfitting Bias High Low Low

--- Page 207 ---
Variance Low Low High Training Error High Low Very Low Testing Error High Low High Model Type Too Simple Just Right Too Complex 6. Mathematical Intuition (Simplified) In a regression scenario, if the true relationship is a curve (quadratic), but we use a straight line (linear), the model has High Bias because it can never "bend" to fit the curve. If we use a 20th-degree polynomial to fit 10 points, the line will wiggle through every point perfectly . This model has High Variance because if we change just one data point slightly , the entire "wiggly" line will change drastically . 7. Code Implementation: Visualizing the Trade-off This Python code demonstrates how increasing model complexity (polynomial degree) shifts a model from underfitting to overfitting. Python import numpy as np import matplotlib.pyplot as plt from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline from sklearn.preprocessing import PolynomialFeatures from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score

# 1. Generate non-linear data def true_fun (X): return np.cos( 1.5 * np.pi * X) np.random.seed( 0 ) n_samples = 30 degrees = [ 1 , 4 , 15 ] # Degrees representing Underfit, Good Fit, Overfit X = np.sort(np.random.rand(n_samples)) y = true_fun(X) + np.random.randn(n_samples) * 0.1 plt.figure(figsize=( 14 , 5 )) for i in range ( len (degrees)): ax = plt.subplot( 1 , len (degrees), i + 1 ) plt.setp(ax, xticks=(), yticks=()) polynomial_features = PolynomialFeatures(degree=degrees[i], include_bias= False ) linear_regression = LinearRegression() pipeline = Pipeline([( "polynomial_features" , polynomial_features), ( "linear_regression" , linear_regression)]) pipeline.fit(X[:, np.newaxis], y) # Evaluate using cross-validation scores = cross_val_score(pipeline, X[:, np.newaxis], y, scoring= "neg_mean_squared_error" , cv= 10 )

--- Page 209 ---
X_test = np.linspace( 0 , 1 , 100 ) plt.plot(X_test, pipeline.predict(X_test[:, np.newaxis]), label= "Model" ) plt.plot(X_test, true_fun(X_test), label= "True function" ) plt.scatter(X, y, edgecolor= 'b' , s= 20 , label= "Samples" ) plt.xlabel( "x" ) plt.ylabel( "y" ) plt.xlim(( 0 , 1 )) plt.ylim((- 2 , 2 )) plt.legend(loc= "best" ) plt.title( f"Degree {degrees[i]} \nMSE = {-scores.mean(): .2 e} (+/- {scores.std(): .2 e} )" ) plt.show() 8. How to Fix These Issues? ● To fix Underfitting (High Bias) : ○ Increase model complexity (e.g., use higher-degree polynomials). ○ Add more features to the dataset. ○ Reduce regularization (if used). ● To fix Overfitting (High Variance) : ○ Get more training data. ○ Simplify the model (reduce features). ○ Use Regularization (L1/Lasso, L2/Ridge). ○ Use techniques like Cross-V alidation to find the right complexity . Ridge Regression: Regularization and Geometric Intuition Regularization is a technique used to prevent overfitting in machine learning models by adding extra information (a penalty) to the model. Ridge Regression is one of the most common types of regularized linear models, also known as L2 Regularization . 1. Why Regularization? Overfitting occurs when a model performs exceptionally well on training data but poorly on test data because it has learned the "noise" or random fluctuations in the training set. ● The Goal : Reduce the complexity of the model so it generalizes better to new, unseen data. ● The Solution : Instead of just minimizing the difference between actual and predicted values (Residual Sum of Squares), we add a penalty term that discourages the model from having very large coefficients (slopes). 2. Mathematical Explanation and Derivation In standard Linear Regression, the cost function (Loss) is: Loss = Sum[(y_actual - y_predicted)^2] In Ridge Regression , we modify this cost function by adding a penalty proportional to the square of the coefficients : New Loss = Sum[(y_actual - y_predicted)^2] + (Lambda * Sum[Slope^2]) Breaking down the formula: 1. Residual Sum of Squares (RSS) : Sum[(y_actual - y_predicted)^2]. This part tries to make the model fit the training data. 2. Penalty Term (L2 Penalty) : Lambda * Sum[Slope^2]. ○ Slope (m) : These are the coefficients of your features. ○ Lambda (Alpha) : This is a hyperparameter that controls how much you want to penalize the coefficients. ■ If Lambda = 0 , Ridge Regression becomes standard Linear Regression. ■ As Lambda increases , the penalty becomes heavier , forcing the slopes (coef ficients) to shrink toward zero. ■ If Lambda is very high , the slopes become nearly zero, leading to a very simple model (High Bias/Underfitting).

--- Page 211 ---
Intuition: By penalizing the square of the slopes, the model is forced to choose smaller coefficients. Smaller coefficients mean the model is less sensitive to small changes in individual input features, which reduces variance and prevents overfitting. 3. Geometric Intuition ● Standard Linear Regression : Tries to find the line that passes as close as possible to all points, which can lead to a "wiggly" line that captures noise. ● Ridge Regression : Tries to find a line that fits the points but also stays relatively "flat" (small slopes). ● The Result : The best fit line is "pushed" or "tilted" away from the perfect training fit to ensure it remains stable. As Lambda increases, the line rotates to become more horizontal. 4. Implementation Code (Python) We use the Scikit-Learn library to implement Ridge Regression. The parameter alpha in the code corresponds to the mathematical Lambda . Python import numpy as np from sklearn.datasets import load_diabetes from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression, Ridge from sklearn.metrics import r2_score # 1. Load the dataset (Diabetes dataset) X, y = load_diabetes(return_X_y= True ) # 2. Split the data into training and testing sets X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(X, y, test_size= 0.2 , random_state= 2 )

--- Page 212 ---
# 3. Standard Linear Regression (No Regularization) lr = LinearRegression() lr.fit(X_train, y_train) y_pred_lr = lr.predict(X_test) print( "Linear Regression R2 Score:" , r2_score(y_test, y_pred_lr)) # 4. Ridge Regression (With L2 Regularization) # Alpha is the lambda hyperparameter ridge = Ridge(alpha= 1.0 ) # alpha=0 is same as linear regression ridge.fit(X_train, y_train) y_pred_ridge = ridge.predict(X_test) print( "Ridge Regression R2 Score (alpha=1.0):" , r2_score(y_test, y_pred_ridge)) # 5. Comparing Coef ficients print( "Linear Regression Coef ficients:" , lr.coef_) print( "Ridge Regression Coef ficients (alpha=1.0):" , ridge.coef_) 5. Key Takeaways 1. Shrinkage : Ridge Regression shrinks the coefficients but never makes them exactly zero . (Unlike Lasso Regression, which can do feature selection). 2. Bias-V ariance Trade-off : Regularization increases bias slightly but significantly reduces variance, leading to a better overall model. 3. Scaling : Because Ridge Regression penalizes the size of coefficients, it is crucial to scale your features (e.g., using StandardScaler) before applying it. If features are on different scales, the penalty will affect them unfairly .

--- Page 213 ---
4. Optimal Alpha : You can use RidgeCV in Scikit-Learn to automatically find the best value for Alpha using cross-validation. YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=aEow1QoTLo0 Ridge Regression: Mathematical Formulation and Implementation From Scratch Ridge Regression (L2 Regularization) is a technique used to prevent overfitting by adding a penalty term to the standard OLS (Ordinary Least Squares) cost function. This penalty discourages large coefficients, effectively reducing model variance. 1. Mathematical Formulation for Simple Linear Regression (2D) In standard Linear Regression, we minimize the Residual Sum of Squares (RSS): Loss = Sum[(y_i - (m*x_i + b))^2] In Ridge Regression, we add a penalty term: Loss = Sum[(y_i - (m*x_i + b))^2] + (Lambda * m^2) Deriving the Slope (m): To find the optimal m , we take the partial derivative of the Loss with respect to m and set it to zero. After derivation, the formula for the slope becomes: m = Sum[(y_i - y_mean) * (x_i - x_mean)] / (Sum[(x_i - x_mean)^2] + Lambda) Key Insight : Notice the + Lambda in the denominator . As Lambda increases, the value of the slope m decreases, pulling it closer to zero. 2. Implementation From Scratch (Simple 2D Version) This code implements the closed-form solution derived above for a single feature. Python

--- Page 214 ---
import numpy as np class MyRidge2D: def __init__ (self, alpha= 0.1 ): self.alpha = alpha self.m = None self.b = None def fit (self, X_train, y_train): num = 0 den = 0 x_mean = np.mean(X_train) y_mean = np.mean(y_train) for i in range (X_train.shape[ 0 ]): num = num + (y_train[i] - y_mean) * (X_train[i] - x_mean) den = den + (X_train[i] - x_mean)** 2 # Adding alpha (Lambda) to the denominator as per derivation self.m = num / (den + self.alpha) self.b = y_mean - (self.m * x_mean) def predict (self, X_test): return self.m * X_test + self.b

--- Page 215 ---
3. Mathematical Formulation for Multiple Features (Matrix Form) For a dataset with multiple features, the cost function in matrix form is: Loss = (XW - Y)^T * (XW - Y) + Lambda * (W^T * W) Where: ● X : Feature matrix (with a column of 1s added for intercept). ● W : Vector of weights (coef ficients). ● Y : Vector of actual target values. The Closed-Form Solution (Normal Equation for Ridge): By differentiating and solving for W, we get: W = (X^T * X + Lambda * I)^-1 * X^T * Y Where: ● I : Identity matrix. ● Note : In practice, we often set the first element of the identity matrix I[0,0] = 0 so that we don't penalize the intercept (b), only the slopes (coef ficients). 4. Implementation From Scratch (N-Dimensional Matrix Version) This code replicates the logic used by Scikit-Learn's Ridge class for any number of input features. Python import numpy as np class MyRidgeND: def __init__ (self, alpha= 0.1 ): self.alpha = alpha self.coef_ = None

--- Page 216 ---
self.intercept_ = None def fit (self, X_train, y_train): # 1. Add a column of 1s to handle intercept X_train = np.insert(X_train, 0 , 1 , axis= 1 ) # 2. Create Identity Matrix # Shape is (n_features + 1, n_features + 1) I = np.identity(X_train.shape[ 1 ]) # Optional: Set first element to 0 to avoid penalizing the intercept I[ 0 ][ 0 ] = 0 # 3. Calculate weights using Normal Equation for Ridge # W = (X^T * X + alpha * I)^-1 * X^T * Y A = np.dot(X_train.T , X_train) + self.alpha * I B = np.dot(X_train.T , y_train) W = np.dot(np.linalg.inv(A), B) # 4. Extract results self.intercept_ = W[ 0 ] self.coef_ = W[ 1 :] def predict (self, X_test): return np.dot(X_test, self.coef_) + self.intercept_

--- Page 217 ---
5. Summary and Comparison ● Lambda (Alpha) : This is the "knob" of the model. ○ Alpha = 0 : Result is identical to standard Linear Regression. ○ Alpha -> Infinity : Slopes tend toward zero, leading to underfitting. ● Shrinkage : Ridge regression shrinks coefficients toward zero but never makes them exactly zero. ● Why Matrix Form? : The matrix form is much more efficient for modern computers because it uses vectorized calculations rather than loops. YouTube Video Link: http://www .youtube.com/watch?v=oDlZBQjk_3A Ridge Regression using Gradient Descent: Comprehensive Notes While Ridge Regression can be solved using the closed-form Normal Equation (as seen in previous notes), this method becomes computationally expensive as the number of features increases. Gradient Descent provides a scalable alternative for finding the optimal weights for Ridge Regression. 1. The Core Concept In Ridge Regression, we want to minimize the regularized Loss Function (L). The goal is to find the weights ( W ) that lead to the minimum error while keeping the magnitudes of the weights small to prevent overfitting. 2. Mathematical Formulation (Vector Form) The Regularized Loss Function: L = (1/2) * (XW - Y)^T * (XW - Y) + (Lambda / 2) * (W^T * W) Where: ● X : Feature matrix. ● W : Weight vector (coef ficients and intercept). ● Y : Target vector . ● Lambda : Hyperparameter (regularization strength).

--- Page 218 ---
The Gradient (Derivative): To perform Gradient Descent, we need the partial derivative of the loss function with respect to the weights ( W ). dL/dW = X^T * (XW - Y) + Lambda * W Breaking down the Gradient: 1. X^T * (XW - Y) : This is the standard gradient for Linear Regression. It tells us how to move to fit the data. 2. Lambda * W : This is the gradient of the penalty term. It tells the weights to shrink toward zero. 3. The Update Rule We update the weights iteratively in the opposite direction of the gradient: W_new = W_old - (learning_rate * dL/dW) By substituting the gradient into this rule, we get: W_new = W_old - learning_rate * [X^T * (XW - Y) + Lambda * W_old] 4. Implementation Logic 1. Initialize : Start with random weights (W) or all zeros. 2. Loop (Epochs) : ○ Predict: y_hat = X .
